Interesting to Brandy and Whisky Drinkers.
rpHE imports of case Whisky during the year 1897 amounted to 115,357 cases,
those of case Brandy to 43,723 cases. These figures exhibit a marked increase
in the consumption of Brandy, and show a strong tendency of the public to revert
to that spirit as their favorite drink. It was thought by many that the consumption
of Whisky was fully eight cases to every one of Brandy, whereas the proportion is
only about to 1.

THE RED PAGE.
Literature is the year-old literary review issued
from Lond. Times office, and edited by H. D.
Traill, a leading Eng. journalist and man-of-
lettersof the old school, capable, Conservative,
and—-in his 56th year—pretty ponderous and dull.
Some of his “ Saturday Songs ” and “ Recaptured
Rhymes ” are readable, and his “ New Lucian ”is
bright ; but he has always been rather solid than
fi er y__and j n Australia, without depreciating
solidity, we appreciate fire. Traill has said a lot
of good things, and few well. And the man
whom he keeps to discuss Australian literature in
Literature doesn’t know the rudiments of his sub-
ject : ’tis small compensation that his ignorance is
out-of-date. In issue May 14 there is the ridicu-
lousest sermon ! The writer even takes Lala
Fisher seriously ! And he comments on Lala’s
God’s-feet book—-
It illustrates the tendency, so conspicuous in all collec-
tions of Australian verse, to run off smooth stanzas on
love and death and clouds and woodlands without dis-
tinction of style or originality of thought.
Well! This does apply to some of the minor
milk-and-water people whom the Australasian
wickedly fosters ; but it doesn’t app to the best
of the milk-and-water school, and, as for the red-
corpuscle school, Boake and Lawson, Paterson
and Dyson, laugh at the idea. There ain’t no
sich a tendency—in characteristic Australian
verse. Maybe we can’t claim much original-
ity of thought ; but for distinction of style
(taken as including lyrical quality) no better
minor poetry is being written in England than
Daley and Quinn are writing in Australia; and
nobody writes in England with a truer, tenderer
sentiment than Ogilvie. And a score of other
names will occur to The Bulletin reader—whose
work is occasionally as good or almost as good
as the work of these.
■--* 4c * *
Here, for example, are two first-rate stanzas by
Arthur Adams (the Maoriland poet who has lately
come to Sydney as secretary to J. 0. Williamson)
which for “distinction of style” and pith of
matter would have attracted attention in Stephen
Phillips’s book—and England can do no better
than that.
4c
And was there no “distinction of style ” about
Daley’s issue-before-last-reprinted “Sunset Fan-
tasy ” ? And if there is any present-day English
writer who puts more of the feeling, the imagina-
tion, the rare mystery of poetry into his verse
than Quinn has put into tins charming piece, he
certainly takes a lot of pains to hide his light that
never was.
And, though Ogilvie have fewer claims to “ dis-
tinction of style, ’ how many English present-day
poets can evoke the warm poetical impression
which he evokes in “Bowmont Water ”?
These three seta of verses (now first published
are taken casually from the matter waiting publi-
cation. Now compare the piece of verse to which
the same issue of Literature gives honorable
place—
SIC TRANSIT.
Dreamy sound of rain at dying summer eve,
Dewy sight of grass at living summer morn,
Drowsy scent of rose at sleeping summer noon.
Ye to me are sweet as life, as death forlorn.
Through your tears I feel your loveliness divine,
For your freshness or your sweetness seems to
blend
With diviner dawns and sunsets soul-create,
Unalloyed with our inevitable end.
I. Zangivill.
* * * «
This is good ; but it certainly is not better.
It has not the emotional thrill of Ogilvie,
or the lyrical thrill of Quinn, or the in-
tellectual thrill of Adams. It is an agree-
able variation on an old theme; but it is
rather cultured verse than poetry, though it ca,n
claim to be poetry. But it is sweet and placid
rather than forceful : it has not the individuality
or vitality of any of the pieces quoted : it is less
arresting, less memorable. Of course, Zangwill
has not the poetical temperament : he is not a fair
Bample of the English minor person. But, any-
how, there is the kind of English verse which
I/iterature prints, and there is the kind of Aus-
tralian—or Australian-bred —verse The
Bulletin prints ; and the comparison isn’t too
poetically favorable to Literature.
» * * *
And the cheerless Literature reviewer says
Australia does not seem to be aiming high so far as
poetry is concerned, or to have found or created any high
standard of literary taste. For it must be confessed that
the Australians are not interested in literature. They
read a good deal for their own enjoyment, and their daily
newspapers are as good as can be found in any quarter of
the globe. Much has been done by the Governments to
OUltivate intellectual activity, and to encourage schools
and Universities. Yet there is an almost entire absence
of literary ideals, of appreciative study devoted to the
English classics, of independent literary judgment. The
colonists accept the verdict of the mother country as
authoritative, and even Australian writers of high merit,
such as Rolf Boldrewood and “Ada Cambridge,” are
without honor in their own country until they have the
caohet of English approval. There are few literary
societies, few successful literary magazines, and an author
who can make his living by writing Australian books is
at present unknown.
! ! ! 11 !
* * * *
Here’s a "Van Winkle for us ! —and the traits of
the breed seem to have strengthened since
Irving’s time. This might have been true twenty
years ago; it wasn’t true ten years ago; it is
ludicrously untrue to-day—when Australians buy
13,000 of Paterson’s verses at 5s. before he sells
500 in England, and Victor Daley gets for a dozen
or so of lus poema a still wider audience in
15,000 “ Golden Shanties.” And they are still
buying the degenerated novels of “ Rolf Boldre-
wood ” in England while we’ve dropped them here
like cold potatoes. More, if Literature will
take the trouble to enquire of any leading
London publisher, it will find that Aus-
tralia is 30 little “interested in literature”
that it buys more books in proportion to
population than any country in the world ; while
English authors continually complain that their
books are not bought by English readers, but bor-
rowed from a library. The Australian’s cash-
justification of his interest in literature ends all
argument. To be sure, we have lower-priced
editions of many books; but this plea does not.
by any means cover the vast disproportion
between Australian and English book-purchases.
To be sure, a lot of what Australians read isn’t
literature —and what do the English masses read
but sporting novels and Family Herald novelettes?
A little of the writer’s charge is true, toned and
modified. We have “literary ideals,” but we
have not enough ; we have less than we might
have of “ appreciative study devoted to the Eng-
lish classics ’’—and other classics as good as
English ; we might have more “ independent
literary judgment.” Literary societies and maga-
zines are wanting ; and only small demur or
qualification can b' made to the statement that
nobody makes a living by writing Australian
books. And the conditions of Australian life,
and brevity of Australian history, though they
help to explain these things, do not altogether
justify them.
• •
Through all the stupid or misleading state-
ments in° Literature's article it isn’t worth while
wading ; but it is permitted to admire the beauti-
ful example of smug English arrogance with
which the article closes. The writer quotes
Brunton Stephens’s “ Australian Anthem in
which he urges God to bless the mother-land and
let Australia be
One in all noble fame,
Still be our path the same,
Onward in Freedom’s name,
Upward in Thine.
And he comments —
It is to the growth of such sentiments as these, not
only in politios, but in literature, that lovers of Australia
must hopefully look forward to the [sic] development of an
intellectual independence, to a fuller appreciation of our
common intellectual heritage, to a wider and deeper
culture.
Reads like the holy peroration to the ser-
mons of our youth—the beautiful “ to’’-mixture
included —and the calm assumption that Eng-
lish literature is to be our chief or only source,
of inspiration is something to stare at. Neces-
sarily, sprung as we are from the. English stock,
our literature must be an offshoot of English
literature. But that it should be a devout imita-
tion on a Jingo basis is neither necessary nor
desirable. The best prose models are in
French. The strongest artistic stimuli are
French. Much of the best history and philo-
sophy is German. A little of the best poetry is
Italian. To filter Australian ideas throug >
modes alone would be the depth of o y> •
have the right and the duty—if we are de '1
a literature—to found it on the widest t
world-widest basis. We are heirs n< > '
English literature, but to every other 1 e
as well. And to march solemnly in the.ruts
which Mr. English Jingo Traill politely P
out to us —with God, in the, guise of a
English Jingo, hovering approvingly overtieaa
is not in the least, so far as this scribe ’
Australian literature’s intention or tendency.

J. A. Andrews writes :
Ee your criticism of Junor’s book and verse, Agueira s
Bride.” You call it “ rattling good.” I admit that it is
“ rattling”—like a disordered machine,with screws loose,
cranks misfitted, and cogs out of gear. The story ta en
to versify is melodramatic, savors of Murder and us i,
and therefore, as a story, must l>e “ rat! ling good in
your estimation ; but since “ Aguerra’s Bride ” has to be
judged not only as a story, but as verse, perhaps you will
concede on second thoughts that even Murder and Lust
do not supersede the canons of versification. As v erse,
“Aguerra’s Bride” is nothing better than clumsy dog-
gerel. I indicate some of the blemishes in the 39 lines
you have quoted.
1. The extracts swarm with artificial inversions, such
as “ Plains Argentine,” “ Turned she her head,” “Drew
back Aguerra,” “Stood he then by its edge close,
“ Shivers a moment, Aguerra.” The foregoing phrases,
though awkward, are not ambiguous ; hut another has
misled the compositor into inserting, and you into pass-
ing, two commas which destroy the intended meaning
and substitute one quite irrelevant. “No straigbter word
he (,) Aguerra (,) could send.” The context shows that
this is an intususcepted paraphrase of “He (the ally)
could not have stated it to Aguerra more explicitly.”
[lt’s so in the book.]
2. The word “straighter,” above mentioned, is used in
“ slang ” sense.
3. In “ Lit cigarillo,” for “he lit his (or a) cigarillo,”
the use of a common noun without an article or qualifica-
tion is against the genius of the English language.
4. “ Back returned ” is a tautology.
6. Jos6 seems to have taken an oath to perform a mira-
cle—“ Sworn his past misdeeds to mend.”
6. The rhythm forces a strong accent upon words and
syllables not requiring it —for instance, upon the first
syllable of “ misdeeds ” in the line just cited.
7. Four times in the extracts printed are nouns severed
from their preceding adjectives by the division into lines.
This severance, always a blemish, is fatal in association
with a rhythm which accentuates the line-periods. In
one instance it happens at the end of a two-line period,
where the separation is yet further emphasised.
So wrote an ally. No straighter
Word he Aguerra could send.
Worse:
Darkfaced the man spurring weary
Horses. Beside him, in haste,"
And worst:
Plaintive the plover’s call, warning
Mustang and w ild deer, the keen
Puma prowls. So breaks the morning
-Light, on the plains Argentine.
These specimens might have been versified with eighteen
“ mutton ” quads and a lead-cutter.
“Kittling” good, isn’t it !
IN KING-STREET, SYDNEY.
A morn, a sallow lamp-lit morn,
A dawn that never breaks to day :
Old, old the faces and forlorn ;
The hearts look out, so seared, so grey !
It is as if some upturned stone
Had flung to light a vermin rout —
For things misfeatured, souls unknown,
Stagger in blind amaze about.
Along their gleaming lines of light
The charging trams go, head to ground ;
Out from the drifting pathways, white
The faces flash—like faces drowned !
And there, with painted features drear,
And eyes whose pathos still is sweet,
The hunted hunters prowl and peer—
Their lair the long, slow-surging street.
Arthur H. Adams.
THE HIDDEN TIDE.
Within the world a second world
That circles ceaselessly :
Stars in the sky and sister stars —
Turn in your eyes and see !
Tides of the sea that rise and fall,
Aheave from Pole to Pole —
And kindred swayings veiled but felb
That noise along the soul.
Yon moon, noon-rich, high-throned, remote,
And pale with pride extreme,
Draws up the sea, but what white moon
Exalts the tide of Dream ?
The Fisher-Folk who cast their nets
In Vision’s golden tide
Oft bring to light misshapen shells,
And nothing worth beside.
And so their worn hands droop adown,
Their singing throats are dumb ;
The Inner Deep withholds its pearls
Till turn of tide be come.
But patience ! wait—the good tide turns,
The waters inward set;
And 10, behold ! aleap, alive
With glowing fish the net !
O Toilers of the Hidden Seas !
Ye have strange gain and loss,
Dragging the Deeps of Soul for pearls,
And oft-times netting dross.
Flushed to the lips with golden light,
And dark with sable gloom ;
Thrilled by a thousand melodies,
And silent like a tomb.
Fieroe are the winds across your realm,
As though some Demon veiled
Had loosed the gales of Spirit-land
To ravage ways unsailed.
But still sweet hours befall at timea.
Rich lit and full of ease ;
The aftermath is like the light
Of sunset on tired seas.
And worse, perhaps, may be the lob
Of those whose fate is sleep ;
The sodden souls without a tide,
Dense as a rotten deep.
Pain paves the way for keener joy,
And wondrous thoughts uproll
When the large moon of Peace looks down
On high tide in the Soul.
Rodtrie
BOWMONT WATER.
O, we think we’re happy roving,
But the stars that crown the night
They are only ours for loving
When the moon is lost to sight 1
And my hopes are fleeting forward
With the ships that sail the sea,
And my eyes are to the Nor’ward
As an exile’s well may be,
And my heart a shrine has sought hei
Where the lights and shadows play,
At the foot of Bowmont Water,
Bowmont Water —far away.
O, it’s fair in summer weather
When the red sun dropping low
Sets a lustre on the heather
And the Cheviot peaks aglow;
When the hares oome down the meadows
In the gloaming clear and still,
And the flirting lights and shadows
Play at hidies on the hill;
When the wild duck’s mate has sought hi •,
And the speckled hill-trout play
At the foot of Bowmont Water,
Bowmont Water—far away.
O, it’s grand when Winter’s creeping,
And the rime is on the trees,
And the giant hills are sleeping
With the gray clouds on their knees
When the Autumn days are ended,
And the glens are deep with snow,
And the grips are dark and splendid
Where the mountain eagles go :
Then the strath is a king’s daughter,
In her purple robes and gray,
At the foot of Bowmont Water,
Bowmont Water—far away.
We have wandered down the valley
In the days of buried time,
Seen the foxgloves dip and dally,
Heard the fairy blue-bells chime ;
Seen the brier-roses quiver
When the West-wind crossed the dell,
Heard the music of the river
And the tale it had to tell,
Where the melody Love taught her
Is the laverock’s only lay,
At the foot of Bowmont Water,
Bowmont Water—far away.
I have tried the spots, in order,
Where the brightest sunbeams fall,
But the land upeu the Border
Is my own land after all,
And I would not take the glory
Of the whole world’s golden sheen
For the white mists down the corrie
And the naked scaurs between ;
And my heart a shrine has sought her
That will last her little day—
At the foot of Bowmont water,
Bowmont Water—far away.
Will H. Ogilvie.

A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-

some of his symptoms :

Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky?
Can you walk without halting ?

Is your blood poor ?

Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Austral Curse.
One Sisyphus (as punishment for wrong),
Condemned a huge round stone up-hill to roll,
Each day his weary burden heaved along
In vain endeavor to an abstract goal.
Another tale (from Mauritanian-lore)
Relates how Atlas, from his dubious birth,
Upon his broad, herculean shoulders bore
The weighty microcosm called The Earth.
But now, in later days, the harrowed soul
Of Austral swagmen feels the double goad :
Each morn, like Sisyphus, their curse they roll,
And then, like Atlas, stagger ’neath the load.
N.S.W.
GEORGE POWICK BROWN.
Emily’s Friend from Town.
Emily’s friend has come :
Emily’s friend from town.
I’ve got my seedin’ all put through
So I thought I’d just run down.
Monday I rode across :
Over to Em.’s, you know,
They’d fixed a moonlight picnic up
An’ they asked me if I’d go.
There was the tilted van :
Emily went in that.
I fancied she’d a little scheme
Eor to ride with Sandy Pat.
Any’ow we was left:
Emily’s friend an’ me.
I drove her in the old spring carb . . .
Me an’ Em.’s engaged, you see.
Down in the lignum bend
(You know the place I mean) —
I don't know ’ow we lost the track . . •
An’ she told me she’s eighteen.
Emily’s eyes are blue
(Sometimes I wish they weren’t ),
Her friend’s are brown, with such hi flash
That it feels as if they burnt 1
Emily’s tall an’ slim :
Laces ’er stays up small.
It looks as if this friend of ! ers
Didn’t ’ave no stays at all.
Wonder if Sandy Pat
Thinks ’e can cut me out ?
I like ’im better than I did ;
There’s a plenty worse about!
Down in the lignum bend . . .
Told me ’er name was Siss.
Em ’as a mouth that’s small an’ sweet,
But ’er friend’s seems made to kiss 1
Emily’s friend from town,
Emily, Pat, an’ me !
There’s something, some’ow, don’t seem right
An’ the way it oughter be !
NIL.
Do a Growl.
If things are going right or wrong—
Do a growl !
This, the burden of my song—
Do a growl !
If shares are up, or going down ;
If you are “ flush,” or been “ done brown,”
Ever wear your blackest frown,
And—do a growl !
If the wife is extra sweet —
Be sure to growl !
Curse the biscuits 1 damn the meat! —
Do a growl !
Don’t admit her dress is pretty ;
Swear the chop is tough and gritty—
So she'll love you—more’s the pity—
Do a growl !
Have the kids been having fun ?
Do a growl !
When you’ve got ’em meek and glum—
Don’t fail to growl !
Say they’ll drive you off your head ;
Pack the little brats to bed ;
Never heed the tears they’ll shed,
But—do a growl!
Has Jones, your neighbor, pushed along?—
Do a growl!
Drop a hint there’s something wrong—
Do a growl !
Split about his brother Bill;
How he eased his boss’s till;
Say nothing good and all that’s ill—
Do a growl.
When your time has come to dit—
Do a growl !
If you mount beyond the sky—
Do a growl!
Grumble at the golden throne ;
Say the choir can only moan;
Sneer the ivory gates are bone—
Do a growl!
When you twang the golden harp—
Do a growl!
Howl it’s either flat or sharp—
Do a growl !
Then, when out of Heav’n you’re turned,
And in Hell have grilled and burned
Ages, you’ll have maybe learned
Not to growl.
S.A.

SATURDAY, JUNE 25, 1898.
Price, 6d.
MELBOURNE, Saturday. — A football matcb be-'
tween Footscray and Williamstown, on the ground.
‘ of the former, to-day, was a disgraceful exhibi-
tion. Soon after the start of the game it resolved
itself into a series of free fights between the op-
posing teams, and after many blows had been
struck the crowd rushed the ground and stopped
play. The WilliamstdWn team left the ground,
and their opponents remaining until time was up,
the umpire gaVe his decision in their fa.vor.
The match between Fitzroy and South Mel-
bourne was also a rough and' tumble, and the
spectators witnessed the players, squaring up to
eich other. Sparrow, of the South team, was'
Carried off the ground.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted —
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Artie’eg,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with A us-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand, that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, JUNE 25, 1898.
Jim Crotty's Will.
There are so many issues of public import-
ance concerned that The Bulletin makes
no apology for returning to the subject of
old James Crotty’s last will and testament.
Crotty was the Lyell prospector who struck
it rich in liis declining years, and died
almost as soon as his wealth had arrived,
leaving behind him between £150,000 and
£200,000. Said wealth consisted mainly of
mining interests ; if there is a slump it will
at least partially evanish, and if, instead,
another Lyell boom sets in it may largely
increase. Probably the slump is the more
likely contingency of the two. But these
are mere speculations ; the important fact is
that Crotty’s pile of scrip and other proper-
ties were worth between £150,000 and
£200,000 when he went where the prospec-
tors cease from shovelling and the windlass
is at rest. And, being a pious man after the
orthodox kind of piety, and anxious to put
an immense fire-insurance on his soul,
Crotty left his wife £lOO a year (rather less
than £2 a week) for her life, and bequeathed
about £160,000 to his church. The Church
takes the bulk of the pile now, and when
the various annuitants on the estate (Mrs.
Crotty, with her hundred a year, being
one) die off’ it also takes the fund from which
their little incomes are paid. In fact, it
takes most of the fortune now and the rest
by-and-bye. And when he had made these
arrangements Jim Crotty died happy—at
least The Bulletin is more than half in-
clined to hope he didn’t.
* » * •
It is a tolerably loathsome thing—this
kind of posthumous benevolence. Crotty
didn’t give his pile to the Church while he
was living, because he wanted it for his own
use ; he gave it when he was dead because
he didn’t care one mean and selfish curse
whether his wife wanted it or not. He didn’t
deny himself in a good cause —he doubtless
considered it a good cause, anyhow —but he
denied his widow manfully. Crotty was a
martyr by proxy ; had he been an early
Christian in the days of the Cassars he
would probably have let Mrs. Crotty be
thrown to the lions rather than abjure his
faith, and have borne up manfully against
her sufferings. His presumable object was
to buy his soul a good front seat in the Here-
after— that was certainly one of his objects,
anyhow—and he didn’t care a straw though
he left his widow nothing at all to give to
the Church when -he died, so that she might
purchase a good front seat for her soul. She
hasn’t even the privilege of making her peace
with God by leaving her miserable 395. and
some coppers a week to the Church, for it
is fixed by Crotty\s will that it goes to the
Church anyhow as soon as she dies. Crotty
wanted the, whole pile to insure his own soul
with. And his piety made him a liar, as
well as a number of other unpleasant things.
When he married Mrs. Crotty he pre-
sumably endowed that lady with “ all his
worldly goods ” according to the usual for-
mula, and then he stole practically the whole
of these worldly goods back again. Accord-
ing to all the laws of God and man Mrs.
Crotty was his full partner in life. She
had to bear her share of his troubles m their
bad, unprosperous days, and when the good
days came she had one brief gleam of afflu-
ence, and now she has to go back to the old
struggle and the old sordid poverty because
her dead husband embezzled her share of the
family pile, and gave the steal to the Church.
If the Church hangs on to the steal and acts
as receiver of the ill-gotten pile—well, so
much the worse for the Church’s conscience.
* * * *
The law relating to the disposal of pro-
perty badly wants revision on a common-
sense basis. The law wouldn’t have allowed
the rich Jim Crotty, when he was alive, to
turn his wife adrift with less than £2 a week
to live upon. It would have compelled him
to maintain her in a fashion proportioned to
his means ; yet if the dead Crotty had
chosen to leave her utterly penniless the law
gave him ah olute power to do so. And why
the dead Crotty should have so much more
power than the live one passes all knowledge.
In The Bulletin's opinion, if the old man
who is dead leaves a widow and no children,
the widow should have an absolute right to
half his estate (or of it is so small that half
of it won’t furnish her with proper main-
tenance, to the whole of it) for her
lifetime, and the Church should at least be
required to wait till she is dead. The
Church claims that it lasts for ever, so it can
afford to wait; the lady is only temporary,
so she can’t. If there are young children
as well, they should have an absolute claim to
a certain fixed proportion of the pile till
they are of age, and the widow should also
have her claim to a fixed proportion for life.
The details of such a law could be easily
worked-out if the principle is kept in view
that the old woman is, by all the laws of
God and man, the old man’s full partner ;
that it is she, and not the. Church, that he
endows with all his worldly properties when
the partnership is first entered into, and
that the old man who is dead has no more
right to throw his wife out unprovided, or
nearly unprovided, for than has the old man
who is alive. Also, the law which allows a
rich man to absolutely disinherit his old
widow and his young children is a fool of a
law on mere business grounds, and contrary
to the public interest and the well-being of
the State. For if he doesn’t provide for
them, and they are unable to provide for
themselves, they must become a charge on
the community in some form, and the com-
munity has enough responsibilities already.
Moreover, it isn’t the community’s funeral.
* * * *
In addition to this, it would be both good
law and good justice to provide that, if the
old man dies, and his estate does not pan
out sufficient provision, according to a liberal
legal definition of the term, for his widow
and his young children, every gift over £5O
in value which he may have made within the
year or two years preceding his death to any
church or any church-managed institution
shall be null and void, and be recoverable
from the recipients. Otherwise the Jim
Crottys of this illogical world will take to
giving their goods to the Church by deed of
gift when convinced that they are’ past re-
covery, and again the old woman will be left
through the old man’s desperate anxiety to
save his valuable soul. The Church is the
one institution against which it is necessary
to make special provision of this kind. For
the world still retains a lingering belief in
hell, and when the average man is dying lie
believes in hell more than at any other
time.' And the Church is the one institu-
tion which knows all about hell, and
how to get there, and how not to get
there, so it has a hold on the illogical old
man who isn’t feeling well, such as no other
institution has or is ever likely to have.
The charitable old person who is not well
may take an unbiassed view of a bequest to
the Benevolent Asylum or the Decayed
Actors’ Fund, for neither of them professes
to be of any special benefit to him in the
Hereafter. But, while man believes in hell
and is scared at it, and there is a Church that
claims the prescriptive right to keep him
out of it, and the vague impression still
lingers that it may improve the chance
of keeping out of it if the Church is pro-
pitiated with hard cash, and w bile the Church
is willing to take the money, the average old
man will go on sacrificing the old woman for
the good of his own miserable old soul. And,
if- the hell that the old man is so anxious to
keep out of is a reality, his brute selfishness
as regards the old woman ought to get him
into it for a dead certainty.
* « * *
Another reason why bequests to the Church
should be severely discouraged is that it is
contrary to the public good that- great wealth
should accumulate in the hands of any one
institution religious or otherwise. The
Bulletin has a profound disrespect for all
Churches, though it disrespects the Holy
Roman sect less than the others. This slight
lack of disrespect isn’t based on religious
grounds. The Roman Church is old, for one
thing, and Churches and ruins are two things
which should always be old. A new Church
is a gimcrack thing ; a new ruin doesn’t com-
mand respect —it merely suggests that it was
run up by a shoddy builder in the boom
days, and that he borrowed the money from
a dishonest building society. There is a
long, picturesque history of battle and siege
and slaughter and old combusted city
about the Holy Roman creed ; it is full of
beautiful architecture, and fine music, and
artistically painted window, and pleasant
incense, and unintentionally humorous
legend ; and it has made the history
of Europe for the last 1000 years
or more. All the sand-drift of all the ages
will hardly fill the big footprint which it has
left on the shores of Time, and it is about the
most interesting thing to look back upon in
all the world’s history. But, as a thing to
look forward to, it is quite another matter.
It is about the biggest charitable institution
in the world, but all its vast charities tend,
directly or indirectly, to spread and consoli-
date its pow'er, and increase its wealth. So
do those of all other churches, but none of
them have the grit and endurance of the
tough old Roman hierarchy. And it is not
good for the Commonwealth that any indi-
vidual or any sect should accumulate
enormous wealth, for such an accumulation
only means that there is so much the less
left for the masses. In all the countries
where one Church is immensely rich, the
masses are miserably poor and ignorant —■
simply because the Church has so much that
there is very little left for anybody else.
And in this respect the Church is worse than
the individual plutocrat. Except in such
isolated cases as that of the Rothschilds,
the pile which the father heaps up is dissi-
pated by the son, or the grandson, or some
other descendant. The Hooleys, and
Norths, and Barnatos come and go like
shadows. The original “ Big Clarke
makes a pile, and son Joseph promptly dis-
sipates half of it on joy, and drink, and
widows, and it melts back again into the
great common fund. But the Church, in its
corporate capacity, doesn’t drink. It doesn’t
go after widows and set them up in public-
houses. It doesn’t sit up late over joyous
poker and champagne. Instead, it spreads
its influence and power more widely as its
pile grows bigger, and that is just what is
the matter with it. Also, that is just why
its friend, Jim Crotty, wants to be rigor-
ously put down.
To be Pasted in the Nation’s Hat.
When the N.S.W. general election arrives,
which will probably be about a month from the
present date, the fact that it will be fought out
on the Federal issue may be taken for granted.
There is no possible use in worrying over the
fiscal question till it is decided whether or not
the whole fiscal difficulty won’t be removed out
of local politics altogether and handed over to
a Federal Legislature. Moreover, there is very
little use in Freetraders troubling over their
fiscal issue, in any case, because they have got
it, and nobody runs after an omnibus when he
has overtaken the vehicle and is inside. And
when Lyne invites Protectionists to drop all
other questions and fix their minds on the
parochial tariff-problem, it is a fair thing for
them to consider how little Protection he has
ever got them, and how little such a very
slow and mechanical gentleman is ever likely
to get them ; and how much more good the
cause he recommends is likely to do to Lyne
than to the people at large, seeing that Lyne
gets all his political importance out of it, and
the people get nothing —mainly because Lyne
is never likely to get them anything out
of any cause. And, as to Council reform and
Local Government and womanhood suffrage,
these have stood over for very many years,
and can hardly be in such dire haste now that
it is necessary to drop the greatest live question
of the day unfinished in order to attend to
them.
* * * *
And, when N.S.W. starts out to consider the
Federal question at the poll, the one fact that
it has to paste in its hat for constant reference
is that George Reid has been all along the
worst obstruction in the path of Federation,
and that, judging by his past record, every
Federationist who trusts him will be “ left.”
Going solely by his record, The Bulletin be-
lieves that the only Federal scheme which Reid
will really support is the one which he is dead
sure none of the other provinces can accept.
To the people who want the old Bill, and the
people who want a new Bill, and the people
who want any kind of Bill, it is equally essen-
tial to find honest leaders ; the only individual
who can fittingly vote for Reid or for any Reid
supporter is the one who wants no Bill at all.
There is no need for anyone to risk his fiscal
faith, if the matter of his fiscal faith is precious
in his eyes. If the Freetrader gives his support
to Bruce Smith and Wise he can feel that the
cause of imported shoddy is still in good hands ;
and if the Protectionist planks his support on
Barton and Dick O’Connor he can rely on
getting a good run for his money, should the
N.S.W. Parliament, unhappily, ever have to
settle down to its local tariff wrangle once more
—which heaven forbid !
* * * *
The Bulletin invites the N.S.W. public to
consider Reid’s past Federal record of shuffle
and delusion, and gather from that the value of
his present promises. Until the end of 1894 lie
was a bitter provincialist running a bitterly
provincialist Cabinet. His chief colleague uas
Attorney General Want, who described Federa-
tion as “ a hydra-headed monster basking in
its constitutional beastliness in this bright and
sunny land,” and both Want and Reid, who
are now busily engaged in ladling out grease to
each other, lived up to this attitude. There is
still on the printed record Want’s assurance
that whenever the Federal question conies up
he will meet it “with an absolute opposit i<-n
to any question of Federation at all, 'without any
regard to what this Bill may contain.” He has
always lived up to that promise, and he has been
taken back to the bosom of the Reid Ministry
with it unbroken. But a year or two ago
Parkes was still alive and hungering to get bac k
to “ hoffis.” He couldn’t oust Reid on the
Foreign-trade ticket, for Reid was as good a
Foreign-trader as himself, so he started gather-
ing round him a new Federation party, which
might have grown to be a big party and
swamped Reid and Dibbs alike. So Premier
Reid promptly changed his convictions, and
became a Federationist for the first time, and
instigated the meeting of Premiers at Hobart,
which started Lite present Federal movement.
He stated that he did this “ to draw Pakkes’s
teeth,” and he certainly drew Parkes’s last
tooth and removed the only visible excuse for
Parkes’s continuance in politics. But, when
the politically-toothless old man dropped out,
and the only reason why Reid should be a
Federationist vanished, that wily trimmer
grew cold. He spent nearly a year passing the
Enabling Act. He started at once to blight
the cause of union by inserting the 50,000
minimum. He further blighted it later on by
permitting the insertion of an 80,000 minimum.
When the first battle arose—the fight between
the Federationists and the anti-Federal Want
and Heydon crowd for the election of delegates
to the Convention — Reid was almost the only
prominent politician who conspicuously stayed
out of the shindy. And, when the Convention
Bill was drafted, it was the same Reid who pro-
posed postponing the Referendum for six
months, whereby the subject would have a fair
chance to grow cold and die of-neglect and
frost-bite.
* * * »
The Convention Bill failed in N.S.W. mainly
because of the things that Reid either insisted
on putting in it, or quietly voted for putting in
it, or allowed to go into it without any protest
at the time—he certainly grumbled about some
of them when it was too late —and which things
he and his satellites denounced afterwards
as insuperable objections to the Bill. The
arrangement that, when the two Federal Houses
of Parliament sit together to consider a question
on which a deadlock has arisen, it shall require
a vote of three-fifths of the members present to
carry the Bill, instead of a simple majority,
which is the truest democratic principle—is one
of Reid’s chief objections to the Convention
Bill. Reid voted over and over again for the
three-fifths majority and also spoke strongly
in support of it, and opposed Turner’s de-
mand for a simple majority. Another reason
why he fought hard to secure the defeat of the
Convention Bill at the poll on June 3 was that
it didn’t provide definitely for Federal railway
ownership. Reid voted against Federal rail-
way ownership. He further objects to the Bill
because it provides, in a vague sort of fashion,
that the Federal Government may take over
railways, provided the State they belong to con-
sents, and he points out as the probable result
of this arrangement that thd Federal Govern-
ment might take over Victoria’s railways and
compete with ruinously low rates for the
N.S.W. Riverina trade, and make N.S.W.
stand a share of the loss incurred in cutting
rates against her. Reid voted in the Conven-
tion for the railway arrangement he now so
bitterly objects to. He also complains in a
loud and strident wail about the absurdity of
giving Tasmania, with 170,000 population, the
same representation in the Federal Senate as
N.S.W. with 1,300,000. Yet he voted for
giving all the States equal representation in
the Senate, and called it “one of the first
principles of the Federal enterprise.” And
when he complains that the power of amend-
ment is inadequate and the Federal Constitution
“ cast-iron,” the short answer is that he allowed
the clause which prescribes the method of
amendment to pass unopposed and without
even a division.
* * * *
In addition to these matters Premier Reid
did not vote against the clause allowing the
States to grant bounties “on their own” till
30th June ; he didn’t even call for a division
on it. He voted for the clause he now so
mournfully laments about, under which the
surplus—after the first five years of Federation
—is to be divided on such basis as the Federal
Parliament considers fair, instead of according
to population. And, whereas he complains that
this is a provision under which the small pro-
vinces (especially the smallest province of all
—Tasmania) are reaching out after the N.S. W.
surplus to divide it among them according to
their necessities, the real fact is that Braddon,
Fysh, and five more —seven in all—out of the
ten Tasmanian delegates voted for dividing the
surplus according to population, by which
method Tasmania would have the smallest
share. The method which Reid objects to and
describes as a conspiracy of the small provinces
against N.S.W. was forced on the small pro-
vinces by Reid and six other N.S.W. delegates
who voted with him. In fact, every matter of
any importance which Premier Reid objects to
in the Federal Bill was a matter which he
directly or indirectly assisted to put there. As
a rule, he assisted directly.
* » * *
There is only one explanation, apart from
mere monkeyish mischief,'which seems to fully
account for Premier Reid’s attitude. That is
that his object was all along to wreck “ Bar-
ton’s Bill.” Reid was the only Premier who
was a bad second at the Federal poll, and ho


was headed by a political opponent. The same
political opponent was appointed leader of the
Convention. When the first Federal Ministry
is formed, in the ordinary course of things
Reid and Turner, as the Premiers of the two
leading provinces, would have first choiceof port-
folios. As matters stand, Barton and Turner
would probably have first choice. When four
Protectionist and one Freetrade province unite,
the probabilities are that the Federal Ministry
will be Protectionist, and Reid is the only
provincial Premier who couldn’t well take a
portfolio and a comfortable salary in a Federal
Protectionist Ministry. It would be a pitiful
fall to remain behind as leader of a mere pro-
vincial Parliament while Barton, Turner,
Kingston and others were running Australia,
and it would be highly unprofitable to be a mere
Leader, or even something less, than Leader, of
a weak Federal Opposition. Reid’s whole
course of conduct offers ground for two ugly
suspicions. One is that he deliberately aimed
at wrecking the Bill for which Barton was
largely responsible by putting things in it to
which he could afterwards take objection; that
he deliberately tried to discredit Barton, and
is now reaching out for the leadership of the
movement in N.S. W. by starting a new Federal
scheme from which Barton is shut out. His
proposal that the Premiers (including himself)
should draft an amended Bill without reference
to the other delegates (including Barton) cer-
tainly looks like that. The other suspicion is
that he was driven to become a Federationist for
fear that, otherwise, Federation would be the
stalking-horse on which Parkks would go back
to power, and that when Parkes vanished from
the scene and Barton quitted Parliament his
motive was gone, and he relapsed into the
same old provincialist as before. In other
words, that he wasn’t merely trying to wreck
“ Barton’s Bill,” but to wreck Federation in
general. His apparent unwillingness to meet
Turner, even upon his own invitation, and to
specify his own amendments, and his remark-
able conduct in the-matter of the 80,000 mini-
mum, certainly look like that. Another pos-
sible explanation is that he doesn’t know his
o.vn mind, and never did, and hasn’t
got any. At all events, his own explana-
tion that he only voted for all these
things that he objected to because they
were the best concessions he could get is
absurdly thin ; a man at least asks for what he
wants before he commences to loudly demand
what he doesn’t want. On Reid’s own show-
ing, because he is a Foreign-trader, he should
be howling his loudest for Protection lest he
should get something worse in the shape of
absolute Prohibition. Whatever view is taken
of the famous N.S.W. Wriggler, and his
reasons, and his possible reasons, the one thing
perfectly certain is that the politician who,
above all others, can’t be trusted to conduct
any kind of a Federal movement—the one
whose promise is of no value for any political
purpose beneath the sun—is George Houston
Reid.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Most Inaccurate Politician on
Record.
Premier Reid’s speech at Miltou was partly a
weak attempt to raise up the old fiscal ghost,
and a revival of the ancient yelp about how
N.S.W. is getting on as compared with Vic-
toria. Queensland and Maoriland are just as
Protectionist as Victoria, but the latter pro-
vince having been badly struck by the boom
collapse, and being only one-quarter the size of
N.S.W. and 50 years younger, is the one alw'ays
used for purposes of comparison. A few samples
of Reid :
Now, in shipping’, the increase in 10 years in New South
Wales is 2,400,000 tons a year. It has increased from
4,300,000 to 6,700,000 tons a year, or 1,500,000 tons more
than it increased in Victoria during the same period, and
it would be 2,000,000 tons more if Victoria did not enter
the big mail steamers four times on each voyage. Now
for the effect on agriculture. ... In the two years 1895-6
against 1897-8 there has been an increase in the area
under crop in New South Wales of 473,000 acres. In two
years there has been an increase of 35 per cent, of culti-
vation over all the cultivation of the previous 110 years.
. . . During the period that the area under crop in-
creased by 35 per cent., the Victorian cultivation area,
under their enormous duties, did not increase 10 per
cent, even in their wheat area, in the same two years
that there was an increase in the area under wheat
in New South Wales of no less than 66 per cent. . . .
Another portion of the community to be blighted was the
artisans. Well, in 1896, after the repeal of the advalorem
duties on January 1, there were 49,055 hands in New
South Wales manufactories. These figures, by an
arrangement between Mr. Fknton and Mr. Cogiilan, are
given on the Victorian basis. This number was 5500
more than in 1887, 10 years before. Now, let us look at
Victoria in the same 10 years. They had only increased
the number of their hands by 964. ... In 1897, instead
of 49,084, there were 50,448, or double the increase in
Victoria in 10 years. ,Of course, in mining production,
our resources account for our great production; but it
will be interesting to know that the increase of miners in
New South Wales during the past 10 years has been no
less than 22,909. In 1887 there were 18,329, and in 1897
41,208. Then, in 1896, we had 7900 more than Victoria.
Now, let us look at the savings-banks. I find that in the
two years 1894 to 1896 that our deposits increased by
£1,305,000—a greater increase than all the savings-banks
of Victoria, Queensland and South Australia. In Victoria
in 1896 the increase was £542,009, Queensland £334,000,
South Australia £380,000—-or a total of £1,256,000 for the
three colonies.
Ib all reads very beautiful when it is set down in
that way, but then so much depends on how
figures are put. The inter-provincial boats which
run from Melbourne to the Queensland ports
are counted at Sydney four times just as the
Sydney mail-boats are reckoned at Melbourne
—when they arrive and depart going up, and
when they arrive and depart coming down.
The whole of N.S.W.’s shipping superiority
depends on its coal export trade ; if it stood
on the same level as Victoria, which never
had any coal to export, it would be much
below its small neighbour. N.S.W. has
now, despite all the boasted increase, only
992,000 acres under wheat as compared with
Victoria’s 1,638,000 acres. The alleged increase
of 5500 in the number of artisans employed in
N.S.W. (if Reid did put it in that form) is
pure Ananias ; the increase was from 43,527
to 45,222, an advance of 1695. Then by the
new method of computation, which reckons a
place employing four hands only as a factory,
the number was raised to 49,055, but if the
same new method had been applied in 1887 the
figures might then have been 50,000 for all G.
Reid knows to the contrary. Exactly what
the N. S. W. Statistician says is that the number
of factory employes was 43,527 under the old
mode of calculation in 1887, and in 1896 it was
45,222 under the same old mode of calculation,
or 49,055 under a new mode then employed for
the first time. The same year, the Victorian
factories employed over 50,000 people. By
taking six years instead of ten, it can be shown
quite as truly that N.S.W. industries are
decreasing—lB9l, 46,135 hands employed ;
1896,■ 45,222 only. As for the savings-bank
yarn, it is simply the usual impudent fake
of the most brassy politician that Australia has
ever possessed. The increase in N.S.W. de-
posits was £1 ,305,000, and, in the other three
provinces, it was .£1,966,000. Reid takes two
years’ figures for N.S.W. and compares them
with one year’s figures for Victoria and Queens-
land, and with some figures for S.A., which
seem to be pure muddle, for they are utterly
unlike the published statistics. The fact that
G. Reid’s figures are always wrong might be
set down to mere stupidity but for the lament-
able fact that they are always wrong in his own
favor and so as to boost up his own argument.
That is the reason why this paper has given up
all hope of meeting him in another and a
brighter world.

Legal By-Paths.
“ S.C.C.” on Judicial Creations versus Legisla-
lative Creations
Ed. Bulletin. —Since the hanging-up of the laws of the
Twelve Tables the exclusiveness of the legal profession
has been as a thorn in the side of the laymen, and various
are the attempts which, from time to time, have been
made to open its portals to the ordinary plebeian who
has had the necessary experience, and who happens to
possess enough brains to pass the examinations.
This exclusiveness is perhaps not without a certain
amount of reason and common-sense—its main redeem-
ing feature, no doubt, being the prevention of too great
an overcrowding of a profession which may be said to
have followers enough.
The rules of N.S.W. Supreme Court of Tuesday the
18th day of December, 1877, provide (sub-clause 5 of
section 3) that persons who at the time of the passing of
those rules had completed, or who had commenced and
should complete the term of five years of clerkship in the
office of the Supreme Court or that of the Crown Solici-
tor, and who should pass the final examination required
by the said rules, should be deemed to be “ articled ”
clerks within the said rules.
Now, that was sufficiently invidious, considering that
the only other way to enter the profession was to pay a
high price for “ articles,’' in addition to passing three or
four examinations and paying the fees incidental thereto.
But it is admitted that at that time the profession was
not so overcrowded that the few who could have entered
it under that fifth sub-clause would be likely to make any
material difference in over-running the profession,
because, it will be observed, that only those who, at the
time of the passing of those rules, had then commenced or
completed the five years, could so enter this glorified
ring of lawyers.
Having in view, doubtless, the privilege thus extended
to this branch of the civil-service, some few years ago a
considerable effort was made to pass a law througirthe
"Leg. Assembly enabling those who had been managing-
clerks in a law-office for ten years and upwards to qualify
by passing the examinations. The measure was smothered,
however, by the weight of “ vested interest” brought to
bear upon or rather against it. The result was that
many an efficient and capable man was compelled to re-
main satisfiv i with the position of “ managing-clerk ”
because of the jealousy of the legal profession (including
their honors on the bench) which arrayed itself against
him.
The Judges, however, appear lately to have waxed
kindly towards that oppressed branch of the civil-service
engaged in the Supreme Court and Crown-Solicitor’s
offices, for, on the 7th day of June instant, they repealed
sub-clause five of section three of the aforesaid rules, sub-
stituting in its stead the following :
“ Any person who has, or who hereafter shall havecom-
pleted the term of five years of clerkship in the offices of
the Supreme Court or the office of the Crown Solicitor,
and who shall produce a certificate from the head of his
office that he has for at least two of such five years been
chief clerk or a clerk in charge of any. branch of any one
of such offices —provided that such person must pass the
final examination required by the rules relating to the
admission of attorneys, for the purposes of which exami-
nation such person shall be deemed to be an articled clerk
within the meaning of the said rules relating to the ad-
mission of attorneys.”
What is the effect of this substituted sub-clause ?
That from this out every clerk who has completed five
years in the offices of the Supreme Court or Crown Solici-
tor (two of such years as “ chief ” clerk) may qualify by
passing the “final” examination.
But what of the “ managing law clerk?” He remains
in statu quo and also retains the blessed privilege of con-
tributing to the fat salary of the favored civil-servant
employed in Crown Solicitor’s or Supreme Court offices.
Meanwhile, the private “ articled ” person goes on pay-
ing his premium and toiling without a fat salary. He also
is privileged to contribute to the salaries of those favored
civil-servants who may one day, by an easy bv-path, enter
his coterie and wrest the bread from his mouth.
Talk of vested interests! If this invidious sub-clause
does not preserve the vested interests of that privileged
few, then, in conscience, commend me to something that
does !
A peculiarity in this connection is this. Although the
law part of the Civil Service is thus provided with a near
cut to the sacred legal profession, should a member of
the latter aspire to fill a position in the “professional”
grade of the C.S., the C.S. Board requires him (though
duly qualified) to undergo a further examination !
I venture to think that from the foregoing I have made
it abundantly clear that there is room for a little more of
both equity and common sense in some of these matters.

An Outlying Railway.
In all that half of N.S.W. west of Hay, Cobar,
and Bourke there are just about 74 miles of
railroad, all of it privately owned. Of this, 36
miles belong to the gorgeous Silverton co.’s
monopoly, which grinds 50 per cent, dividends
out of the Broken Hill public by its enormous
fares and freight charges ; the other 38 miles
is the property of the Tarrawingee Flux co.
The latter co. is going out of business. Its
quarries have closed up, because the re-
moval of the smelting industry from the
Barrier to the S.A. sea-board has re-
duced the demand for flux to about
nil, and, as it can’t keep up its big
Melbourne office and management and board
of directors, merely for the sake of being
a small railway co. humping goods and wool
for the scattered pastoralists north of the
Barrier, its line is for sale. It is offered to the
N.S.W. Government for £ls,ooo—less than
£4OO a mile, a mere fraction of its original
cost, about one-tenth the cost per mfle of the
cheap line which runs over the plains to Cobar,
and about one-fifteenth of the cost per mile
of the other cheap line which runs to
Bourke. If the State doesn’t buy, the
line will be torn up and the materials sold,
and more than half of N.S.W.’s far-west rail-
way system will have vanished. Apparently
the main objections to the purchase are no"t
based on the unprofitable nature of the deal.
The interest bill would be only £450 a yearj
and even a very poor railway couldn’t lose
much on these terms. When the private
Silverton line falls into the hands of the State
through the company’s concession running out,
and the Cobar railway is extended to the
Barrier, and the two form a through line to
Adelaide, the little Tarrawingee road would
be the beginning of a much-needed western
branch system. It couldn’t involve the loss of
much money in the interim, and it affords an
outlet for a district which has, otherwise, no
railways, and which has few roads and no
reliable water-carriage. The great trouble
seems to be that it allows an outlet for the
local trade to Adelaide, instead of directing it to
far-off Sydney, and, if the line is torn up, a few
more pastoralists will haul their wool at great
expense over the immeasurable plains to the
N.S. W. lines and send it to swell the trade of
Sydneyand the profits of the Sydney middlemen.
It is the same policy which led the present
N.S. W. Government to break the agreement
which Dibbs entered into to join Victoria in
constructing the much-needed Murray bridge
at Cobram, and even to refuse Victoria the
right to build the bridge wholly at its own
expense. The Cobram bridge would give
adjacent settlers a short road to the Melbourne
market only 155 miles distant, whereas they
now have to laboriously haul their pro-
duce to Finley and then send it by
train 450 miles to Sydney, so they have to
be sacrificed to the interests of the bloated
metropolis. And apparently the desire of the
N.S. W. Government to get rid of the Tarra-
wingee railroad is based on similar considera-
tions. In N.S.W. practical politics the Federal
spirit is so weak that it needs to lean up
against the Sydney Telegraph’s anti-Billious
statistics for support. They are strong enough
to support anything.
Premier Reid’s intimation that he will support
the N.S.W. Cold Water brigade in legislating
to prevent the issue of new publicans’ licenses
is a tricky election cry that yields not a whisper
of consolation to intelligent temperance work-
ers. Such legislation will leave the drinking
habits of the people exactly as they are. The
pubs now in existence will be protected from
the risks of fresh competitors. In fact, the
Reid scheme is an application of drastic Pro-
tectionism to the existing community of whisky-
mills, and is now obviously expounded to win
the voting influence of pub-owners, their brew-
ers and distillers. The temperance party should
scout such support. If Reid wishes to honestly
help the public and the temperance party let
him force the Licensing Magistrates to refuse
licenses to all tied houses in the trade. Brew-
ers and distillers now hold numbers of publi-
cans by the throat and make square dealing
on their part impossible. No publiean who is
simply a servant or agent should receive a
license. That is a beneficial and easily-under-
stood reform. But then it would lose Reid the
votes and influence of brewers and dist.llers.
Tiie N.S.W. Labor League has decided that its
attitude Federation at the coming general
election shall consist mainly of this one pro-
position : “ That the Federal Constitution
should be amendable by a simple majority of
the citizens of the Commonwealth.” This is
cood democratic theory, but if it is adhered to
it ends Federation for the present in practice.
For if all the six provinces federated on this
basis, the fact remains that a good-sized majority
in N.S.W. and Victoria could alter the Cousti-
tution any way it pleased, and if any question
arose on which the interests of these two
provinces were identical, the other pro-
vinces would have no voice at all. And the
small provinces, logically or illogically, are
not likely to take the risk ; it would be too
much like practical re-annexation to N.S.W.
In such a case it wouldn’t matter how
liberal were the concessions which the large
States made to the small ones when the bar-
gain was struck, because the large States could
take them all back immediately afterwards.
The large States would probably not do any-
thing that their people didn’t consider just,
but the trouble with the small States would be
that everybody’s idea of justice is so liable to
be biassed by his interests. If the Constitution
can be altered in any and every particular by
the two large States, and an entirely new Con-
stitution created within the first year or two
after the Commonwealth is established, then
the four small States will probably decide that
it would be just the same thing for them if they
federated without any Constitution at all.
Melb. Age— i.e., David Syme —couldn’t find
courage to enthuse for Federation until a big
Victorian majority looked a sure thing. A
character for tremendous political influence is
a terrible possession in plebiscite times, when
the country is thinking a lot, but not saying
much. David Syme’s early fears of becoming
a shattered oracle could be seen in the Age’s
leading article, the morning after the vote :
During the recent Convention, no indication was given
by the majority of the people that they looked upon the
question as more than an academic exercise in dialectics.
There was no outward evidence that they felt much, if
any, of the significance of the movement of which they
were constantly told by their delegates and by the press.
No one could have anticipated, even so recently as three
months ago, that Federation would become, as it did, a
warmly discussed question in the province.
During those three months the oracle sat
with his weather-eye turned in half-a-dozen
directions, watching for signs. Meanwhile,
the other morning paper rushed in and
barked for Federation so vehemently that
David’s only chance of creating a stir would
have been in barking for the Anti-Feds. So
he waited and waited, and when there was no
visible hope of a majority for Higgins he cried
“ Yes ” in a sulky tone, and nobody remarked
that the oracle had spoken. The Voice of the
People went “on its own,” and poor David
felt like one crying “ Hallelujah ! ” when no-
body can be heard above the drum. Next time
Victoria has a movement on hand the Oracle
will probably make a rash guess at the result
and cast his influence into the wrong box. He
has lost his nerve. The knowledge that he is
expected to foresee the future makes everything
seem misty before his eyes, and at the present
moment Oracle David is positively incapable of
shouting directions to the country.
Judging by the Commonwealth Bill voting, the
day will yet arrive when the average Austra-
lian will have to be taken “ by the scruff” and
violently dragged to the poll and there com-
pelled to vote. The sooner that day arrives
the better. Taking the city and suburban con-
stituencies only, where the voter cannot plead
distance or bad roads as a reason for his neglect
of duty, in Sydney and suburbs, out of 108,717
electors, only 52,587 voted ; in Melbourne, out
of 91,807 —which, however, includes the plural
voters —only 47,680 thought it worth while to
walk across the road; while in Adelaide—the
most fearsome example of the Jot—out of 54,648
electors, but 21,964 roused themselves suffi-
ciently to overcome “that tired feeling ” and
go and mark a piece of paper. Quite a number
of respectable citizens having duly considered
the matter, arrived at the booths only to find
their names missing from the roll. Then there
was much “ langwidge”— but that didn’t alter
the vote. Ballance’s plan seems to be the
best—put every man (or adult) on the roll, and
as long as he votes at every election let his
name remain ; but should he, without sufficient
reason, omit to vote, remove his name forth-
with. Then it would be open for him either
to get his name replaced—at some little trouble
—or to join the “ society for being too tired to
do anything.”

“ Pedagogue ” tells some home-truths :
Ed. Bulletin.— The public, represented by the news-
paper correspondents, are up in arms because the N.S.W.
railway people are running special excursion trains for
the convenience of school-teachers. Whenever increased
traffic is expected, whether on account of public holi-
days, races, cricket-matches, shows, or whatnot, the
Commissioners run “ Special Excursion.” There’s’ one
this week for the Sheep Show. Then why not for
teachers when they number thousands, and the reduced
fares will result in increased traffic? Strange that
teachers —as a class—are so unpopular with the public. Is
it because they unconsciously assume in general life the
superior, authoritative demeanor of the school-room?
Are they too dogmatic? Too supercilious ? Whatever
it is they rank next to bailiffs in unpopularity. They are
considered to be overpaid and underworked—they have
Saturday “ off,” and have more holidays than any other
class. As far as the pay goes, just look at it A pupil-
teacher starts at £4O a year, and if he qualifies himself
by exams, and results, his salary rises successively to
£46, ±57 and £6B. Then he spends a year, or perhaps
two in “ training.” When he comes out he gets £96
next year £lO4, and finally £ll3. In other words, after
eight years’ service and a course of study that would have
fitted him for any of the professions he gets £2 5s a
week. For this he is buried in the bush among people
whose ideas, as a rule, never rise above sheep. The pub-
lic doesn’t realise the loneliness, the monotony the
physical discomfort, and the humiliations an educated
miserably-paid man must suffer in such dreary isolation’
The man would go mad if he did not have an occasionai
glimpse of civilisation. And to get it he frequently pav3
a month’s salary in travelling expenses. Head teachers
of big schools get fair salaries and have good times ap-
parent.ly, but how many years’ experience has qualified
them for the position ? Teachers are moulding the future
of Australia, and the public want it done at starvation
rates.
The N.S.W. Police Superannuation Fund
has gone utterly broke. Every man who joins the
force has to sign an agreement by which 21 per
cent. (Gd. in the £) of Ins pay is deducted and goes
into the Superannuation Fund. In return, when
he retires aged GO, after 30 years’ .service, he is en-
titled to a pension equal to the salary he was re-
ceiving at the time of his retirement. And about
100 officers who have paid the compulsory levy
for 30 years and more, and whose pensions are
now due, are informed that they can’t get a cent
in their old age because the money has disap-
peared, and there is no fund, and the whole thing
is a myth. The only thing left for them to do
is to run each other in for vagrancy. A monstrous
tate of things. Repudiation with a big R,
Tasmania to New South. Wales,
4th June, 1898.
You, who proudly claim the title “Mother State”
—is this well done ?
You, who raised the cry of “Union!” —prayed
the warring States be one !
They have answered ; rings their answer strong,
reverberant and true !
Answered “ Yes ! ” in rolling thunder,
Mother State —and you ?
You, who led the van, to falter ! you, who cheered
us on, to fail !
You, who summoned us to enter, you to stand
aside and pale !
We have followed, fought, and conquered ; burst
the barriers through and through ;
Sprung towards you glad, exultant,
Mother State ! —and you ?
You, the rich, the strong, the mighty, shamed by
us, the poor, the weak !
We had trust in you, our Mother ; dared ye not
our trust to seek ?
Prophets prophesied disaster ; yet we ventured :
“ Dare and Do ! ”
Was our watchword ; and we kept it,
Mother State ! —but you
Few in number, heavy burdened, we found heart
to face the cost;
Fearful, timid, stunned with warnings, to the
winds our fears we tossed !
Dared to face the hidden future, dared believe
where no man knew,
Dared be worthy of our lineage,
Mother State !—and you?
Have we proved ourselves unworthy? Read our
record ! let it tell
How we bare what came upon us bravely, silently
and well ;
How we faced our adverse fortune, cleared the
ground, commenced anew.
Strangers praised us ; strangers trusted,
Mother State ! —but you ?
Did you doubt our self-reliance ? fear we might
unfairly lean
On your strength ? O fear unworthy ! we shall be
what we have been.
None who trusted us have ever yet had cause their
trust to rue;
In our honor we are equal,
Mother State ! with you.
Not in anger! not in anger ! speak we thus, but
sad of heart;
Ye were blinded ! ye were blinded ! saw us through
a mist apart;
Fooled by jealous, vain self-seekers, not to you let
blame accrue,
’Gainst them are our hearts hot; never,
Mother State ! ’gainst you.
Ming away the base suspicions ! You can trust
us, we are leal;
Though our hands be few and feeble, yet our
hearts are true as steel.
One in blood, in speech, in fortune; one in faith
and freedom, who
Dares to part us ? Strangers cannot—
Mother State ! will you ?
Hobart.
W. H. DAWSON.

At Her Majesty’s (Sydney) the last lion
ate the last Christian on Friday night, and on
Saturday, the stately pageant of the “Royal
Divorce, ’ made a very popular re-appearance.
Ihe new “Divorce” has—or else through some
cunning re-arrangement of the lights it seems to
have one distinct advantage over the previous
one. The retreat from Moscow appears to be
shifted further to the front, and the frozen
legion plodding along through the snow with its
frozen Bonaparte in the front rank looks less
misty than it did. That fleeting view of the van-
guard of the 500,000 men who had an appalling
cold in the head in a strange land was always the
least magnificent of the four great pictures, partly
because it is a small gloomy legion buried in its
overcoat, and partly because the city doesn’t burn
naturally in the back-ground. The improvement
J ®> therefore, all the more welcome. Otherwise,
the “Divorce’’ is the same fine upstanding out-
rage on all historical accuracy as it was aforetime,
and the two huge Waterloo tableaux blaze forth
with a pomp and glitter, and a shriek of red
uniforms, and a wild rush of painted horse at the
back and real horse in front, and a boom of
cannon, that are good for the soul. After the
average man has seen it he feels so full of martial
ardour that it is with the greatest difficulty he
restrains himself from going out and assaulting
the aged and infirm pea-nut vendor on the spot.
The great cloud of battle-smoke which drifts
across the bald heads in the stalls, and the gaudy
King of Naples in the front rank of the retreat
with a splash of blood on his face, are some more
patches of realism which make him feel equal to
killing the pea-nut vendor right there. And the
last picture of all —the lone Bonaparte on his rock
looking out at a red sun which sets because the
sea climbs up in front of it —is an effect which
calls for loud admiration.
* * * *
In a literary sense, it is better not to con-
sider the “ Divorce ” too closely. Its dialogue is
only respectable, and not always so good as that.
Its historical inaccuracy rises to a pinnacle of
bosh that is above the line of perpetual snow.
The, tawdry appeal to British sentiment in Napo-
leon’s oration about the fighting qualities of the
English, the night before the great Irishman
banged him at Waterloo, is ridiculous ; and the
only big streak of mirth against the background
of woe is the last inhabitant of Jenappes trying to
drink up all the town’s abandoned stock of liquor.
But, as a pictorial effort, it is all there, and the co.
does it full justice, and wears its gaudy plumes
and its gay uniform bravely to the bitter end.
The pomp of that flight from Waterloo alone looks
as if the whole European national debt was run-
ning away simultaneously. There are no changes
of any importance in the cast, and the people
who were favorably commented on last time the
“Divorce” came this way may consider the
remarks still standing in type. A brief revival of
the “ Prisoner of Zenda ” follows.
** * *
Durward Lely, and his songs, and the sweet*
violin of Elly Fusch, are on view at. Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, at the same remarkably low prices as
heretofore. Lely is a hard-working volcano who
erupts music every night. In this respect he is
unlike the average musical star, who is generally
an intermittent geyser and only blows off melody
twice a week or so, at high prices. Yet Lely’s
fine vocal organ seems to stand the strain without
the slightest difficulty. Very few Caledonian
songsters that Australia has met with have made
the old haggis melodies sound so beautiful as Lely
contrives to do, and many people who never
respected that kind of music before will hold it in
high esteem hereafter for his sake. As for the
plump and pleasing Elly Fusch, she and her violin
fill a void in this paper s soul, to the great surprise
of the paper itself, which wasn’t quite sure that it
had one. Till Elly Fusch came along and filled
the void it had set down the inward yearning to
indigestion.
** * *
The present Bland Holt show pegs away
prosperously at Sydney Royal. No change of
bill is mentioned, so far. The bright red hue of
“ Straight from the Heart ” has a cheering effect
these cold, damp nights, and the only matter
which anyone can really object to is the dark
mystery which enshrouds the fate of Albert Nor-
man. Just when Baker is going to be beheaded,
in the last act, the comrades of that very bad
anarchist discover that he has plaved them false,
and range up one on each side of “him, and lead
him away to have a drink. He never comes back.
Whether the drink was poisoned, or whether
Norman was brained with the bottle, or whether
he escaped while the other two anarchists are
struggling to get out the cork, there is absolutely
nothing to show. His fate, whatever it was, is
evidently some kind of a proof of the horrors of
drink, but it is a very gloomy problem, and
further information will be thankfully received at
this office any day between 10 and 4
** * *
The Lyceum has closed for four nights
while the management makes tremendous prepara-
tions inside for the production of “ Indiana, or a
Woman’s Vengeance.” Indiana, if that was the
lady’s name—possibly it was Sarah—will revenge
herself under circumstances of great pomp on
Saturday night, the fates permitting.
** * *
In the absence of any more definite re-
marks to offer it is merely chronicled, in a vague
sort of way, that Sydney Tivoli appears to be a
paying spec, as usual.
** * *
A big charity benefit is to be given at Her
Majesty's, Sydney, to-morrow (Friday) afternoon.
Half the proceeds go to various established
charities, and the other half to help the widows
and orphans of those mariners who went down
during the recent gales to dwell with the bloated
barracouta and the remains of the lost galleon
that was overdue in Acapulco Bay three centuries
ago. All the companies in Sydney will bear a
hand on this great occasion. The Firm gives its
theatre and supplies Act 111. of the “ Royal
Divorce.” For this occasion only, J. B. Athol-
wood will retreat from Moscow in place of the
star. Atholwood usually represents that last lone
drunk who is seeing snakes at Jenappes when
the French army arrives. The Bulletin, as
a paper with a large experience of liquor,
has always greatly admired that oldest and drunk-
est inhabitant, and will be pleased any day to see
Atholwood retreating from Moscow. Julius
Knight can’t retreat from Moscow personally
because he will be busy just then getting into his
most beautiful clothes to do the balcony-scene
from “Romeo and Juliet” with Miss Elliott
Page. One act of Bland Holt’s drama, “ One of
the Best,” with Cyril Keightley in Baker’s old
part as the hero, will be humped down from the
Royal and staged with Bland’s usual magnificence.
The Lyceum co. supplies the fifth act of the “Sea
of Ice ’ —the one w here the bad man wins his ill-
gotten girl, and goes slowly mad for love of her,
and then crumples up all of a sudden like a bell-
topper that an elephant has sat on. In addition
to all this, Miss Billie Howarde, of the Royal
Standard, and Rickards, Geo. Wood and Miss
Peggy Pryde, of the Tivoli, do various things on
their own. The public trusts that the loot will be
enormous, and that the souls of the dead mariners
wandering on the sands at the bottom and dodging
the sunken ships that drop on them from above,
will rest easier by reason of it.
** # *
At Melb. Royal, on Saturday, the revival
of Titheradge as Silver King brushed away any
cobwebs of doubt that may have gathered round
his reputation for absolute supremacy in this
popular character. Half-a-dozen imported Wil-
fred Denvers have arisen since Titheradge’s first
time. For the unswerving adherents of that way-
back identity there is much satisfaction in being
able to justify their faith with his present per-
formance. Titheradge to-day is so distinctly the
best Denver we have seen that he will perhaps
get credit for surpassing his original self, which
he probably doesn’t. The point is not worth dis-
cussion, . anyhow. Enough to be reminded of
his special excellence in the murder-scene, where
he wakes up to discover a moist, unpleasant
body on the other side of the table. His
numerous successors have each seemed weak
or artificial, by comparison, in their treat-
ment of the corpse episode, and it was here
again that Titheradge made his strongest effects
on Saturday night. To think of Wilson
Barrett’s recent melodramatic attempt at impress-
ing an audience in the same scene was to feel the
difference between a naturally clever, sincere
actor and a laborious mummer. The live, per-
pendicular Geoffrey Ware, at the Royal, is a
lamentable young man, but his dead body comes
in particularly useful.
* * * *
The company supporting the “star” is
mostly below the average “ Silver King” stand-
ard and some of it is very poor quality. Hans
1 hillips s Corkect is a exception, likewise
little Miss Holloway, as Cissie. George Chalmers
makes an amusing Cripps, and three or four of
the little parts are neatly filled in. On the other
side of the ledger John O’Neill is a tiresome
I ather Christmas; the Spider is gaudily uncon-
vincing ; Miss Duggan plays havoc with the fine
part of Denver’s wife ; Miss Gresham isn’t a
forcible Mrs, Spider ; and Charles Holloway will
be a fairly passable Jaikes when he knows his lines
by heart, and starves in a more poverty-stricken
suit than he wore on Saturday. But the play
beautifully mounted as usual, is likely to flourish
for a week in spite of these troubles.
* • • •
Titheradge, after fifteen years’ residence
Australia, is to make a farewell appearance
this Saturday (25th) as Wilfred Denver afore-
said. For which occasion only prices will be
raised to the old original tariff, so that the de-
parting artist may take a benefit worthy of his
first-class record.
** * *
“La Perichole,” at Melb. Bijou, is a
respectable amateur struggle (by the Australian
Opera Club) with one of the dullest old opera-
bouffes. “La Perichole ” dates back to the time
when pantomime noses were humorous, and light
musical entertainment was divided into four acts
and the world laughed easily. Emelie Melville
made it attractive, but times have changed. Also
the A.O.C, performers are not Emelie Melville
and co., neither have they seen a professional co.
on this ancient job. If the Bijou show is to
benefit the local hospitals, the balance of cash in
hand will be due to Melbourne’s splendid appetite
for any sort of musical story with a short-skirted
chorus. Miss Ray Jones is La Perichole, the
young woman who gets married under the in-
fluence of mixed drinks, after admitting
her “ tightness ” in a simple French ballad.
Miss Jones scores a negative success throughout.
She isn’t a full-sized heroine, and her giddiness
doesn’t look very wicked, nevertheless she is a
cheerful antidote to the Women’s Christian Tem-
perance Union. Three female cousins are bright,
tuneful damsels, as amateurs go and the funny
dungeon-prisoner of 12 long years’ experience con-
trives to raise several harmless, necessary laughs.
Geo. Tutton, although his tenor voice begins to
creak ominously, gets through his part very well,
and, in a mild, faded kind of manner, sundry
other people hold their own. The chorus is
pleasing, like most amateur choruses ; the opera is
pretty smartly trimmed and mounted; the
orchestra, conducted by Harcourt Lee, leaves
nothing in particular to be desired. But why all
this trouble to produce a work which no modern
theatrical manager can see money in ?
