“The Little Minister,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, is understood to be going off the bill before
the Pattie Browne season closes, and “ Sweet
Nancy ”is getting ready to follow. Considering
that there are only two more weeks to provide
for, and the “ Minister ” is calculated to gather
popularity as it goes along, and the date of the
other play’s arrival wasn’t advertised at time of
writing, The Bulletin would not be surprised to
find Babbie, Gavin, and the Auld Kirk elders
fighting the season to a successful finish. Mean-
while, the actor who was specially imported to
act the name part appears to have left Cecil Ward
in possession of the fat.
** * *
At the Opera House, a substantial pro-
gramme stands where it stood last week, but the
Hegelmanns are about to depart, and the re-
nowned Biondi is a revelation of the near future.
The other variety show, at the little hall under
the Bijou, was reinforced on Saturday, and is
moderately thriving. Alexandra horrors have
ceased to be. A drama full of bloodshed and
secret interments under the hearth-stone was tried
as a last resource, but a low-priced public couldn’t
see the awful humor of it, and the shutters went
up once more.

At Melb. Town Hall, last Saturday, melli-
fluous Phil Newbury wound up his concert series
with “You’ll Remember Me,” in tones not likely
to be soon forgotten. This Saturday (25th) the
Alva-Verne concert co. will take the Town Hall
floor, or if they don’t arrive in time for a first ap-
pearance on Saturday, they will certainly be there
next week. Also, the Athenaeum Hall will have a
tenant on Saturday, when F. M. Alexander is to
give, an assortment of recitations, supported by
music and song.
At Poverty Point.
At the inquest, at Adelaide, on Gomez, the circus-hand
whose neck Harroston’s tiger broke with a bite, “ Robert
Love, manager of the circus, stated that ‘The Duke,’
the tiger which killed Gomez, had had six trainers,
and had attacked three of them. At Rangoon, in
’96, a man named Streiff sustained some bites or claw
marks at the back of his neck. The tiger also attacked a
Chinaman, but did not hurt him, although he got the
Chinaman’s head in his mouth. When the Chinaman
called out ‘ What for?’the tiger let him go, and walked
to the other end of the cage.” Truth is funnier than fic-
tion ; this strikes The Bulletin as the quaintest thing it
has heard for years. After all, this paper doesn’t blame
the tiger for letting go when it heard a Chinaman talking
in its mouth.
It was curious, at Broken Hill the other Sunday, to see
a priest standing at the altar-steps thanking members of
the “French Maid” co. for singing in the choir, and
recommending his congregation to go and see the show.
But, after all, why not?

The beauteous Maud Jeffries avowed before leaving
S.A. that Adelaide was the most charming city she had
ever visited. Maud didn’t have the bad luck to run
against some of the defects of the gospel city. She didn’t,
for instance, hit against, the numerous tribe of local
preachers still out of gaol, or become introduced to the
psalm-grinder with a large suburban harem. Further
Maud only spent two Sundays in Adelaide and did not
properly realise the unutterably pious gloom that per-
vades the snufflesome city on the Sawbath. Maud
evidently hasn’t any shares in the numerous building
societies now being liquidated at Yatala Labor prison.
The real Little Minister, Leslie Thompson, is seriously
ill of rheumatism at Menzies’ Hotel. He has had a bad
time, and deserves sympathy. Cecil Ward plays the part
like an Englishman, making the Little Minister a kind of
indiscreet young curate ; but Thompson, an Aberdeen
man, is said to have the correct refined Scotch accent
that the part requires.
Hudson’s lease of Adelaide Bijou Theatre—which is
closed nearly all the year round—expires in July next
year, and it is not unlikely Rickards will take a lease of
this pretty little place, just suited as it is for variety
show's. Hudson has lost a big sum during his lease but
Rickards might make it pay well.
Wilson Barrett’s Adelaide season of nine nights broke
all previous records. The previous average-best was
scored by Bellew and Potter, some years ago, in a longer
season. Barrett plays for a week in Perth, Westralia
prior to appearing in Perth, Scotland.

In their notices of “The Little Minister” play, all the
Melb. journals assumed that J. M. Barrie’s novel is
familiar to everybody. As a matter of fact not more than
one playgoer in fifty has read the story, and not one h
twenty has heard of it. When “ Trilby” came to An.
tralia, the book had been actually read by uerhins’
15 per cent, of the public that supports theatres whilst
fully 60 per cent, had heard the name mentioned ’ A few
months later the other 50 per cent, had heard of “ Trilby”
as a girl, or a cant term for the female foot divine. Hence
the otherwise inexplicable success of Maggie Moore’s
“Trilby” burlesque. It attracted swarms of outer barba-
rians, who wanted to discover what the blazes this ’ere
bloomin' “ Trilby” really was, and what her bloomin’
foot had got to do with the show. The same sort of
notoriety, though in a much milder form, might have
come to “The Little Minister” if it had been titled “The
Little Gipsy."
Tenor Rose, who was imported by the Firm, but some-
how didn’t seem to be an absolutely gorgeous success in
their touring comic operas, is expected to show to great ad-
vantage as a member of “ The American Girl ” co. Here
he will have a part suited to his somewhat finicky style,
and his singing is good in any part.

should make it easier for Rickards to import expensive
“ turns ’’ from London.
Carrie Moore is in danger of getting jaded by overwork.
She is but a youngster—not yet 17, and stili growing—
but she has already played several leading parts in comic
opera—girl or boy—when principals were “ indisposed.”

Some of the pupils of one charming Australian teacher
of singing seem to sing with an accent which mixes the
musical blessing considerably. A certain damsel who
left for Europe, the other day, might have been mis-
taken for a foreigner wrestling with English words. It
was so with Buzz), the Italian singing-master. Buzzi has
left his pronunciation on the tongues of many Mel-
bourne pupils, who start an amorous ballad with the
announcement that they “lofe" (love) somebody or
other.
Dear B.—No fear of anyone murmuring “ Fattie
Browne ” at the Princess. I was agreeably surprised,
after so much talk about the increased proportions of our
favourite confection, to find Pattie not an apparent ounce
heavier than when she filled the t rs so completely
(and the theatre, too) in “ The Country Girl ” six years
ago.—C.M.G.
S. : Eloise Juno has been complimented on her accent
as Nanny in “The. Little Minister” at Melb. Princess,
but that is only a minor feature in a perfect bit of v'ork.
The actress’s dialect is a birthright, and not an effort of
art, but Nanny in her hands is the most cleverly-drawn
character in a play of neat caricatures. The part is com-
paratively a small one, but there is not a faulty shade or
inflection, and to me Nanny is the one reality in a crow'd
of well-worked and rather amusing Scotch eccentrics.
Veteran Gladstone emerges again from Coppin’s kindly
retreat to act Gaffer Pottle in the “Silver King,” he and
Jack Bryan, the original Melb. “Cabby," walking over
from Wiison Barrett’s cast. The once-brilliant Hoskins
was the original Melb. Pottle.
“ Haresfoot ” : Miss Pattie Browne has genuinely
advanced in her London training, instead of deteriorating
as is common. I used to think her rather superficial,
relying a little too much on the fine artillery of her ex-
pressive brown eyes and remarkably good stage face. She
is a complete embodiment of the dramatic Babbie. Yet
she trembles on the verge of being too full in the tooth.
Her figure has a pleasant aplomb, d la embonpointy
Bernhardt.
During first night of “ The Gay Parisienne,” at Broken
Hill, the performers were bombarded with dead pigeons,
which they had helped to shoot in the afternoon. Several
of the people were hard hit by the birds, and the proceed-
ings nearly ended in an attack upon the offenders by the
better-behaved part of the audience.
The jingoistic song, “ Stout, Strong Hearts of Old,"
musicked b.v Robert Horne to words by H. C. Coghlan, is
to he published shortly. It has heen sung with success at
Melb. Opera House these last few weeks.
“ Pro.” : Coppin’s last stage appearances were in Ade-
laide, where his attempt to galvanise excitement by cele-
brating his stage jubilee proved a fiasco, and he gave
it best. So dropped out of mind, a word on his act-
ing may be instructive to some. It was his habit to some-
what forget the injunction, “ Be thou familiar, but by no
means vulgar.” His best things were Paul Pry, Mould in
“Not Such a Fool as He Looks,” Putzi in the “Young
King,” and generally the unctuous line indicated by his
predecessor, Liston, on whom he is said to have moulded
his style.
Oily Deering, after a career of 10 years—to the writer’s
recollection—on the Australian stage, moves up most
decidedly by reason of recent work. For years he has
figured mainly in the broad, vulgar low comedy of the
mechanical melodrama, without fixing himself particu-
larly, but, off-and-on, he has shown himself capable of
rising to an opportunity. His small comical plug-ugly in
“ The Two Little Vagabonds” was miles ahead of the im-
ported effort, and his Snecky Hobart stands out promi-
nently amongst the canny Christians in “The Little
Minister.” Really, we have few opportunities of discover-
ing the true quality of scores of local mummers. Now
and then one gets a look in, and surprises us. Young
Lester, for instance, after gyrating practically unobserved
in Alexandrine horrors, was given a chance with Cart-
wright’s co., at the Bijou, and favored with a neat and
clever bit of gentlemanly comedy, quite an unexpected
pleasure. But they drift out again, and smugs from ’Ome
fill their places, and are patiently borne by a public that
has not yet dared to think for itself.
He Ada Crossley’s want of insight and soulfulness.
Pupils of the late Madame Simonsen relate how she used
to despair of ever making a front or even second-rank
vocalist of Ada. “ She has a grand voice, but she will
not learn.” And, to judge by her p-’esent devotion to the
inevitable “Sunshine and Rain” and like ballads, the
tuneful Australienne recognises her limitations.

They make me think of Miss Leak, the Armless Ladv,

marriage with Mr. Thompson, - an engineer, but most of

N. : Of course the happy married pair get on best on,
as well as off, the boards. Look at the Kendals and
Keans. They can continue Romeo-and-Julieting before
the decorous young person till they are bolsters. Nor
can a singer who lives with her husband utter a false
note.

At the telephone. Reid and Want, with
copy of Bill in hand; Turner at the other end.
Turner: “Hello, are you there? That you,
Reid?” Reid.: “I’m here; the same old
George.” T. : “ Please define your objections to
the present Bill.” R. : “Ail right; wait a
minute, while I consult Want. (Aside to Want):
What is it we do object to ? ” Want: “ Federa-
tion ? ” Reid: “Hush! keep it dark. (To
Turner): Are you there ? Well; First, Braddon’s
blot; second, the* three-fifths-majority clause.”
Turner : “ Yes. Anything further ? ” Reid (to

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The London Bank of Australia issues a balance-
sheet for the complete year, 1897, instead of the half-year
as usual, and the net result is a loss of £8124 on the 12
months’ operations. The first half of last year showed a
loss of £231 and the second a loss of £4396 —total for the
year, £4627, so things are not improving much as yet. The
London Bank had special ill-luck with its reconstruction
arrangements. It applied for a reduction of the heavy 4J
per cent, deposit interest agreed on in ’93, but, being
located in England, a small, foggy isle in the North Sea,
where the ways of reconstruction are hard, it had to get it
by the ponderous and expensive process of an Act of Par-
liament. And a fool of a Legislature solemnly enacted that
the London Bank, instead of paying 4| per cent, interest
on its old fixed deposits, might pay anything not less than
3 per cent, and owe the balance —it may owe it for a long
time if necessary, but said balance is to be paid before any
dividends are declared. Therefore, beyond granting more
time, the new reconstruction left the bank very much
where it was. Last year the earnings, after paying work-
ing expenses and the cost of the extraordinary Act of Par-
liament, were £139,388. Against that were :
| year’s interest at 4J p cent. j_
Balance of j per cent, owing
for second £ year 8,151
£147,512
The earnings of the London Bank for the last
few years have been as follows :
31st December, 1891 (year) Profit ... £104,437
31st „ 1892 ( ~ ) „ ..■ 71,643
31st ~ 1893 (5 months) ~ .. 5,987
The other seven months’ profits were absorbed in the ’93
reconstruction.
30th June, 1894 (6 months) Profit .. 7,026
31st December, 1894 ( ~ ) ~ .. 8,216
30th June, 1895 ( „ ) „ .. 7,082
31st December, 1895 ( ~ ) ~ ... 6,673
30th June, 1896 ( ) Loss .. 231
31st December, 1896 ( „ ) ~ .. 4,396
31st „ 1897 (year) „ .. 8,124 .
The London Bank has now a paid-up capital of
£1,419,345 and its only visible reserves are a profit and
loss balance of £1839, but against this there is the contin-
gent liability of £Bl5l for arrears of deposit interest,
which item is not included in the balance-sheet. There-
fore, affairs can’t be said to look over-prosperous. During
the year £455,409 has been paid off the reconstructed
deposits, which now stand at £3,260,678. Four years ago
(December, 1893) they stood at £ 4,845,156, so a considerable
reduction has been effected. During 1897 the new deposits
have increased by £151,000 and cash (which now stands at
£846,560) has decreased £143,662. The new business, which
has priority over old deposits, keeps growing, and with a
diminution in the expensive 4i per cent, deposits, and no
more reconstruction expenses to pay, the London Bank
should make ends meet in 1898. But evidently the pros-
pect of dividends is very far off, and, barring a very im-
probable new boom, the only chance lies in the slow
extinction of the old deposits. The London, the National
and the Victoria are the only banks remaining which are
still liable for the full 4£ per cent., which was rashly agreed
upon in 1893. The London never was a boom bank ; its
biggest dividend was 9 per cent. ; but it became over-
weighted by a stupendous inrush of deposits in the boom-
time of 1888, which were the cause of all its after troubles.
* * * »
In regard to recent comments pn the Colonial
Mutual Life Assurance Society, the management offers
certain explanations. (1) It states that the provision
made for losses and depreciation in the value of securities
during the last six years has been, instead of £22,429 as
stated by The Bulletin, something over £116,000. This
is made up of £22,429 actually and visibly written off
(which was the sum referred to by this paper) ; £39,718
set aside as an Investment Fluctuation Fund, but not yet
written off ; and £54,746 of interest which has not been
taken into account in the six years. These figures make
the accounts look better, though, so far as the last and
largest item is concerned, it is, of course, not traceable
in the published accounts. When a society has a mort-
gaged property practically in possession, and no interest
is being paid on the mortgage, and it is doubtful
whether, if the interest was regularly charged
against it, the property would sell for enough to
pay both the principal and the interest, the only
straight course is undoubtedly not to include that doubt-
ful interest among the receipts. If £250 becomes due
and isn’t paid on a £5OOO property, it is mere inflation
to put down £250 interest among the receipts and square
the accounts by putting the security on the other side of
the balance-sheet as worth £5250, when it isn’t worth it.
Undoubtedly some institutions do this, and The Bulle-
tin is glad to have the Colonial Mutual’s assurance that it
doesn’t. At the same time, if that society would show in
its balance-sheet the amount of foreclosed property on
hand, as some other institutions do, it would throw a lot
more light on the situation. And yet the informa-
tion these other societies give doesn’t necessarily, in
all cases, throw very much light on anything. An assur-
ance society may show £lOO,OOO worth of foreclosed pro-
perty on hand, and, at the same time, £400,000 more of
its mortgages may be bringing in no interest and may
represent properties practically abandoned to the society
yet not formally foreclosed. In the absence of State
supervision, or any decent law on the subject, assurance
societies’ balance-sheets are a good deal of a mystery.
The Colonial Mutual further desires to explain
that its very low rate of interest (about £4 Is., or a little
more, per cent.) is purely net interest, after leaving all
doubtful items out of account, though some of these may
be recovered later on; and that it could, by taking in doubt-
ful items, make the apparent interest as high as £4 15s. 7d.
per cent. Also that it has an unusually large proportion
of its funds invested in liquid securities, which are safer
than mortgages and bring in less interest; and an un-
usually low proportion in loans on policies which bring in
comparatively high interest. The Bulletin is pleased to
receive these statements, though they only tend to con-
firm it in the opinion that a rigid insurance-law, and a
uniform system of accounts which will make it possible to
institute definite comparisons, is much needed. The
things which this paper wants are—(l) to have the
assumed future rate of interest fixed at a uniform low rate,
certainly not over 3.) per cent. (2) To have it legally
enacted that every balance-sheet shall show separately
the amount of foreclosed property on hand, the amount of
mortgaged property in possession even if not actually
foreclosed, the amounts of rent and interest accrued,
or due but within the days of grace, and the whole
amount of interest and rent in arrear. (3) That there
shall be a State valuation of the assets and a State audit.
These remarks are tacked on here, not as applying to the
Colonial Mutual only but to all societies. They are uni-
versal wants.
• » * *
Adelaide corporation calls for tenders for the
electric-lighting- of the S.A. capital for 21 years, the whole
of the works, plant and business to be handed over to the
corporation at the end of that term free of charge. The
idea of State or municipal ownership of great public ser-
vices steadily gains ground. The pity is that it didn’t
start earlier. Sydney’s great gas-monopoly has been in
good health for 61 years, and there are no definite signs
that municipal ownership is any nearer than it was in the
late ’3o’s.
* * * •
A new joint-stock money-lending and pawnshop
co. is being formed in Sydney out of the business of
“ Henry Tait,” money-lender. Henry Tait, by the way,
is only a business alias, the real owner of that great
discounting industry being one Philip London. The
promoters anticipate that the business will ray large divi-
dends, and, if there is a competent Solom n or Aarons at
the wheel, it almost certainly will. There is no business
which booms more continuously, in wet weather and dry,
than the pawnshop. Also, there is no industry which is
better fitted to be a State monopoly, and to help to fill up
the yawning void in the revenue, than that same pawnshop.
Every State should be its own three-ball Solomon. A State
monopoly of the grog and tobacco trade and the pawn-
shop industry, a tote where the State annexed 4 or 5 per
shent of the profits, a State note-issue, an absentee-tax,
and a State fire-insurance system should .yield enough
among them, if the proceeds were rigidly set apart to feed
a Public Works Fund, and kept out of the reach of im-
pecunious Treasurers, to make these provinces inde-
pendent of the British money-lender.
* * * *
The Equitable Permanent Benefit, Building,
Land and Savings Institution (Sydney) again contrives to
pay 9 per cent, on its paid-up shares and 8 per cent, on
contributing shares, and have a balance of £'454 to the
good as the results of a year’s work. The society with the
long name has now existed for 24 years, and by keeping
its assets unencumbered and laying to its book it is able
to show a prosperous business and over £27,000 of re-
serves even in these times of hard frost.
* * * *
A. J.S. Bank shares (£7 10s. paid-up) have re-
cently sold at 65., which looks like a move in the right
direction after the monotonous quotation of 3s. 6d. which
prevailed so long. The A. J.S. has lately called for ten-
ders for the redemption of another batch of its “ B ”
deposits, and the profit on these transactions should do
something to strengthen its position. “B ” deposits are
now quoted at 14s. 3d., and if they can be bought up even
at 155., an investment of £50,000 leaves the bank £16,000
to the good.

ocalitylal together,
* * * *
In Melbourne the mains of the Metropolitan
and the Brunswick Gas Co’s often lie side by side in the
same street, and the Metrop. has just discovered two
places where the Brunswick Co’s employes had fixed their
pipes to the other Co’s main, and supplied their custom-
ers from it. In one case the Brunswick Co. has collected
the price of the other concern’s gas for three years. Now
the Metropolitan Co. is hunting for other mistakes of the
same kind, and using language that is calculated to lift
the gasometer.

Q.N.B. committee has just furnished a supple-
mentary report re the famous MTlwraith letter of some
months ago. According to the commissioners, aforesaid
letter simply bristled with mis-statements. Also MTI-
- famous assertion that the accounts in which
he was interested with Drury were “ all entered
into at a time when he was neither a Minister of the
Crown nor a member of Parliament ” turns out to be
erroneous also. According to table prepared by the clerk
of the Leg. Assembly, M‘llwraith was in office from June
13, 1888 to Nov. 30, 1888. The account known as No. 2
was opened on Nov. 27, 1888. Also, the Adelaid e-street
Land Account was opened on October 21, 1891, while M‘Jl-
- was Colonial Treasurer from August 12, 1890, to
March 27, 1893. Committee’s report adds, apologetically,
“ But, then, Sir Thomas may be speaking entirely from
memory.”

* * * *
That ancient land-grab association, the Van
Dieman’s Land Co., pays a div. of Bs. per share, equal to
18 per cent. In ’97, in London, the shares were £9 to
£lO ; now, £l5 to £l7. In 1825 the co. purchased
250,000 acres on the northern side of the island for 2s. 6d.
per acre, and, naturally selecting the best, has sat
heavily on the chest of settlement ever since.
* » * *
“ Employe ” . Why doesn’t Melb. Tram Com-
mission managers cause production of all time-tables,
records of reports, fines, &c., and take the evidence of a
man of each class at each shed to check the line-manager’s
evidence ? Were this done, and the cost of head-office,
detective branch, &c., arrived at, it would show that,
from the costliness of these branches, the actual workers
cannot be receiving fair treatment. Confining the in-
quiry to the men mentioned in the Act (as was done by
Parliament), is like ordering a thorough medical examina-
tion, and only submitting one limb.

The two Vic. Customs officers convicted of a
share in the Weenan frauds were guaranteed by a
fidelity insurance co. to the amount of £5OO each.
The £lOOO recoverable on these two policies will barely
pay the costs of the Crown prosecution.
* • » *
On the west coast of Tasmania beef is selling at
9d. per lb., and an agitation for the repeal of the Stock
Tax has been begun at Hobart. The Assembly resolved
by a majority of one the other night to take the duty off
for three months, though Treas. Fysh wailed piteously re
the loss of revenue. Since ’94, despite a £2 per head tax
on cattle and a 2s. ditto on sheep, the former have de-
creased in Tasmania by 20,000 and the latter by 77,000.
Thus Tasmania is two years’ supply short, and asbacon is
scarce and badgers a lot less numerous than they were
before the prospectors got on the job, Tassies are com-
pelled to import meat or live on the codlin moth and
mussel-scale apples so unfederally returned from Sydney
of late.
* * * *
In Queen v. executors of late Jas. Austen, Vic.
Chief Justice Madden gave a common-sense and equitable
decision. Deceased had before death transferred to
trustees by deeds of gift certain large estates, and the
Master in Equity, claiming that there had been an
attempt to defraud the revenue, sought to collect from
the executors probate-duty on value of aforesaid estates.
The C. J. held, in effect, that not the estate but the per-
sons who received the spoil should have been proceeded
against. Certainly the man who eats the cake should pay
for it, not the man who was done out of it.

The Occidental (Cobar) “ deal ” appears to be
“off.” The failure of the battery experiment at the
Cobar gold-mines hard-by must have had a damaging
effect on the Occidental’s prospects in London. A project
is now on foot to form a local co. to acquire the right to
cyanide Occidental and other tailings-heaps.

Cobar Great Western returned 2850 z. for a
month’s run. This is understood to be about the last of
the free-milling ore in sight in the present workings. The
copper formation in the bottom of the shaft is remarkably
rich in gold, but the extent of ore is not known—outside
the Cobar “ duck ” circle, anyhow.

The Exploration co. has had bad luck with

phide works and the Lake George mines have been prac-
ticallj7 run by it, and the “assured dividends” have
not been forthcoming. Report has it that a gold-mine

* » m *
Mt Stewart mine and plant (Mudgee district,
N.S.W.) have been bought at auction by Mr. Garland for
£1750. Mr. Buckland, who wanted some of the plant,
kept the bidding going ; otherwise, in all likelihood, the
whole concern would have been knocked down for £5OO.
The Garland syndicate has hopes that the mine will
develop into a payable copper show. Not long since it
asked the public to assist in the enterprise, but the
public would have none of it on the terms offered.
* * * *
Lake George mines’ prospects look gloomy.
Returns for April and May show that 13,278 tons ore
were treated, and the products (contained in matte)-were
9090 z, gold, 27,8640 z. silver, 152£ tons copper. At £4 4s.
per oz. for gold, 2s. per oz. for silver, and £52 per ton for
copper, the value of these products would be £14,500, or
a trifle over £1 Is. 9d. per ton of ore. The deduc-
tions which are made by the refiners will bring
this down to £l. Neither by stretch of imagina-
tion nor by any arrangements of accounts can the
mining, smelting, refining, and realisation charges
be brought under £1 ss. per ton at the very lowest. So
that on the two months’ run there must have been an
actual loss on working account of nearly £4OOO. Even J.
Bull, with all his abundance of faith and his patient
suffering, could not tolerate this for long. No wonder
that on the publication of the last report a leading bank
insisted on realising on a line of 10,000 shares held as
security, with the result that the shares slumped to 3s.
Several Melb. speculators have been badly hit, but Syd-
ney men escaped without much loss. No information
seems available as to the financial position of the co. It
is not even known outside official circles whether or not
the last issue of 30,000 shares at 30s. per share was taken
up.
* * • •
The Sabbath shocker, in a frantic effort to boost
North Nymagee shares, asserted that “ the co. has now
enough money in hand to keep on working for the next
few months and shareholders need not anticipate another
call for five months.” The secretary officially states that
a call of 3d. per share will be necessary every second
month. The tipster said, “the improved prospects in the
lower levels warrant a greater rise than has yet taken
place.” The mine manager reports : “ No. 1 shaft: So far
have not struck lode previously mentioned, now doubtful
of its existence. No. 2 shaft: Am inclined to think we
have passed through the lode. Will sink No. 1 shaft
another 100 feet and crosscut west for lode which believe
will be at that depth a copper lode.” This looks like
“ improved prospects ” with a vengeance ! What delight-
ful contradictions ! Who is romancing—the tipster, the
secretary or the mine manager ?

The famous Milburn Creek Copper-mine (N.S.
Wales) after being idle for practically 20 years, is to be re-
opened. A six months’ working option on it has been
secured on behalf of a London corporation. On its records
the mine is well worth a further test.

Dear Bulletin. —Worse luck, I have not now
any interests in Chillagoe district. Like many other
Bananalanders, I had to throw up good things in ’9O or '9l,
not because the “ shows” were not good enough, but
because of the impossibility of working them without
railway carriage and capital. Doubtless, swindles will be
worked off through Chillagoe, but the man who puts a
single note into a “ wild-cat ” has only himself to blame,
for, let it not be forgotten, the facts about any mining
property in Queensland can be obtained through the
Geological Survey Dept. If new finds, the lessees, by
paying Govt, a comparatively small lee (a tithe of what
would be paid to a “ fashionable” expert), can obtain the
(private) report of a Govt. Geological Surveyor as to the
facts concerning any deposit of mineral within the four
pegs of a lease.— Tom.
* * » *
A Chillagoe prospecting co. sent a sweet thing
in mining reports to the Melb. papers the other week—
“ Herberton 9th. Pegged 60 acres, good position ; valu-
able lodes. Writing.” Chillagoe just now is a place
where the casual travelliftg “expert” detects valuable
lodes protruding through the ground and just pegs ’em
out as he goes along.
* * * »
The man who sends this from up North has, up
to date, told The Bulletin no lies“ Bedford, the
embryo mining-field (christened by the redoubtable Ran-
dolph with the first important name that happened to
occur to him), looks like being a big thing. It lies 20
miles west of Mungana, the westerly of the two Chillagoe
townships. The Linedale Syndicate has about 200 a. This
country was taken up for copper and silver, but the
holders have quite inadvertently, via the assayer, dropped
upon gold in addition to the other valuables. A dozen
samples, taken from various points along the outcrop, ex-
tending for a mile and a-half, gave an average of loz.
21gr. of gold per ton. One £lO-share (there are 1500 of
them) in this syndicate changed hands at £75 ; later, an-
other at £lOO. Beeston, Maclelland Bros., Lewis, and
other parties have pegged in this locality, and, of course,
are all reported to have good shows.
“The ‘array of boomsters’ has not yet fully
arrived, but they are dribbling along slowly, and
beginning to peg limestone bluffs. Probably prospecting
for caves and looking for divs. from the charge of a
shilling- a-head entrance-money, when Chillagoe shall
have become a health-resort. Pine and large outcrops of
granite, suitable for building purposes or macadamising,
are also drawing some attention, especially when adjoin-
ing or adjacent to proprietary leases.
“ If you know of anyone sending good miners
from good wages to Chillagoe, under the impression that
they will get 14s. a day, kindly kick—hard—on behalf of
the itinerant miner generally. Miners’ wages here are—
surface, Bs. ; below, 9s. ; shift bosses, 10s. ; and no great
demand at present. Prospectors with money and outfit
have plenty room to walk about, and reports are coming
in daily of finds on the Walsh and Tate Rivers. The
latest is an alleged big thing between here and George-
town on the Einasleigh River, near the junction with the
Copperfield River. Said to be very rich and very large,
but as it is situated in the bed of a river, how to work it
will be a knotty problem.
“ The trial-survey of the railway from Mareeba
to Chillagoe is just completed, and the permanent work-
staff is busy cutting sleepers at various points along the
line.
“ Harper group of leases, 280 acres (Chillagoe
Proprietary), contains a copper-bearing lode with a con-
tinuous outcrop of 142 chains and a varying width of
from 3to 12 chains ! Probably the biggest metalliferous
outcrop ever mined in Australia—or anywhere else—ami
should be a giant among 1 mines by itself. Surface pro-
spects, from 4 per cent up to 20 per cent, copper.
“It is unlikely that anything startling will be
procured by outsiders in Ohillagoe District itself. The
Proprietary now holds approximately 2900 acres, having
taken up an additional 900 acres last week with the object
of having what work-sites, waterways and so on, they
may require, far from the madding crowd of pegs. More-
over, they or those assigned to them have been eight or
ten years picking the eyes out of the country, and know
every copper - bearing outcrop the size of a four-quart
billy for miles round. The big Harper Lode runs plump
into a small mountain of solid granite which is in the
hands, let us hope for good, of a big Melbourne syndicate.
“ Calcifer is an appropriate and ingenious name.
Lime, Copper, Iron—Cal., Cu., Fer. —all here allegedly in
limitless quantities. For the sake of euphony, or because
he couldn’t spell, or for some other valid reason, a post-
master in the early days altered the “u” of the copper
symbol to “ i,” and there it has stayed.
♦ • * *
Tbe rumor that Mr. Stewart had been induced
to leave the Broken Hill Proprietary to take charge of the
Chillagoe Proprietary mines did not have the expected
effeetof increasingthe value of shares in thelatter. Accord-
ding to a wire from Townsville geologist Jack denies that
his report on Chillagoe will be damnatory ; on the con-
trary, he predicts a big future for the district. Both
statements are boomster’s “ fake ” —Jack is too cautious
a Govt, official to divulge his opinion before he lias sent
in his report. Anyhow, suppose he said that Chillagoe
has a big future before it ? That does not signify that
Chillagoe extends 200 miles north and south by 100 miles
east and west, as the booming sharks would try to lead
the public to believe. Chillagoe proper may be rich
beyond the dreams of the promoters, but is there any
likelihood of history failing to repeat itself—viz., that
there will be one group of big mines and nothing more,
except scrip-shows ?
As a matter of fact, Mr. Jack—whose opinion
no man can gainsay—does think well of Chillagoe district ;
is, in fact, enthusiastic about it. Seven years ago he
wrote : —“ Considering that the Chillagoe and Koorboora
fields have only been known since the middle of 1888, and
that the work done has been purely exploratory—for
although Messrs. John Moffatt and co. and Messrs.
Delany and part}' have expended an amount roughly esti-
mated at £20.000 (on a great number of mines scattered
over at least 100 square miles of country), there has neces-
sarily, in the absence of machinery and smelting works,
been almost no return—the foregoing notes will show that
the district bids fair, in the near future, to rival or excel
the richest of our mineral fields. Some of the deposits of
silver and copper ore are on a gigantic scale. In the case
of the silver ores, which are, as is generally the case,
really ores of lead containing a proportion of silver, their
value can only be estimated by assaying from day to day
as the ore is raised, or computing by the actual output of
smelting works. In the present undeveloped state of the
mines, all that can be said is that the chances are all in
favor of the mines from which ores can be raised in large
quantities.”
« * » *
Cabled that the Great Western Railway (Tas.)
Gridiron has been under-written in London. Pending
flotation, the first section of construction—to comply with
the Act of Parliament—is being hastened with slowly.
* * * m
Mt. Lyell shares are on the down-grade in value,
investors evidently not approving of the payment of divs.
beyond actual profits. The latest move to bolster up the
co., financially, is an application to Tas. Parliament for
authority to double the freight-charges on the co.’s rail-
way. Does this mean that the co. will charge itself
double the present rates on its own traffic and credit itself
with the difference as extra profits ?
* * * «
The English co. which bought the tailings at
the Bird-in-Hand and other mines on the line of reef at
Woodside (S.A.) has already made a profit of £3OOO by
cyaniding them. It was on the Bird-in-Hand that J. C. F.
Johnson made £30,000.
* * • •
A thousand tons of the West Coast (M.L.) iron-
sand is to be sent to England to be experimented upon.
So far, no one has been able to* make a commercial suc-
cess of its treatment.
* * * •
Westralian goldfields men growl about the de-
nudation of the country close round the towns of all
timber. The unnatural barrenness helps on the dust
fiend, of course, —in fact, makes him immortal. It was
the same at Broken Hill, N.S.W.—Govt, waited till the
country was bare before it gazetted a belt reserve.
* * * *
Fosterville (Vic.) seems to have more horse-
sense than most young struggling mining fields. A deep
prospecting shaft being called for, tbe co’s are not waiting
for one another, but a local development co. is being
formed, practically to subsidise whichever owners will
get on with the work at once. Bendigo men are behind
the scheme.
* * • •
Murchison (Westralian) miners, grumbling to
the managers over the cut in wages forced upon them,
point to the ruinous cost of provisions on the field.
Managers concur, and promise that if prices don’t run
down within the three months for which the reduced
wage-rate is to operate, they will, at the end of that time,
help to introduce a complete system of workmen’s co-
operative stores.
* * * *
Norseman: “The recent rich discovery in
Kirkpatrick’s mine has caused quite a sensation in the
camp. There are 300 tons of rich rock at grass, and over
1000 tons of good ore ready for crushing. A great number
of shares have been bought at Is. 6d., buyers being
strong.” Caesar’s ghost! what will it take now to send
shares of any mine on the Norseman up to £1 ? Buyers
strong at eighteen-pence ! Boomsters are advised to keep
away from the Norseman. The aftermath of the boom.
A really well-got-up description of the As-
sociated Mines, W.A., was recently given in Adelaide by
George Brookman. He said that the work done on the
Australia was more than on any other two mines in the
camp, and that he had the assurance of both manager and
metallurgist that the big plant now being erected would
enable the mines to turn out 24,0000 z. a month. The
first div. of 2s. a share has been declared, and, having
cleared away from the Bottomley slump, shares are ad-
vancing.

Kalgoorlie, Associated and Boulder Mines.

SOCIETY
Mention of the Crotty will recalls that
King O’Malley, arch-priest of the “moon-eyed
goddess of Democracy,” last year introduced a
Testamentary Dispositions Bill into S.A. Parlia-
ment, to force a testator to leave at least a certain
large proportion (a third) of his estate to the
widow. The measure was generally commended,
but not carried to the final stage, because a general
desire was expressed that provision should also
be similarly made for the children. The Repub-
lican King has consequently amended the Bill
on those lines ; it will be brought in again this
session, and almost certainly become law before
the end of the year. Thus, as in many another
instance, S.A. will lead the way in a much-needed
Democratic reform. “The power of the dead-
hand ” is doomed in S. A
* * * *
Mrs. Crotty’s evidence of undue Church
influence upon her deceased Jim is an unknown
quantity at present, and its production in Court
is awaited with much interest. On the other side,
seemingly, there will be an attempt to show that
Crotty was a noble-minded creature, who found
his wife comparatively indifferent to the comforts
of religion, and feared she would “ bloo ” his cash
in frivolous entertainment if he gave her the
chance. But the hard fact remains—Crotty might
have secured it all to the “glory of God ” —after
his wife’s death. What troubled'him was the fear
of her achieving a second marriage on the strength
of a life-interest in the property, for his jealous
heart told him that a second husband was pretty
well bound to run rings round the memory "of
Crotty deceased.

“You see, Reggy, I find it most difficult to obtain a typewriter whose locks are exactly of the same
shade as my wife' s; being of a pacific disposition, doncherknow, I invariably adopt

Editor Bulletin. — One can’t possibly
sympathise with Murray Prior, the usurerwho took
advantage of his girl-servant under his own roof,
but with the mode of his conviction there is room
for disagreement. N.S.W. Full Court says in so
many words, “ We can’t compel this girl to return
to her mother—she is in the eyes of the law suffi-
ciently old to determine her own course of action.”
She does determine her own course of action, she
returns to Prior ; yet for this he is arrested and
convicted. Only for the immaculate character of
N.S.W. justice, the Full Court decision would
look like a trap to catch Prior. The moral of the
whole immoral proceeding is that it is much the
safer course for men of the Murray Prior stamp to
turn the girl into the street at the first approach
of trouble. —G.C.

Ed. Bulletin. — Now that the Murray
Prior case has made it plain that the “ age-of-con-
sent ” gag does not mean that any miscreant can
call in two policemen to sit on the parents while
he keeps on seducing their over-16 years-daughter,
it is urgently necessary that some one should wipe
the floor of the Criminal Court with the women
who hail home-going schoolboys, send them for
liquor, and then prematurely initiate them into
“ Life.”— Hermes.

N.: “ The bishop who transubstantiated
a kiss by first touching his own lips with his hand
and then those of a young lady, must be related
to the johnny who found a melancholy pleasure in
gently stroking a tighted leg with his cane. Also
to the clergyman who slyly cayenned his cut
orange, at Her Majesty’s garden-party, for fear of
too much joy. Does the bishop transubstantiate
with elderly ladies ? A lady-novelist enthuses on
the ‘Ghosts of Disembodied Kisses.’ What is
the value of the ghost of a disembodied transub-
stantiated episcopal kiss ? It is like those ex-
changed between the winged angel boys with no
bodies.”

Mag : “ Magisterial enquiries into cause of
death may serve well enough where there are no
suspicious circumstances, but it must be remem-
bered that the sole object of inquests is to ferret
out any possible foul play. As far as the average
Jaypee’s acumen is concerned, any murder that
wasn’t actually red-handed would stand a good
chance of escaping detection. The wife-murder
at Bendigo, for which Hall was hanged last year,
was returned as an accidental death by the magis-
terial enquiry, and had it not been for the con-
sistent ]legging away of the Bendigo Independ-
ent Hall would probably have remained to this
day a shining light of the football-field.”

A landmark of Sydney is now being
demolished in Bent-street —the big house which,
for over half-a-century, was the home of the Aus-
tralian Club. Its old walls are redolent of much
Toryism, the place having had as members Alex
ander M'Leay (Colonial-Secretary), Deas Thom
son (his successor), the Duinaresqs, and many
others of the Imperial regime. Then the club
was exclusive. Sixty years ago the building was
the Pulteney Hotel, kept by the original Petty.

Every man has his mission, and every

* * * *
“Teacher” : After repeated applications,
the N.S. W. Commissioners are running excursion-
trains for the school-holidays. It would have
been far better had the time-table been arranged
■so that the trains left Sydney for the country after
the arrival of the country trains. Sydney teachers
can go to any part of the country, but those even
a short distance from the metropolis can only
travel on the line they are on— i.e., to Sydney. It
would have meant a day’s loss of time to the Syd-
ney teachers, but it would have given the man
from Dandaloo a chance to see the Snowy River.
* » * *
Quite the most artistic thing in the way of
a tragedy that Australia has produced for many a
day is the latest from W. A. It reads almost as .if
it had been contrived and stage-managed by the
shade of Maupassant. The respectable, wealthy
resident deserts his mistress, and marries piously,
abandoning his family of illegitimates. He visits
the latter to say farewell ere departing for his
honeymoon, and the eldest son, a boy of 16, blows
out his brains before his father’s eyes ; whereupon
the father rushes from the scene, distracted with
horror, and is not seen again. That is the posi-
tion at the time of writing, and the story is com-
plete, so all “ later information ” must come in
the nature of an anti-climax, and will be studi-
ously ignored by the present scribe.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin. — Re female partiality for
mixed railway carriage; in Maoriland we always
claimed the right to eject women from smokers.
One frosty morning a party of auctioneers came
into the office at our station, and requested that
two females be removed from the smoker. Writer
hung on his most engaging smile, went to the
compartment, and gently asked the fair to shift,
as the smoker was the only one on the train. The
nearest houri promptly took out a packet of
cigarettes, lit one, and passed the case on to her
companion, who replied: “No, thanks, dear;
I’ll have a cigar.” And she did! There was
nothing for it but to pensively retire. —H.
* * * *
“Kurrajong”: Bulletin, 4/6/’9B, was
quite right: “Lovely Woman is Peculiar,” but
when her treatment of the ladies’ compartment is
under discussion, the fact should be considered
that it is usually the babies’ compartment also,
hence its careful disuse by an averagely-well-
dressed woman. Let The Bulletin travel with a
tribe of children constantly eating fruit or sweets,
who claw wildly at the nearest garment when the
carriage oscillates, and it will never again wonder
at the avoidance of the ladies’ compartment by
the feminine traveller.
* * * *
The latest Southern demi-mondaine ruse
to attract patronage and cash takes the form of
letters addressed to prominent married citizens,
requesting their attendance at specified tunes
“ for most urgent reasons ” at private residences,
which eventually turn out to be of the assignation
order. Several prominent Melb. city men have
lately had to pay smartly for their curiosity (or
“gallantry”) inasmuch as the lady always has a
blackmailer handy who wants something to
keep his discovery dark.
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent :
Supervision of slaughter-houses must not stop with the
condemnation of all diseased meat. Went through Sydney
abattoirs last night —the whole system is disgusting.
The butchers have a playful habit of throwing offal at
each other, and the visitor, unaccompanied by anyone in
authority, is certain of a bad time. If he remonstrates,
so much the worse. To see refuse thrown about pro-
miscuously in the midst of meat intended for food is a
practical argument for vegetarianism. In the ideal
slaughter-house, all the butchers will have to undergo
daily medical examination —at present, a consumptive
may handle meat and spread disease for months.

“N.” : A Melb. paper, giving names, or a
name, which I need not do, has a timely anecdote
about a Salvation Army officer. A poor man ap-
plied to him for relief. Says the officer, “lean do
nothing, unless you get sent to gaol. Then you
will be received by our Prison Gate Brigade.”
Lovely. I was walking one night past the brigade
establishment in Melbourne, with its windows
brilliantly lighted, and an honest pauper said to
me, “ Ah, I can’t get in there : I’ve never been in
gaol.”

* * * *
A few days ago, in Melbourne, a house
was burgled of the entire contents of the first
floor, and, although 13—fatal number ! —people
were sleeping in the apartments overhead, the
property—chairs, piano, sofa, carpet, &c.—was
carted away successfully, and the inmates knew
nothing of the fact until morning. The detectives
admit that they see no earthly reason for the steal,
as the burglars would find it difficult to dispose of
the goods ; and, as nobody seems to have, seen a
stray cart loaded with furniture wandering around
in the early hours, the affair is wrapped in mys-
tery. Most likely the perpetrators are a deserving
newly-married couple, and very likely the husband
is a young man, one of many who get married on
a pound a week or less and are therefore unable to
plank down the necessary coin to start housekeep-
ing. Anyhow, it is hoped the Bench will let them
off with a caution. What this young country
wants is population, and population can’t come
respectably without marriage, and it is almost
essential that marriage should be accompanied by
something for the bride to sit down upon.

was in Sydney.”

the garden. It was an “ ideal ” garden a year
ago, and to-day its glory is departed. A good
many landlords have the same grievance, and the
sympathy of the outside public is with them, for
the outside public loves to look over the gate or
the fence, and admire the beauties of nature.
Also the o.p. delights to express its feelings by
sneaking roses and things from the benefactor’s
garden, back and front. But nobody sympathises
with the tenant who gets his rent raised in con-
sideration of his having created an attractive
garden out of a howling wilderness. Contracts
between landlord and tenant are for the protection
of proputty. For the yearly tenant who improves
the proputty there is not even a law of common
justice.

One Brisbane barrister is doing so well
that he recklessly refused a 300 guinea brief and
2ogs. per day refresher to go to Charters Towers
in a big mining ease. Attorney-General Byrnes
was “on” in same case, but it has since been
settled amicably, to the great discontent of the
Ch. Towers public, who anticipated spicy reve-
lations.
* * * *
The liquor forwarded by a certain Vic-
torian goods-train had so frequently been broached
in transit that two detectives were planted in a
covered truck with a consignment of grog. But
the guard and engine-men had got the tip ; they
ran the truck on a siding between two walls, which
prevented the truck doors (opening outwards)
from giving egress, and marked the vehicle in
chalk, in the regulation style : “Journals (axles)
heated.” As it was the last train through, the
stationmaster went to bed, and the unfortunate
d’s. were not discovered until noon next day,
when the truck was shunted on to the goods-
train.

tenance-order. And she gave him a bad charac-

Dear Bulletin.—A late number of a cer-
tain medical journal gives an analysis of one of
the most popular and largely advertsied nostrums
for removing irregularities of all kinds “ from
whatever cause arising.” Here it is :
Carbonate of potash (pearlash) 40 grs.
Myrrh (with a large percentage of dirt
and sandy matter) 80 ~
Water flavored and sweetened with nut-
meg and spirit of choloform £ a pint.
A box of pills said to be “efficacious in even the
most obstinate cases,” contained 27 four-grain
coated pills identical in composition with the
common compound rhubarb pills of the British
Pharmacopcea. These quack remedies, which
cost at the outside 4d., were sold at 15s. The
bitterness of the fraud is that the above nostrums
are utterly useless for the purposes advertised.
Poor ignoramuses driven by desperation to part
with their scant earnings in the hope of making
the irregular regular, the crooked straight, are
left at the end, worse off than ever, full up in
heart and body and absolutely empty in jjoeket.
Where is the remedy?—Medico.

* * * *
AN.S.W. resident army man who draws
his rank and title from Imperial sources is so
sorely aggrieved at the prevalence of captains,
colonels and majors of Australian manufacture
that he intends “inquiring in thepropah quartah”
whethah or not such expressions as, for instance,
“Captain Farnell ” and “Colonel Neild ” are
legally correct. The touchy martinet holds that
they should be “Colonial Captain Farnell ’’ and
“Colonial Colonel Neild”; or, if the provinces
like, doncherknow, each could prefix its own name
to the title of its warriors. In the early days the
word “Colonial” was prefixed to all titles con-
ferred for services rendered or to be rendered in
Australia—Colonial Chaplain, Colonial Secretary,
Colonial Quartermaster, and so on.
* * * *
An old man who went missing at South
Melbourne was found dead in the place he was
employed to take care of. The cat, which was
locked in with the body, had lived on the latter
for a week, and there was no face left when the
searchers entered.
* * * •
S.A. Govt, has re-engaged Drs. Napier
and Smith, the imported Adelaide Hospital
doctors, for another five years. Despite slander-
ous statements by opposition doctors and news-
papers, Smith and Napier have been a great suc-
cess, and the percentage of deaths at Adelaide
Hospital has never been so low as it is at present.
Owing to the influence of the striking doctors,
however, medical students still migrate to Mel-
bourne to finish their studies.
* * * *
If N.S. W. Govt, take no action to save the
patients in Govt.-subsidised hospitals from raids
of lady visitors of evangelising dispositions, the
various hospital-committees should pass stringent
by-laws in that direction. Almost all ladies who
visit hospitals think it their duty to console the
patients, and women’s general idea of consolation
is to convert the dying man or woman to their
own particular brand of salvation. Some try to
do it with tracts, some with a raspy wail, and
some with a wheezy whisper ; but all who try it
any way should be run-out either by a policeman
or the hospital-warder.

A country damsel recently journeyed over
300 miles to Sydney to be wed, but the bridegroom
failed to come to scratch. Whereupon the lady and
her mother hied them gaily off 400 miles in
another direction, to the missing one’s place of
residence, and there discovered him upon a jam-
boree which had wiped from his remembrance all
about his little appointment in the metropolis.
He was forthwith ironed hand and foot, conveyed
to Sydney,' and duly executed.

T.D. : In my suburb, the public-school
piano needed tuning last week. To raise the
requisite five bob, a concert was held among the
children themselves, each of whom had to pay one
penny admission. Yet the only people who use
the instrument are the teachers.
* * at *
Ed. Bulletin.—Some time ago you said
that tobacco isn’t a luxury, but a necessity.
But physiology teaches that smoking is injurious
to the nerves, and physiology (as you rightly
pointed out in a later issue) embraces the know-
ledge of all time and all nations. It seems to me,
therefore, that in saying that tobacco is a neces-
sity, and not a luxury, you are as inconsistent and
illogical as you often show others to be. —Dago.
[Physiology does not say that moderate tobacco-
use is injurious—while, as for immoderation, all
excess is hurtful. Also, the distinction of mean-
ing between “ practical necessity ” and “necessity
absolute ” ought to be visible to the naked eye of
even the Inconveniently Literal Person.—Ed. B.]
* m * «
John Forrest's marvellous perspicacity
paralysed the Esperance people during his recent
visit. “Itis 28 years since I first visited Espec.-
ance, and I can already see the great changes that
have improved the township." Esperance con-
sisted, when Forrest was there before, of a sheep-
station, a police hut, and a blacks’ camp. John
buried a faithful black tracker on the shores of the
Bay, and it may have been to the fence and slab
which now mark the spot that he referred as the
“great changes.” Esperance, now boasting more
than one two-storeyed building, a theatre, and a
post-office, has a surjace morality sadly lacking
in other Westralian towns.

The Jubilee
left one visible re-
sult behind it in
England which it
is hoped will give
the “silken
bonds ” a friendly
lift. The troops
which gathered in
from all parts of the empire
were located in and around
London. They were a mixed
lot—white Australians and
Canadians, Malays, Maoris,
Chinese, Sikhs, Bengalis,
and many other samples—
and they all went out and
mashed Mary Hann, the suscep-
tible servant-girl, in their spare time.
Mary Hann fell down and worshipped
their beautiful uniforms at first sight, and
the result is that there are twice as many
unorthodox infants as usual this year in the
localities where these troops hung out, and that
the infants are black, brown, yellow, and other
surprising colors. Also, one dark-brown infant
has been born into the British peerage, and a
great scandal has ensued.

In N.S.W., recently, three brothers
Quinn, two of whom are twins, married three
sisters Hoffman, two of whom also are twins.
Another brother Quinn had previously espoused
another sister Hoffman; so that, in electric
phrase, “ the connection is complete ! ”

Dear Bulletin. —Your “ Woman’s Let-
ter ” says of Sydney street-sold violets that they
smell only of water —“a clean smell, certainly.”
I think, however, if Gouli-Gouli had ever wit-
nessed a vendor watering his posies, she wouldn’t
have found the odour quite so blameless. The
fellow I noted had a bottle of water, from which
he would take a swig, and then, after the manner
of a Chinese laundryman, spray the mouthful
over the bunches of violets in his basket.—D.

The recent Melb. garotting-case suggests
an idea. Let all confidential-clerks who have to
carry large sums of money from banks to places
of business be supplied with spiked or saw-edged
steel collars, to fit neatly under their shirt-collars.
One hug of these would discourage the boldest
thug in the profession.
ROSES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Roses, roses, roses,
Golden and white and red,
Lusciously twining and trailing
Over the balcony railing ;
Pretty, scented roses ;
When Sweetheart and I were wed
She gathered a golden, smiling bloom,
And Memory loves its sweet perfume.
Roses, roses, roses,
Honest, sunny, and true
As her pure life at the dawning
Ere the fatal words gave warning;
Faithful, clinging roses —
In the time of the deepest rue
We only remember rosebuds red,
Like drops of blood on her snowy bed.
Roses, roses, roses,
Golden and red and white—
Little one merry and winning,
Knows not she is pinning
In her bosom’s roses
The cold of that chilly night,
Where Sweetheart silent and still has lain
Like a white rose wilted under the rain.
Tas.
BARKIS.
THE BEREAVED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He moves about the world again ;
His grief he never speaks ;
The wrinkles deep of pinching pain
Make channels in his cheeks,
And in his eyes a settled woe
Lies like a ghost that will not go.
No mourning wears he to proclaim
His agony, but white
His hair has grown since pale Death came,
And robbed him in the night.
On Sunday morns his hot tears lave
The flowers he scatters on her grave.
A few there are who sympathise ;
They press his wasted hand ;
And deep and true emotions rise,
For they can understand.
He’s lost the best of wives? Nay, Nay !
The best of cooks was called away.

All Drapers Everywhere.
Influenza and Colds
Nothing so suddenly obstructs the perspiration
as sudden transitions from heat to cold. Heat
rarities the blood, quickens the circulation and in-
creases the perspiration, but when these are sud-
denly checked the consequences must be bad. The
most common cause of disease is obstructed pers-
piration, or what commonly goes by the name of
catching cold. Coughs, colds, sore throats, &c.,
if attended to in time are easily subdued, but if
allowed to run their own course generally prove
the forerunner of more dangerous diseases. Nine-
tenths of the consumptives date their affliction
from a neglected cold, and the diseases that are
caused by wet feet, damp clothes, or exposure are
more numerous than are generally supposed. One
of the most efficacious medicines for the preven-
tion of diseases of the throat and lungs is BILE
BEANS. Bile Beans are an undoubted Specific
for Biliousness, Sick and Nervous Headache, In-
digestion, Constipation, Piles, Female Weakness,
Pale-faced Girls, Irregularities, Bad Breath, Pim-
ples, Blotches, Dizziness, all Liver and Kidney
Troubles, Dyspepsia, Influenza, Colds, Heart
Palpitation, Pain in Back and Side, Fulness after
Eating, Lack of Physical Tone, Heartburn, and
that Tired Feeling.*

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 21, 1898.
Mr Dear Myee—
Sydney Liedertafel made a big, bold innovation
by inviting women to that most sacred masculine
revel—a smoke-concert. So the thin end of the
wedge slips in and in, and women now flaunt
fearlessly where grandmas feared to tread, even on
tip-toe, in elastic-side cloth boots. Up to the
present time the Liedertafel’s lady guests are held
in check by the gallery railings, and the smoke
and the beer and the men have the body of the
hall to themselves. But, as a member of the ad van-
ning sex, I hope the time is not far distant when
little tables, and mild cigarettes of brown paper
and tea leaves, will be set apart behind screens for
those of the petticoated who think they like
them.
The concert was a joyous one, as viewed from
the gallery, through the mist of many pipes.
There seemed U be more women than men pre-
sent, but that may have been an optical illusion
arising out of several big hats growing gardens
and aviaries in one. A few evening gowns hid
themselves shamefacedly under opera-cloaks, and
the majority compromised the matter by removing
their head-gear. Up in the gallery corridors, tea
and.coffee were available, and a dearth of cups
pointed to the fact that the committee had not ex-
pected such a generous attendance. Coming out
of the hall I heard one man remark : 44 It’s not the
same thing when you know her eye’s on you.”
But as he was one of the little men who marry the
big, fat women, his remark does not count.
Specially in the lenient light of the comment made
by a matron to a spinster, 44 1 thought they all got
under the table.”
As for the musical part of the evening, the pro-
gramme was pleasing and included two local com-
positions, Alfred Hill’s orchestral 44 Slumber
Music,” and N. J. Gehde’s song, “The Voyage.”
A very German gentleman, Herr Staedtgen, with
curly hair and legs, and rich, rollicking baritone,
went 44 The Voyage ” twice over at the wish of
the audience, but the encores were not the un-
limited wickednesses of concerts where women are
allowed to clap. From a women’s point of view,
the most peculiar feature of the evening was the
unfaltering determination of the smokers not to
encore every item on the programme.
So far the Pleyel Chamber-music concerts have
not gripped the public to any large extent.
Chamber music doesn’t go straight to the heart
like 44 Home, Sweet Home’’with variations. A
little education is needed to he p it heartward, and
Saturday’s programme was admirable beyond all
hope of the public. An exquisite rendering of
Schubert’s Quintet was something to hold on to
till the next Concert.. Staell, Vollmar. Alfred
Hill, Podenzana and F. M. Carter were re-
sponsible for its interpretation. Mrs. Lance, who
always sings as if she means it, was the only
vocalist. In the middle of the concert the big
piano misbehaved itself and nearly fell off the
platform, when it was being moved aside for the
string quintet. And of course everyone stood up
and looked towards the door. Panic is still a
fashionable sensation.
Recipe for making waltzuptodate. Take a lean
piece of motif, the leaner the better, and hang it
up where the wind can blow on it. When
thoroughly dry and hard, wash and wipe it well,
and thus remove all signs of blood, melody, or
other disease common to the poor old motifs that
have been on hand for several hundred years.
Then score heavily with a butcher-knife, chop up
into, small pieces, put half your pieces up on the
octave shelf above your head, and leave them
there till needed ; lay the remainder away at the
bottom of the board. Run about a little here,
and thump the board with the handle of the
carving-knife. Then gather your motif-mince
together, scatter some real stale crumbs of ocher
people’s compositions over the top of the dish,
decorate with a picture of a lady in a Grecian cos-
tume, and serve hot some steaming summer night
when the ballroom is 100 - in the shade and
“there’s no shade.” That recipe carefully carried
out, I defy any waltz to be different from any
other of the waltzes made by similar recipes.
“ My Marjorie,” by Bertha Lord (Paling and
Co.), is a bold opponent of my recipe, and scatters
a tender melody abroad with reckless hand. I
consider this the most pleasing and tuneful com-
position of the season, and its rhythmic impulse
is bound to provoke dancing feet into the third
and fourth extra. The same publishers send a
variation on the usual provincial Muse, in the
shape of 44 Narcissus,’’ sub-titled a water-scene, by
Ethelbert Nevin. The scene is kind enough to
move on after two pages of a gentle andante
movement that leaves behind it nothing particular
to mark its brief, two-leafed existence. From
Glen and Co. comes “The Bismarck March,” by
Wilhelm A. Haas. The picture of a big frown
adorns the cover, and a notice to the effect that
Bismarck has allowed this march to be dedicated
to him, points to the fact that the frown must be
Bismarck. But, after all, with the open pedal
down, the march is a good, loud one that rather
puts the frown out of countenance, as it were.
The Dante Societa had its third conversazione
on Friday—a mild, artless festivity, variegated
with coffee and buns, and some mandolin per-
formances, and some pure Italian. But for the
ordinary English-speaking folk, who are limited
t o the word “macaroni,” there was too much of the
language of the ice-cream-shop in the entertain-
ment, The lecture on Dante, spoken in Italian,
by Signor Turolo, was appreciated by five or six
black-bearded gentlemen, with Southern eyes,
but failed to raise a thrill in other people’s breasts.
At intervals, the stage was taken by bevies of
white-robed maidens, who made as much music
out of mandolins as mandolins will allow. Elsie
Hall, in a white blouse, sat in the front row beside
her mother and listened carefully with no indica-
tion of ennui, but when you consider that Dante
was a man who had Heaven and Hell open to
him, the mild mediocrity of the Dante Society’s
musical offerings seem scarcely fitting.
Astronomer Russell entertained the N.S.W.
members of the British Astronomical Association
last week, in three relays, by showing them Saturn
through his large telescope. So well entertained
were they on one occasion that they took no ac-
count of the flight of time, and had to walk home
after midnight to their respective suburbs. As
none of them had seen the new comet, they all
gave glowing accounts of old ones, but, when Mr.
Russell had got back so far as the brilliant appari-
tion of ’43, the other yarners gave it best, and
wished their host 44 Good-night.”
Durward Lely has been drawing better houses
every night, but the entertainment would be
brisker and more effective if the little intermediate
sketches were cut out altogether. To give the
history of a song before you sing it goes against
the ethics of artistic singing.
Julius Knight, to my mind, now fits into the
part of Napoleon like a snail in an empty snake-
skin. He does not act much, does not elocute in-
spiringly, and more or less spurns facial expression.
To be branded Nap, and wear a white face, and a
black cloak, and a hunched back, is good enough ;
but to put these things on the stage without any
redbloodman inside to make the wheels go round
is not history, and is not art. But no one can be
surprised if so many consecutive performances of
the “Sign of the Cross ’’ have temporarily dulled
the gentle and accomplished Julius. Miss Ferrar
is still the Josephine of the glittering sequined
frock, fast darkening with wear and stage-dust;
and Marie Louise still clings to the reddest of red
wigs—lso-carrot, I should judge it. Public en-
thusiasm over the “Cross” co. has worn off a
little, and everyone is looking forward to Herr
Pauli and the coming opera-season.
The next few weeks are to be brisk with dances.
Woollahra Benevolent Society Ball comes off on
Tuesday, 28th June, at Paddington Town Hall.
The Artists’ Ball follows in the same hall on
Thursday, and the Prince Alfred Yacht Club’s
Ball on Friday, July Ist. The Art Society is
responsible for this Artists’ ball, with Mr Lister
Lister as president.
Rumored that Jack Want had fifteen tickets

The sale of so many of the pictures, at the Aus-
tralian Artists’ Exhibition in London recalls that,
prior to the selection of the works in Australia, it
was suggested to one of the gentlemen in charge
of the exhibition-scheme that the Australian
public should be given an opportunity to view the
pictures before they were packed for London.
44 Oh, never mind,” said he, 44 we’ll exhibit them
when they come back.” And he didn’t mean it—
also, he wasn’t an Irishman. I see that, after all,
Mr. Piguenit heads the sale-list by trading off five
pictures for <£433. The careful old-fashioned
style of work, which some people deride as 44 tea-
tray ” art, still has the strongest clientele.
A popular belief that the Gibho is the very
bushiest of all the bushy baekbloek towns will be
dispelled by the following cutting from a Dark
River paper. I give it to you to let you see that
even in the very remotest bush they know how to
do the thing correctly :
On Friday night Mrs. Jones gave a splendid ball at her
residence on the Gibbo. The hostess, with admirable
taste and lavish generosity, entertained her guests
sumptuously. When the visitors, whicli comprised a large
party of ladies and gentlemen from Benambra and some
gentleman from the Dart, arrived, they found the tables
overflowing with everydeliciousedible that themost epicu-
rean palate could desire, and after the long ride, the guests,
with appetites keen from the fresh mountain air, did full
justice to the ample board. A large hall, tapestried with
many emblems of the rich variety of Gibbo evergreens,
was prepared for the ball-room, and dancing, occasionally
varied with songs, was kept up with animation. But all
too soon the early dawn heralded the approach of day,
and then the dancers reluctantly retired from the
brilliant scene of enjoyment with many a wish that night
had kept her pinions furled a little longer. After
breakfast the party, with many felicitous expressions,
bade adieu to their hospitable hostess, and returned along
the winding and picturesque valley of the Gibbo, gazing
again with admiration upon the wild and magnificent
scenery that is here so marked by contrast that monotony
is conspicuously absent. That the party enjoyed the
trip even beyond expectation was manifest from the glow-
ing cheeks and vivacious eyes of the ladies, and satisfied
demeanor of the gentlemen as they arrived at
Benambra.
And society in the back-blocks is becoming as
particular about the reports of its hats and frocks
as leaders of city fashions. Up in the Riverina a
lady is trying to work the dismissal of the editor
of the Calf Creek Times because his journal
made it appear that the daughter of the lady in
question was married in a picnic hat. It ought to
have been picture, don’t you see !
A strange feature about our Victoria Salon is
that women who are notoriously skilful hostesses
in their own homes seem to sink into a shy or
stupid awkwardness when they receive their guests
in the club precincts. Conversation freezes as you
pass the drawing-room doorways and for some
oc< ult reason those who do talk, whisper. The
most elastic imagination could not call one of the
Salon meetings a merry party. As for the Intel-
lectual element, which was promised so lavish a
development in the early days of the club, its time
has not yet come. Various grades of society hold
drawing-rooms weekly, and now and then a lonely
artist does a lightning sketch, or an unfledged
young man sings a song, but it is never
the same lonely artist or the same young man a
second time—a sad fact and full of meaning.
If I were asked to make a suggestion, I
have one ready that’s not in the least
brilliant, but is easy to carry out. Move the salon
chairs about and mix them up in the middle of
the room. Of all certain ways to ensure a
Charles Lamb party “all silent and all damned”
commend me to a room where the guests sit all
along the walls, and an empty space gapes in the
middle of the room to echo what they might say if
they talked.
A certain debt-collecting co., recently, sent a
very big sassiety dame a demand for £2B due for
corsets, and she replied by calling in person to say
that she wasn’t in a position to pay, but would
leave some old pictures, arriving by next boat
from ’Ome, with the firm as security. The offer
was accepted, and a week later the footman or
someone in mufti, carted the pictures straight from
the steamer to the co’s office, where they will re-
main pending developments in the corsets account.
Just as fashions have settled down for the
winter, spring goods—the peach-trees are already
blossoming in Sydney—begin to unfold themselves
in shop-windows, and all one’s conceit in one’s
winter clothes is smartly overthrown. Another
of the cruel little habits of our shopkeepers is
their present system of selling you a frock. In a
French drapery-house you sit down at a counter,
and remain there until you have bought every
item that goes to make a dress. But in our shops
we buy the material on the first floor, the bones
down the basement, trimming in the top storey,
lining in the warehouse across the lane at the
back, and the buttons —well, we generally have to
go to another shop to match the buttons. Conse-
quence is that jaded shoppers, whose limbs won’t
drag themselves up three flights for the right shade
of button-hole silk and the true tone of trimming,
take whatever comes first, or leave the matter of
etceteras to the shopkeeper, and presently find
themselves burdened with a gown that plays 10
different variations to one note of color.

’kerchiefs? Anyone wishing to ingratiate herself

earth here by expatriated poor relations. How-

tea break no bones, the big people really suffer

Passing out, at Bathui’st (N.S.W.), 86-

know that he is one of a family of 15 children.
Fortunately, none of the others are in Australia.

climate disagrees with him.

classes as “ Monomanias”—Mormonism, Nihilism,

tarianism, Spiritualism and Salvationism.

dentally observed that on several occasions he had
been introduced to ladies, and the following day

“The pince-nez killed Gladstone.” An-
other version is that the inflammation on which
cancer supervened was caused by the apple
(thrown by a woman) which at a political meeting
struck him on the bridge of the nose.
Judge Fenton, who died the other day at
Auckland, M.L., used to say that there were three
things necessary to a gentleman—“ brains, cour-
age, and a clean shirt. And the most important
of these,” he would add, “ is the shirt.”
At a recent Sydney Uuiversity examina-
tion. Question: “Tell all you know of Sir
Thomas More.” Answer: “He lived in the reign
of Henry, wrote Irish ballads, and was afterwards
killed at Corunna.” What’s in a Christian name?

in the same breath, with “ Are there—er—any
small cases we might dispose of before—ah—
lunch?”

before Vic. Law Commission—“ the Supreme
Court Bench required lawyers, not advocates”—

Goodna (Q.) people glory in the possession
of one John Byrne, a patriarch who was born in
Wicklow (Ireland) in stormy ’9B. As a baby he
was concealed in a haystack to save his life. He
now, at the century’s end, cuts up his own tobacco
and talks Irish politics to his local admirers.

Cardinal Gibbons, who used to be spoken
of as a future Pope, has, though the only Cardinal
m America, erected neither palace nor house to
conserve his dignity. He owns but one room in
the ordinary clergy house. He wears no distin-
guishing dress when out of doors, and takes his
share of the sick-calls and other ordinary curates
duties.
When Lieut. Clarke, R.N. —who has just
inherited a lot of his father’s Australian property
—entered the Britannia training-ship, the old
man went on board the flagship at Sydney and
paralysed the captain with an inquiry as to
whether he thought ,£5OO a year would be allow-
ance enough for a cadet. The shabbily-paid man
of epauletes thought at first he had to deal with an
escaped lunatic.
Major-General French, at the prize distri-
bution at King’s School, Parramatta, on Thurs-
day, held up General Sir Russell Baker, of the
Imperial army, but one time a King’s School boy,
as a shining example to the pupils. But Baker, as
a boy, didn’t shine to any great extent. He was
chiefly remarkable for his close chumship with
Justice Simpson, of N.S. W. Divorce Court, then
also a King’s School boy.
Federal City Shoppee, who lately suffered
his second defeat for Vic. Leg. Assembly by six
votes, is one of the youngest old men in Victoria.
He lately issued a challenge to race any man over
50 on the bike fora mile (he is fifi himself) —but no
acceptors. Though he has worked all his life for
Ballarat, he is not too popular there, and at the
’B9 general elections only polled 413 votes against
1886 recorded by the successful Smith.
There are few records of deliberate ex-
plosive outrage in Australia. The most memor-
able is the Haunted Stream murder, in which
Cohen lost his life, although accidental explosions
have been frequent enough. The most tragic of
these occurred in Nov., 1865, when 2g tons of
gunpowder on a dray belonging to a carrier named
Gamble exploded on Bathurst Road, near Penrith,
N.S.W. Gamble and his six horses were killed
and his dray smashed to atoms.
Gov. Brassey does not give away more
than he can help giving, but Melbourne Public
Library contains one handsomely-bound volume
presented by him. Guess what it is ! A cata-
logue of the works of art and curiosities con-
tained in his London mansion in Park-lane. Poor
Us ! We are the hungry child flattening our nose
against the pie-shop window. And Brassey-he
is the bloated Pieman who has stuck in his plate-
glass window a list of his pies.
A legal disturbance between old Johnny
Wallace, M.L.C., the Victorian gold-bug, and his
son Peter promises to be interesting. Son Peter,
representing several beneficiaries under the will of
the late Tommy Monahan, alleges that his father,
as executor of the will, “ treats money belonging
to his children as his own, being apparently of
opinion that he can do what he likes with it.”
Nobody can accuse Johnny of a disposition to get
rid of any property that he handles, anyhow.

Though a raging Imperialist, he has one great
and shining virtue. He does much public speak-
ing—yet never once has he used his ’Erald to
boost himself up and keep himself before the
public. No weary reporters have to chase round
and report him in full, and you and I have known
that done by editors who were also public speakers.
And then those leaders in the ’Erald, page 2 ! !
Without them, what ivould the Vic. public do
for its humor ?
Paddy Hannan, the fifth anniversary of
whose “ find ” was made a public holiday at Kal-
goorlie on 17th inst.., received from Westralian
Govt, a township allotment of land as a reward
for his prospecting success. Paddy Flannngan,
his mate at the discovery of Kalgoorlie, received
nothing. Flannagan lost a leg and is now hard up
in Melbourne, and Hannan, failing to get his
claim on Govt, acknowledged, transferred to his
mate one half of his own allotment. The trouble
of both is to get a purchaser for the land.
