The writer once called
on old Dr. Lang at a very
late hour. Wanted some
information for the Empire.
Naturally felt intrusive, and
communicated the senti-
ment to his knock. It was
answered by the Doctor,
who half-opened the door
and thrust his arm out
with a shilling in the hand.
Explanation : “ I thocht
you were one of the puir
bodies who have no beds.
They call pretty often," he
said, “ an’ I just gie them a
shullin’. Ye knocked as if ye
had na bed, and wanted my shullin’ ’’—and the old
man chuckledconsulnedly. WhichstrikesTHEßul-
letin as a very charming story and one that sug-
gests the ideal minister of religion. A companion
picture, in fact, to that story of the charitable old
Melbourne priest whose executors after his death
searched for his estate. They found it—twopence
—in the pocket of the rusty old waistcoat hang-
ing behind the door of the room in which he died.
The curse of Deeming proved a bad thine:
for the original investors in relies of that amorous
identity. A Bulletin man bought a heavy black
ebony walking-stick at a “ second-hand ” shop
the other day. It was a well-carved stick, with
an elephant’s-head handle, so the purchaser
thought it cheap at five shillings. When the
money was duly paid and pocketed, the dealer
gleefully confessed that the formidable weapon
was the original stick, the stick which figured as
an exhibit at dear Albert’s trial, and was probably
concerned in his last murder. Its early relic value
had departed—all along of the curse.

“Melb.”: Demise —in Sydney—Father
Ayhvard. Hugo’s Quasimodo, in Notre Dame
from the view-point of his devotion to the sacred
edifice, symbolises many clergy of the Roman
Church. Thus Monsignor Fitzpatrick, at St.
Patrick’s, Melb., and Father Aylward at his new
gigantic W. Melb. Church, which is towering up
on its hill, like St. Ignatius’, at Richmond. The
authorities of St. Francis’, where Father Aylward
long officiated, have viewed his W. Melb. success
with a little jealousy. They fancy it is connected
with an occult scheme to dispose of St. Francis’,
even as Bishop Coe is credited with the opinion
that the sale of St. James’ would be a good thing.
An unthinking reporter who once ad-
dressed a certain Australian cleric as “ Bishop
” received a horrified rebuke for not saying
“ My Lord,” nor did the scribe’s plea of ignorance
prevent that bishop from writing a letter of com-
plaint to the office. His regular habit of complain-
ing of reporters, in fact, makes “ Turkeylegs,” as
he is locally called, the best-hated man among the
local journalists. Another little weakness of his
lordship is to get up and walk out of any public
gathering if his presence is not specially referred
to. No matter where he happens to be seated, if
the first speaker doesn’t begin, “Mr. Chairman,
my lord bishop, and gentlemen,” away goes
Turkeylegs with his episcopal nose in the air.
Rupert Bunny, the Melb. artist whose
“Descent from the Cross” is said by many
English critics to be the finest work in this year’s
R.A. exhibition, was originally intended to follow
the legal footsteps of his father, Judge Bunny,
and was sent, at the instance of his German
mother, to Heidelberg University. Here, how-
ever, Bunny’s artistic nature revelled more in
Rhine scenery than in legal lore, so that when he
returned to Australia he knew rather less about
law than when he started, besides having a face
badly scarred with duelling. At Brighton (Vic.)
baths Bunny used to bury himself in the sand and
so remain for hours contemplating the changing
colors of the sea, the results of which practice
appear in his mermaid picture in the Melb.
National Gallery.

W. J. Sowden, editorof Adelaide Register,
really will, it is said, contest Gumeracha at the
coming general election. Sowden is among the
first three platform speakers among Australian
working newspaper - men ; and, unlike most
studious folk, excels in repartee. For instance,
at a “smoker” given by local pressmen to the
visitors during the Federal Convention sittings in
Adelaide, Sowden (in the chair), introductorily
talking of journalists and their status, remarked
that the public rarely heard of the journalist un-
less some vagabond calling himself a journalist
got into trouble—“as, say, ‘John Jones, journa-
list ’ was fined ss. for drunkenness.” Thereupon,
John Jones, the Argus’ barrister-reporter,
solemnly protested against the introduction of
personalities. Sowden: “My friend will pardon
me. I desired to illustrate the impudence of
these vagabonds. Not content with stamping
themselves as members of this honorable pro-
fession, they presume so far even as to take the
name of so distinguish' d, so eminent a member of
the profession as Mr. John Jones! ”
It is an extraordinary fact that a big
proportion of the most eminent and most
sought-after Australian surgeons are the.
hardest-up. The higher the fees, the
surgeon commands the more “ stony ”
he appears to he—as a rule. But
this disna’ apply to Sydney, where
so many of the lights of the profession
are Scottish, ye ken.
A former well-known Vic. P.M.
was, when a lad, clerk to the late
coroner Youl, and lived in the build-
ing adjoining the old Morgue. Among
the visitors to the place was a noted

when the young fellow stepped into

found the penal person there with an

Frank A. Nankivell, our late black-and-
white visitor, has arrived safely back in New
Fork, and says he admires it more than ever,
despite the war-gong sounding and the air being
full of flags and Yankee Doodle. On the advice
of Puck he has quit drawing for the “Yellow”
dailies, thus giving him more time to take up
magazine work. At present he is besieged with
orders, and says that the trip has benefited him
much, as his style has considerably strengthened.
He was greatly amused to find on arrival that an
Australian artist there had borrowed one of his
figures from The Bulletin panto, sketch and
used it as an original poster for burlesque show.
In Queen Yictoria’s young days her rela-
tive, the Duke of Brunswick, was accused of a
certain offence by Barnard Gregory, of the
Satirist. Gregory, after doing two years’ im-
prisonment for his impious libel, lived with a
widow named Parlby, whose daughter married a
Major-General Perry. An impecunious Countess,
in consideration of a ten-guinea fee, presented
Mrs. Perry at a drawing-room, and when our
Sovereign Lady came to hear that her hand had
been kissed by the illicit step-daughter (so to
speak) of a man who had once said something
nasty about a royal duke, there was an awful dis-
turbance at court. Inquiry into the family char-
acter of unknown presentees, which was a very
casual matter up to that time, has been more or
less inquisitorial ever since--except as regards
lydy visitors from the Kan linies. Tlieir character
is reckoned of no consequence.
Premier Dick Seddon lias broken out as a
humorist. Lecturing at Nelson (M.L.) on his
trip to England—the only lecture he lias ever
delivered—he said :—“ When I reached America
I went to look up two mates who had worked in
the same workshop with me at St. Helens, my
home in Lancashire. They had emigrated to
America about the time I left for Australia, and I
had not seen them for 36 years. I found them in
the neighborhood of Salt Lake City, both of them
Mormon bishops. That’s point No. 1. When I got
to England I told the joke to the old foreman we
had worked under as lads. ‘ Richard,’ he said, ‘if
you’d ’a gone with them two you’d ’a become the
Prophet himself.’ That’s point No. 2. Perhaps
I’ve missed my destiny after all.” It is now known
how Dick managed to “ blue ’ some of the £l7OO
which the Opposition made such a boggle about
paying. “ Imagine the form before you,” he said
to his audience, “clad in white-silk stockings,
slippers, white velvet breeches, and a coat so
covered with gold lace that an old West Coast
friend of mine calculated it would pan-out an
ounce to the dish.”
Apropos of General (now Marshal) Blanco.
A South Melbourne publican was, once upon a
time, proprietor of a travelling circus which visi-
ted Havana. Permission to exhibit was sought
and promptly refused by the Governor (Blanco),
but His Ex. was known to be susceptible to female
charms, and the circus-man had just marrii d an
attractive ci-dcvante widow. She interviewed
Blanco—in her recently discarded weeds—and ob-
tained his sanction at one fell swoop. A proviso
that the tickets should be printed at the Govt.
Printing Office, and distinct ones used for each
night, looked harmless enough. The mean-
ing of this stipulation was understood, when
at the end of a short season the pro-
prietor of the show found himself taxed with
a considerable royalty on ewry ticket issued.
Now, after many years of smouldering ire, an irate
Boniface, declares he will forward five shillings to
the U.S. Treasury as soon as he hears of the death
or capture of Blanco aforesaid.
The Melb. bomb-shell outrage round-
aboutly recalls Australia’s only avowed Anarchist
of note—clever, eccentric J. A. Andrews. He
was originally a clerk in Vic. Education Depart-
ment, from which he was precipitated for using
forcible language to Col. Templeton, then Chair-
man of the Public Service Board. Then he
dabbled in journalism, wharf and Yarra Bank
oratory, etc., drifted to Sydney—where he was
awarded five months for sedition after a villain-
ously-prejudiced prosecution and trial and
finally went to America, where he ran an Anar-
chist journal, the Firebrand, in Philadelphia.
This was, a month or two back, suppressed, but,
on appeal, was allowed to continue. Andrews, a
philosophical, and not an explosive, Anarchist,
was one of the mildest of men. His chief eccen-
tricities were exhibited in his attire. He always
tucked his trousers into his boots, buttoned liis
coat crosswise, and wore a hat through which his
hair wandered at the top and waved in the breeze
like leaves of grass.

Aboriginalities.
J.S.A. : “How's this for ‘long arm ? ’ In
the palmy days of Gulgong it was all team car-
riage from Wallerawang. Teamsters used to
throw off a few bags of feed at wayside places to
lighten the up-load and have them available on
the down trip. Smith, a teamster, ‘ went on a
tear, ’ lost a purse containing £53, and concluded
that he’d been robbed. Six weeks afterwards he
was on the trip down, and, meeting another
teamster loaded with chaff, asked for the loan of
a bag, and was asked to take one. He threw one
off the load, opened it, and shook the chaff out
into his feeder ; entangled in the old stitches he
found his purse and cash intact. That bag had
been re-filled several times from the time the purse
was lost! ”

“ Morodian ” : He stopped me as I toiled
up the hill that ran past his selection and asked
me to have a drink. In the chat which followed
the burning subject of the hour cropped up.
“This yer Federation,” he said; “most of us
chaps down here don’t understand it. I asked a
bookmaker the other day what jhe thought of it,
and ho wanted to know who ho was by and what
were his performances. But what I want to know
is, will Federation give us more police protection
—perhaps you might know, mister.” I said I
didn't, but it was possible. “ Well, then, if it is,
I’ll vote for the blanky Bill. You see, it’s this
way : I makes a few bob \\ hen the season’s bad by
snaring ’possums, and, being an old man, find a
difficulty in getting up before daylight; and if I
don't get up, the blanky boys from the township
comes round and steals my ’possums out of the
snares. What I says is, this yer country wants
more police protection.”

Some years ago a tobacconist-barber at St.
Arnaud (Vic.) bought a wooden Red Indian,
second-hand, at Ballarat, and set him up as a
symbol before his door. The Indian was dread-
fully wooden, and too three-cornered to have de-
ceived the blindest, one would have thought; but
men from “out around” got very drunk in St.
Arnaud in those prosperous days, and the drunk
arrived on the morningof the third day, and re-
ceived a gross and gratuitous insult at the hands
of the red-gum Sioux. Immediately the tocsin of
war rang out, and the Indian and the man from
Paddybangle clashed on the footpath, and went
down together, and fought each other into the
gutter and up the road. When the combatants
were overtaken and separated, the Indian had not
a rag left on him, and the bushman had a broken
wrist and about 150 wounds and bruises. A few
hours later the battered cripple was snug in hospi-
tal. “My oath ! ” he said enthusiastically, “ that
cove would ’a-done me up if he could ’a-lastfed
another round.”

Dear Bulletin.— After a brief experience
of towns where the Chow reigns, t have come to
the conclusion that ho is tolerated only where the
white is weak ami wan. For instance, in the
early days of a goldfield, when the people are the
strong men of the earth, the Chow is afraid to
show his face—he comes only when the glory of a
field is departed. On Broken Mill he could not
live, nor on other big mining rushes —in Towns-
ville he is the cook and bottle-washer, because
Townsville’s whites are strong ; at Bowen, where
the Caucasian is a perfunctory sort of person, ho
wears spurs and rides the noble horse ignobly, and
has the front to drink in a public bar and ogle the
barmaid. You find him strongest where the white
is weakest. Alleged white men at Cairns work
for the local Chows, but only because the Chow is
stronger—a Chow on the Hodgkinson goldfield
owns a pub. So the white man works for the
Cairns Chow because he can find no white em-
ployer, and the Cairns Labor-party talks through
its hat and thinks with its elbows.— Australian.

Dear Buluhtiw.—
Failing a more scien-
tific criticism by Brig-
ham Brown, let me say
an interim word to Mr.
J. A. Philp. There is
no prejudice against
individual Chinamen,
amongst reasonable
persons. The China-
man is not law - abid-
ing ; he is essentially
law-evading. It is as
much a breach of the
law to live in filth as
to distribute smallpox.
The fact that “the
standard of their liv-
ings high and their
consumption of Euro-
pean food and cloth-
ing much greater than
generally supposed”
doesn’t make them any
better than the Ro-
man who lived on
nightingales’ tongues
—and he wasn’t much,
except as a gastrono-
mist. Some years ago
I told you (in answer
to Rudyarcl Kipling’s
Eurasian eulogy) that,
however good the half-
caste, the offspring
thereof is a failure,
morally and physically.
It seems to be a com-
promise on the part of
Nature, who doesn’t
like to give to a reason-
ing hybrid the sterility
of the mule. That
half-caste Chinese girls
are generally well edu-
cated, and the boys
often so, I admit. I
hold as my friend a
half-caste, who was
best pupil, best ath-
lete, best pigeon-shot,
and most gentlemanly
of ' the alumni of a
large State-school in
Riverina. His sisters
also are eminently
good scholars and
ladies. But, as my
friends, on account, of
their unenviable posi-
tion, will not marry, the
proof of their desirableness as nation-builders is wanting.
I am, however, at fault as to the comparative values of
mule and jennet. We have here the real mnldfortn half-
caste, With the Lahore Light Ilorse (disbanded for
inefficiency and immorality) we had the jennet type— i.e.,
the mixture of superior (as equine) male blood with the
inferior (asinine) female—a decided failure as a beast of
burden. Whatever I may acknowledge Mr. P. to have
proved regarding the morality of his half-castes and their
exceptionally large percentage of attainments (physical
and mental) in a very meagre centage, I do not think he
would advise the propagation of a tribe originating in
what he himself admits to be a “ debased class.”

“S.K.” on barramundi fishing : -
Dear B.—Let me describe my first experience thereof in
Eurombah Lagoon. On our way to the water we filled
our pockets with stones and captured all the grasshoppers
and such we could ; also a large green tree-frog. Arriving
at the bank we stripped naked, flung the stones far out-,
making as much splash as possible, and then dived in
ourselves and vigorously floundered around. Then
climbed out and threw in our lines : he with the frog
inserting the hook in its back, so that it might kick
about and invite closer inspection. That’s where your
recent contributor was wrong. If you want barramundi
to bite, you have to take trouble to attract their atten-
tion. This turn we got a splendid haul. Largest we
oaught weighed 71b., and was about 2ft. 6in. long, but 20
or 30 pounders are sometimes landed. The scales are
enormous (half-'crown size on quite a small fish) with a
pink spot in the middle. The fish is a surface feeder, and
the lines therefore need floats, to keep the bait about two
feet under the surface. The blacks, by the way, main-
tain that there are bunylps in Eurombah Lagoon, and the
owner of the adjacent station solemnly assured us that
he hat| once act J ually seen some huge creature splashing
a } 3oU t in the deepest part; others told of something
which emitted strange wails and moans; and the fact
that the thing is said to have a semi-human face may
point to the existence of some kind of large fresh-water
seal.-—K. t t *

Dear Buli,kti.v. —Re the great bush - thirst.
“L.M.’s” shriek is only a slight variation of the
weary old Cold-Tea lie that all evils spring from
the cursed drink. “L.M.” apparently judges all
bushmen by the fewcadgms who occasionally ask
his boss for a pint of “ dust,” but my long hush ex-
perience has taught me that it isn’t, and never
has been, the man who “k nocks-down ” his cheque
(rather than buy a bag of fionr, and eat- it out- in
sodden dampers in a bend, neat' the station-home-
stead, until his late boss sees tit to employ him
again) that cuts down wages. It’s the “ steady,”
servile, saving, and “ smoodging ” individual, who
does the trick every time. “Creeve Jtoe ” pointed
out the real culprit in his recent verses, “The
Worker’s Foe.” I’ve taken part in three big.hush
strikes, and I have always found that the bootless
men, in rags, “ who knocked-down their cheques,”
were the most loyal in those days of trouble ; while
those with selections, and horses, and good rig-
outs, and money in their pockets, rushed in and
black-legged on any price and conditions! The
sober cocky'-s sons have, so far, won every hush
strike for the / astoraluts. Squatters w ill tell you so
themselves. Uocky's sons need money for fencing,
&c., &c., and, no matter who suffers later, they’ll
go in and make it. If that is “ thrift,” it is thrift
at other people's expense. Bushmen call it “ scab-
bing t” Likewise, it isn’t the boozer who has
brought down burr cutting, lamb-marking, &c., to
12s. 6d. a week or less. It.’s the cooky shearers who
arrive at the sheds in mobs long before commencing-'
time, and worry the life out of the squatter for a
job. The latter seizes the opportunity, and lets
them contracts at ridiculously low prices ; or gives
them wood-cutting, lamb-marking,, &0., at 10s. t o
12s. 6d. per week. I’ve even known shearers to
work for bare tucker till the shed began. The
half-starved, ragged swagrnan, who “ knocks down
his cheques,” indignantly scouts such offers, though
unluckily he’s gradually settling iloun to the level
of these " thrift "-men, and, from sheer necessily,
accepting same terms. When aforesaid abject
wage-slaves are reproached for cutting down wages
their answer is—“ Oh, my horses were poor, and
I’ve come out tc make as much money as I
can.” Again, you seldom find a “ lushing-
ton” in a dummy’s berth. “Must have sober, steady
men,” says the squatter; “men I can depend upon.”
Further, when hard-up or in need of any kind of assist-
ance, I’ve always found the “ lushington ” the readiest to
help. Altogether, I’ve found the man who “ melts ” his
oheques a manlier, straighter, more independent and
better working fellow than the thrifty, sneaking, sober
ohap. The former drinks, works, and dies hard; the
latter cringes, creeps, and crawls hard—not forgetting
that there is no rule without the usual exception, (i xB.

A recent N.Q. charity-ball was decked
with a few casual episodes which would be con-
sidered remarkable in the homes of an effete civi-
lisation. The elderly local publican ordered his
young and pretty wife not to attend ; he couldn b
dance himself because of his age and weight, and
Mick, the butcher, could, and Mrs. Public-House
always danced a good deal with Mick, which led
to disagreements. As the lady replied curtly that
she was going, her lord and master pot over the
difficulty by sawing her ball-dress in 16 pieces
with the meat-knife. Then he went out for a
drive. When he returned he found that Mrs.
Public-House had broken 18 bottles of good liquor
on the pavement, and had gone to the ball in a
borrowed frock, and Mr. Infuriated Public-Houso
promptly followed at a gallop, leaving a streak
of language behind him. A fast and furious waltz
was in progress as he burst into the hall, and by
ill-luck the fairy form of his wife was just
whizzing by in the arms of Mick, the butcher.
There was a smile of triumph on the face of that
hated Lothario, and his scalp-lock was carefully
anointed with fresh beef-fat. Mr. Public-House
made a smart grab at his wife’s frock as she went
by, and tore it right off, and, as the pace was
rapid, the lady went half-way round the room in
a state of wild alarm and a short petticoat before
Mick could pull up. Then he dropped her, and
went for Mr. Public-House, who heaved a chair at
him as he came, and swept his legs from under
him, and brought his oiled head with sorrow to
the floor. The rest of the proceedings filled the
entire premises, but the M.C. managed to get the
combatants apart before all the furniture was
destroyed, and effected some kind of a reconcilia-
tion. Mick agreed to state that he had no dis-
honorable intentions, and Mr. Public-House ac-
cepted the explanation. Then Mrs. Public-House
was taken home in disgrace, and ordered to con-
fine herself to the house till further notice.

“ Wiry Jimmy ” lost his dog three months
ago, and hasn’t been “ the same man since.” A
week or so ago he was forwarded by the authori-
ties at Albury to the Old Men’s Home, Parra-
matta. Jimmy is 93 years of age. He was in
the China War, but arrived in Sydney in 1837,
and has been practically on the “wallaby” ever
since. He was over the site of Albury before
that township came into existence, and looked in
, on most of the goldfields as they were discovered.
His reason for retiring from the fight now is that
“ he lost his dog three months ago, and isn’t the
same man since.” Poor Jimmy !

“Alia” : What may happen to a man in
Queensland. Was living in a bark humpy on Can-
ning Downs, and one night, leaning wearily against
the stone chimney, watching the billy boil, was
bitten by a scorpion that crawled out of the inter-
stices. Lay down on the bunk later, and was
awakened from a doze by a centipede dropping
from the roof on to my face. Got up, went out-
side, flung myself on the grass, and was just drop-
ping-off, when a clammy sensation on my right
hand aroused me ; and I felt pretty clammy all
over when I saw it was a black snake of an in-
quiring turn of mind. With a cruel effort of will,
I managed to remain still while that snake
finished investigating the interior of my coat-
sleeve. Then I leapt up with a yell and fled.

The Drink Problem.
At midnight, in a large Melbourne hotel, August
J. Metzler, manager of the N.S.W. Lager Bier
Brewery, was aroused by a vehement knocking at
his bedroom door. He sprang up, fearing the
alarm meant fire. Opening his door, he beheld a
well-known Sydneyite in pyjamas. “ What’s the
matter?” sharply asked Metzler. “Is the house
on fire, or what?” “Excuse me !” interrupted
the other, “I must humbly apologise for this
rude intrusion, but the fact is—my child, my wife
and myself want to beg of you a couple of bottles
of your Bock Bier ; we cannot obtain any in Mel-
bourne, and we are in dire need of it.” Metzler
understood what was the matter, and at once gave
the visitor the Bock Bier. It is not generally
known how invaluable Bock Bier is in nourishing
the lacteal glands. “ Stop the child crying,” says
Metzler—“ give the mother Bock Bier! ” Metzler
is an oracle on breweries, biers, and babies. His
father, a big brewer in Germany, had all his
brothers slain in the first battle of the Fr anco-
German war, and consequently was left with a
brewery and 60 children all to himself. So the
Metzlers are fortified with facts in child-rearing,
and know what is good for a family. Metzler him-
self has studied brewery building and bier brewing
in various climates the world over, and his versatile
knowledge is both compressed and expressed in the
splendid Sydney brewery which he now manages.
Says Metzler : “ A rational man teaches his child
rational drinking ; drinking is like swimming—
the child who doesn’t know how is most liable to
get drowned. I give my children Bock Bier regu-
larly three times a day ; small child, small glass ;
big child, big glass And since every Australian
doctor who has experience of the Bier recommends
it as a strength-producer and tonic, Australian
parents cannot do better than follow my example.
Bock Bier, once its merits are impartially recog-
nised, once its minimum of alcohol and maxi-
mum of nutriment become popular facts, will
take its place as the national winter drink
of Australasia. The lagers brewed in Ger-
many are the common beverage of the Ger-
mans ; arid as we must drink something, it
is wise to drink what is most beneficial.”
Of course, the whole drink question is, what to
drink; to drink that which means maximum
benefit and minimum harm. The summer lager,
which the N.S.W. brewery manufactured last
season, was a marvellous success. Business men
found that they could take this light, stimulating
lager without such after-effects as drowsiness,
dullness, heaviness, regret, or, in other words —
they relish the Bier the more, because of
its minimum of alcohol and price, and its
maximum of nutritive and digestive qualities.
Bock Bier, the perfept winter lager for Australia,
has been analysed by Dr. Helms ; and the man
who drinks the Bier merely because he likes it
will be better pleased to know that the analyst’s
figures tell him to drink Bock Bier regularly. A
number of Sydney doctors have been so impressed
with Bock Bier that they have bought up shares
in the company. And many a doctor has flatter-
ingly testimonialised the Bock. Says Metzler:
“When fiery alcoholic liquors are the rage, de-
lirium-tremens hospitals cannot be built quickly
enough to accommodate patients. But once
rational drinking—the lightly alcoholic lager—is
popular, medical students will have to drive miles
to see a case of horrors.”*

* * * *
An agricultural item. In many districts,
no sooner is wheat sown than flocks of birds —
sparrows, starlings, and others —swoop down and
carry away much of it; even harrowing-in does
not save too much of it. In S. A., the up-to-date
farmer is beginning to mix a pint and a-half of
coal-tar in each bag of wheat. The grain thus
treated is not touched by birds, and its value as
seed is not impaired.
* * * *
Simpson Newland, president of the South
Australian geographers, and himself pretty deeply
versed in aboriginal lore, devoted much of his
this year’s presidential address to advocating
State action “to preserve the traditions and
memorials of the remnants of such tribes as exist
throughout the settled districts.” Mr. Newland
mentions that already the erstwhile numerous
Adelaide tribe is totally extinct, and that the Port
Lincoln tribe, once exceedingly numerous and
fierce, is now reduced to a dozen aged and child-
less men and women.

J.W.S. : As Premier Reid, must be badly
in want of a hollow log of much capacity, the fol-
lowing information may be of use to him. In
Huon district (Tas.) was recently found a hollow
log 150 ft. in circumference, the inside diameter
being about 36ft. If this doesn’t happen to be
the log Lyne has already in his eye, I dare say
Reid might get it cheap.

Dear Bulletin.— Talk about the pastoral
patriarchs of old ! Met a bullockyon the Hughen-
den track lately, loaded with wool. He was
driving the dray-team; the “missus” an old
white-tilted waggonette, with an odd foal or two
running alongside; two urchins of about four and
six drove a mixed herd of goats, pet-sheep, and
calf, while a boy of seven on an old grey horse
was rounding up the lot. —A.U.S.
* * * *
The latest industry of the Melb. casual
hawker is the selling of live kangaroo-rats as pets.
The line has proved such a promising one that the
pup-vendors of the Exchange pavement have
changed their stock to marsupials.
* * * *
Higgins, M.P., says that an ingenuous
squatter told him he always expected his em-
ployes to vote his ticket. “If they don’t,” said
Tallowfat, “ by the Lord, they get the sack. They
get my money, and ought to vote for my man. ”
This is cheerful, as showing that the Dodo is not
extinct, and the Megatherium still abides on earth.
These good old paunchy ideas are hard to kill.
* * * *
Borroloola, the N. Territory township,
only gets an occasional mail. The residents are
just beginning to smell the Yankee-Spanish war,
and with them Gladstone is still alive. What
Borroloola thinks about its chance of being the
Federal capital is also not yet known. It might
have happened that the fate of the Australian
Commonwealth depended upon Borroloola’s
Federal vote, which won’t be known for some
time yet—and it would have been nice, under the
circumstances, if it turned out that no one had
voted at all !

* * * *
The closing-down of smelting at Broken
Hill has caused the extinction of the slag-beetle,
an insect about an inch long, which lived in the
vicinity of the slag-dump, and pestered sight-
seers and workers by biting them on the back of
the neck. Locally believed that the beetle bred
in the red-hot slag, boring into the iron pots to
lay its eggs !

N.S.W. Attorney-General Want as yet
shows no sign of redeeming his famous promise
that, when the Federal Bill was defeated, he
would sit down, and, within an hour, produce a
Bill which would be acceptable to all the pro-
vinces. When a man can do such a thing as that,
his conduct in not doing it is simply disgraceful.
However, as soon as King Cabbagetree has
finished defending The Bulletin in its libel-case
with Cowlishaw, the promised Bill will doubtless
be forthcoming.
The Governor’s speech at opening of Wes-
tralian Parliament promises an Amending Tariff
Bill, the object of which will be to give preference
to goods manufactured in the mother country over
those of other countries. Which recalls an ordin-
ance with which the tariff system of N.S.W. prac-
tically began. In 1802 Gov. King issued an Order
levying a duty of 5 per cent, upon all goods enter-
ing Sydney, save those of British manufacture.
King admitted at the time that the arrangement
was whimsical, but he said he wanted money to
support his orphanage, then in perilous need, and
that he would levy on goods of British manufac-
ture also, but that he feared the Home Govt,
would squash the Order and remove him from his
post.
Dear old George Republican Dibbs (who
before the Convention was elected, denounced all
Federationists as disloyal insurrectionary people
who wanted to blast the Crown —the same Crown
which gave Dibbs his title and his big cigar) on
the Federal poll:
But the actual result of the election is that Mr. Barton
as leader has been thoroughly discredited. It must be
borne in mind that I and others to the number of 100,000
voted for Mr. Barton as a delegate to the Convention,
and in that he failed by over 25,000 to score that many
votes for the Bill, it amounts to a huge vote of censure
upon him.
How does the above logic apply to Reid ? Reid
got between 80,000 and 90,000 votes for the Con-
vention. Then the side he voted for got some-
thing like 75,000 yotes, and the one he barracked
for about 69,000, and he was “ discredited ” both
ways.
Jack Want, who solemnly attested that
his retirement from the Reid Ministry was a
genuine affair, and not a mere temporary pre-
tence, now goes back to his old billet as N.S.W.
Attorney-General. Also, he and Reid have
greased each other at a banquet, each of them
being absolutely lost in admiration of the other’s
great services to the cause of Federalism. It is
only a year or two since Want denounced any
kind of Federation as a “ hydra-headed
monster basking in is constitutional beastli-
ness in this bright and sunny land,” and he
has never once said or done anything since then
to give the cause of union the slightest assistance.
And when the man who denounces the other pro-
vinces as “ wolves ” and “ robbers ” goes back to
Reid’s Government the chances of these other
provinces taking that Government seriously are
hardly worth mentioning. Jack Want is taken
back as a guarantee of bad faith.

THE PHANTOM RACER
A CYCLIWG LEGENG
For the Bulletin
The field flew out before the bell
And faster grew the gait—
They felt their muscles shoot and swell
And, straining tight each nerve as well,
Around the final bank they fell
To battle up the straight.
Outside them, Harvey Raynor shot
A clear two lengths ahead ;
The pace throughout had been so hob
That every pedal’s turn he got
A further lead—for all the lot
Seemed ridden-out and dead.
Save one who, as that jaded ruck
Fell one by one away,
Clenched tightly teeth and fists and stuck
In Raynor’s wake with dogged pluck
While thousands cheered and wished him luck—
The youngster Jack Moray.
To Raynor’s eyes, the livid line
Grew golden as he sped ;
He saw the stakes and bets combine,
Two big brown eyes with rapture shine,
He thought, “ A thousand pounds are mine;
At last will we be wed.”
Then rose a roar like rolling sea,
A wheel beside him spun ;
And by his elbow he could see
The youngster coming strong and free ;
And Harvey Raynor learned that he
Too soon had made his run.
His spirits fell—his chance was small
Against the gold and black ;
He knew the lad could him o’erhaul
And then he thought “ The girl and all
Are mine if Jack Moray should fall ”
And swung across the track !
Behind his pedal on the right
A sudden flash of gold
Across the darkness flickered bright,
And dimmed with fear the winner’s sight,
While following his falt’ring flight
The moan of “ Murder ! ” rolled.
None saw the way the smash occurred,
Nor how the youngster died ;
The quaking winner's eyes were blurred,
He saw but flashing gold and heard
Still ringing in his ears the word
That Jack Mora}' had cried.
None read the truth upon his face,
Though all the tracks began
To see the change that came apace,
And soon his altered moods to trace ;
For Raynor, when he won that race,
Became another man.
He mooned around the livelong day
The track whereon he trained ;
He watched the riders sprint and stay
As though they rode far, far away ;
While home in tears, neglected, lay
The maiden he had gained.
But soon another change he knew:
He lived upon the wheel;
By day and night he fairly flew,
Grossing the country through and through ;
No sun that seared, no breeze that blew,
Did Raynor seem to feel.
He rode not as the men who pride
Themselves upon their pace ;
But, like a fugitive who tried
To leave a law that he defied,
H j rode the way that demons ride,’
And wore a demon’s face.
Around the racing tracks his name
Was heard on every hand ;
Victor o’er all his class, he came,
And, treating champions just the same,
Jumped in a single week to fame,
That travelled round the land.
His fellow racers marvelled o’er
The form that Raynor showed ;
When mighty stakes were up before,
He’d lost, and fairly lost, they swore ;
Yet now none else had chance to score
When Harvey Raynor rode.
He saw his sweetheart fade and waste,
Yet never sign made he ;
Nor paused in his career to taste
The good of riches in his haste ;
For by his side a Rider jiaoed
That none but he could see.
Through midday sun or darkness dim
The Pacer rode beside ;
He dared not waive the order grim
When, racked in each reluctant limb,
The Phantom Pacer beckoned him
To mount his wheel and ride.
So passed a year; then came a day
That Raynor rode alone;
With heart elate and spirits gay,
He laughed and loved the old, old way,
And when that night in sleep he lay
Ail Phantom fears had flown.
But flashed a light before his face
And Raynor sat awake,
To hear a Voice cry out of space :
“ Rise, Harvey Raynor, take your place ;
To-night we ride our final race—
Your life the winner’s stake.”
They pedalled through the shadows dark
Far from the city’s host,
Ere spoke the fleshless Phantom stark,
Pointing far out a glitt’ring spark :
“ That light shall be the winning mark
That flashes down the coast! ”
He bent his head and dashed away,
His chest he opened wide ;
He felt again the lust of fray,
He tired not at his muscles’ play,
He felt the way he felt the day
The youngster fell and died.
He sprinted where the highway dipped
With surface like a track ;
And while the corks he tightly gripped,
He guessed the Phantom he had slipped
When rang a hollow laugh that dripped
Like ice upon his back.
The light was yet a mile ahead,
But scattered o’er the waves
In bright white dots its rays were shed
Like rows of tombstones o’er the dead
That oft the greedy depths had fed,
As if to mark their graves.
He seemed to hear the final bell,
As round tlie road that twined
Along the cliff, he battled well,
And felt his sinews shake and swell.
“ They’re ringing Harvey Raynor’s knell! ”
The Phantom laughed behind.
The fearsome outline gaunt and gray
Beside his elbow showed,
And Raynor, sickened with dismay,
Remembered suddenly the way
That he’d defeated Jack Moray,
And swnng across the road.
At dawn, the waking sunbeams’ glow
O’er sea and cliff was shed,
A bruised and battered oorpse to show,
For full five hundred feet below,
Laved by the gentle wavelets’ flow,
Lay Haivey Raynor—dead !

A careless, dull scribe, misreading hit
notes taken at the Vic. Law Commission, wrote
“ magsman ” for “amalgam,” and the sub. of his
journal barely spotted the mistake in time.
Judging by the evidence given before the Commis-
sion, however, the difference is very often incon-
siderable.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post i/4, 2/6,
3/8. Senior, chemist, George-street, Sydney.*
Papuan Mac, chief fakir in the much-
ventilated land-grab, abdicates in September,
after about 10 years of sovereignty. He is a re-
markably energetic man of absolutely ferocious
personal courage, much dignity, endless patience
—an ideal chieftain of a savage people. He is
Boss in British Papua, and no mistake about it;
and all his officers shake in their very shoes at the
sound of his voice. A pupil of the late incom-
parable John Thurston, he follows in the foot-
steps of that mighty autocrat. But many years
of unquestioned authority in a world-forgotten
corner have utterly unfitted him for a compre-
hension of constitutional government. He re-
gards Papua as his domain, and resents trespass.
He is slowly infusing the spirit of obedience into
the unruly native, and outside interference is
objectionable to the execution of his plans. A
syndicate running the show on his lines seems to
him preferable to the casual immigration of mixed
and disturbing private elements. He wants to
leave his kingdom in the assurance that his work
will be continued on the old autocratic lines, and
the long and arduous labors of the past not thrown
away; the idea of Papua dropping away into
Constitutionalism and democratic ideas is abhor-
rent to him. Hinc ille syndicate 1

Jos. Symes remarks in his Liberator on
the completion of its fourteenth year :
It has endured and survived the general and long-con-
tinued boycott of the news agents—very few of whom
will sell it, for fear of clerical wrath and pious malice.
The Liberator has survived treachery quite unparalleled,
and has outlived many of the traitors. It has survived
the want of advertisements,much to the astonishment of
most people ; and it has survived the want of money.
How many times every shilling at all available has been
paid away on Saturday at noon, and we have had to trust
in luck for Sunday food, I cannot tell, for they are many.
How many times the Liberator has been printed late,
because there was no money to buy paper for the week s
issue, will never be told.
As cheerful John Haynes used to say in the early
days of The Bulletin, “Jehovah Jira, the Lord
will provide.” How beautifully testifies atheist
Jos’s journalisticsurvival to the Efficacy of Prayer!

J.E. Shakespeare wrong once more! according to
‘Holdar M.,’ re suicide, because ‘There is no prohibition
vihatever in the Bible . . . against self-slaughter. But
Exod. xx. ch. and 13 v. says : ‘ Thou shalt not kill! ’—who
or what it doesn’t specify. And as the ‘ self-slaughterer’
kills himself, a lot of people, along with that shallow-
thinking YV. Shakespeare, believe that the 6th command-
ment appli< s to the self-murderer as well as to the man
who murders the other fellow.”—[The only point is that
language commonly distinguishes between killing, which
is the slaughter of the other fellow ; and suicide, which is
the slaughter of this fellow by himself. If “ J.E.” pro-
poses to take the commandment in its widest sense, then
it equally prohibits the slaughter of the moo-cow for
dinner. —Ed. B.]

Bland Holtian melodrama there is the same trusting girl
betrayed and going steadily downhill through five acts
in the usual way. Now, let an author (Pinero, say, or
Jones) introduce this very identical character into his
piece, and, if it be not labelled “melodrama,” he is
charged with writing a problem play ! It is the same girl
and the very same “ sin,” but when she comes on in the
drawing-rooms of genteel comedy, and it is spoken of in
epigrammatic language, the whole becomes a matter for
controversy which is never raised in the other case. I
have also noticed that family people who put perdition
and “ The Second Mrs. Tanqtieray ” pretty well on the
same level, listen with unmoved countenance to the
demands of the “fallen” one to be made “an honest
woman of; ” but let a reference to the illicit baby turn
up in the so-called “problem play” and they sit up at
once. And, anyhow, isn't there about room now for a
piece where the betrayed young person doesn’t want the
villain to marry her, and won’t be made “an honest
woman ” at any price ?
Mr. de Jones, “advance” for (say) the Grand Oriental
Dramatio co., arrived in Whaukoro, Maoriland. The
Oriental D. co. was in a bad way, but Mr. de J. himself
had made a haul at the Mason races the day before—had
£5O in notes in his pocket. He introduced himself to
Mr. O'Hooligan, of the Farmer’s Arms. Thus O’H.,
who does not know the financial standing of the co. :
“O, yez is the very payple for Whaukoro. Yez ’ll do
foine business. We’ve got here d’ Smith’s circutus
but dey did not bring all de animiles wid dem, andd’
payple was most disappinted, yer knaw ; and den we’ve
got d’ Ghostes family. Well, yer knaw, dey don’t tinlc
much of Ghostes in Whaukoro, yer knaw. Oh, yez'll do
foine business here.'’ “Well, I am triad to hear that,”
says de Jones, “for we’ve been having a bad time lately,
and we’re pretty well on our beam ends. I suppose you’ll
have no objection to financing us while we’re here and
letting us stay with you?” “Oh well, yer know whin I
come ter think av it, yer know, Dunedin is a bigger town
than Whaukoro, and aldough d’ Smith’s circutus did
not bring all de animiles wid dem, dey’s considerable
opposition, yer know, and den d’ Ghostes, yer know, well,
I don’t take much intherist in ghostes meself, yer know,
but you’d be surprised phwat a lot of intherist some
payple takes in them. I think p’r’aps yez’d do betther
business in Dunedin.” “ Well, perhaps we would. Any-
way we’ll have a drink,” and De Jones takes from bis
pocket the great roll of notes and places one ostenta-
tiously on the counter. “Oh, well yer know, as I was
sayin’ beefore, d’ Smith’s circutus did not bring all
de animiles wid demj yer know, and de payple was most
disappinted, yer know. And de Ghostes family, yer know,
as I was sayin’ before, dey doant take much intherist in
ghostes in Whaukoro, I rink yez’ll do foine business in
Whaukoro.” They stayed with Mr. O’Hooligan, but they
did not do “ foine ” business. Mr. O’H. presented bis
bill. But Mr. de Jones was in Dunedin. So was Mr.
de Jones’ roll of notes.
Entr’acte (Lon.): “Mr. Wilson Barrett has been
referring to those critics of things theatrical who invade
the inner life of the actor, but I am not at all sure that
he supplies us with more light. Mr. Barrett is a some-
what fervid denouncer, but he fails to get us any for-
rader. There is a conspiracy to obscure the shady things
done by the ladies and gentlemen of the stage, and all
this has been plainly demonstrated since the present year
began. Actors and actresses break up their own homes
and the homes of other people, but you must not say a
word about it, or the conspiracy of silence will be down
on you like a thousand of bricks. If Mr. Clement Scott
chooses to justify his charge, of course he can do so, and
very abundantly. I very much doubt, however, his
stomach for the fight.” The doubter was wise. Scott
hadn't any stomach for the fight. His bread wasn’t but-
tered that way.
“Old Jack”: Billy Hoskins used to say that brains
were not essential to good acting. “It is instinct, dear
boy—instinct! same as that which makes a dog hunt.
All this babble about Art is rot. The best actors I have
known have been dull fellows off the stage and out of
harness. All the study in the world won’t make a good
actor—but it may spoil one!” But, then, the old man
was talking of old-time acting. Nowadays, Art has a lot
to do with it— the alt of not acting. Another deoade, and
Duse or Bernhardt will shock the sensibilities of an in-
vertebrate generation as exponents of passions which
have no existence.
Macready acted Virginius in the evening, after burying
his daughter in the afternoon. He writes in his Journal
that his poignant grief exercised unwonted power on the
audience, and made their delight uncontrollable, but he
felt it degrading.
“ The Firm,” The Bulletin hears, proposes breaking
its rule of only producing London successes, and at the
end of the Pattie Browne season will stage, at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, a locally-written and composed musical comedy,
“ The American Girl.” Moulton supplies the music,
while Mr. Temple—known only, so far, as a schoolfellow
of Victor Daley—provides the libretto. Hitherto
managers have absolutely declined to entertain the idea
of local stuff, not because it was necessarily inferior, but
because the public looked for the London hall-mark. A
Melb. journalist once took his comedy to B. and 8., and,
having induced Boucy to read it, was told : “ I would put
it on to-morrow if I were in London, for I believe it to be
an excellent thing, but here it would simply mean failure
to acknowledge to Australians that the author is an Aus-
tralian.”
Business, Robbery, etc.
Up South there are two will-o’-the-wisp gold-
mines adjoining each other, the shares of which jump
when a rich ore-pipe is struck, and fall to zero when it
pinches. Suspecting a local assayer of market operations,
the name of the wrong mine was attached to a parcel of
rich “ tack ” sent for assay recently. It went many ounces
to the ton, and, after the assayer had loaded himself with
more shares than he could pay for, the man who left the
rock obligingly turned up and rectified the error. The
assayer nearly fainted.
* * * *
At Cloncurry, a few weeks back, a man brought
into town and exhibited several valuable nuggets. Local
branch of the Q.N.B. weighed the specimens and
advanced on them altogether £l7O, or about £3 10s. an
ounce. The vendor, in the fullness of his heart, forth-
with made the hospital a present of £5, and a bank
official, hankering after fame, was photo’d with the
specimens in his hand. Also, the Warden was inter-
viewed and requested to purchase the specimens and send
them ’Ome as exhibits. Sample nuggets were sent south,
arid in a few days arrived two wires from expert and
geologist. Wire 1 : “Parcel assayed. No value. Col-
lect V Wire 2 : “ Weight, 420 z. 18dwts. Melted alloy of
gold, copper, coke, quartz, and pure copper.” The
nugget-artist is at present in durance, and an in-
furiated bank official is reported to be offering- fabulous
prices for copies of a certain glorious photograph.

A custom prevails in Vic. wholesale houses of
posting notices outside the counting-house, “ Our pay-
day is ” —such and such a day of the week. An enter-
prising Victorian has invented a movable slip underneath
the board which announces “ Our pay-day is.” The in-
vention booms amongst the shifty concerns. Collector
calls on shifty house —say, on 4th of month. Shifty firm’s
clerk, who sees the collector coming, had “ 4th of month ”
up the day before, so he turns a handle, and, to the con-
fusion of the collector, it reads, “ Our pay-day is 2Uth.”
Collector imagines he has been mistaken previously, and
waits over to 24th. Calls on 24th, and is simply paralysed
on that day to read, “O.P.D. is 15th of the month.”
Eventually, of course, the shifty firm has to pay, but
some extra credit is gained by the device, which,
especially in Melbourne, sells like hot cakes.
* * * *
Matthew Davies (to witness in Melbourne
Insolvency Court) : “You paid a farthing in
the pound ?” Witness : “ I beg your pardon, I
•paid a shilling, and that is a good deal more
than some people did.” An excellent chance promptly
availed of, especially as the insolvency of the poor devil
under examination was partly attributable to indirect
losses over the examining counsel’s many busted institu-
tions.
* * * *
Gyles Turner, lecturing to Melb. Bankers’ In-
stitute on the ethics of Insolvency and the general sin-
fulness of financial Man, said that the local Trades Hall-
ers “ had shouted themselves hoarse in proclaiming that
the banks had robbed the public, whereas the banks knew
•by the indubitable evidence of their ledgers that it was
the public who had robbed the unfortunate shareholders
in the bank.” Gyles Turner states what isn’t false,
whereas the Trades Hall people told a much more import-
ant truth. A lot of “ unfortunate shareholders,” ns
shareholders, robbed one another when they robbed the
banks which they directed, or had privileged business
dealings with. Banks, for robbery purposes, are men,
also those men are shareholders and members of the
public, if they don’t happen to be directors. And even
directors form part of the public in a Gyles Turnerian
sense, perhaps. If we are to consider banks as mere
buildings with money in them, it is evident that the
people who borrow the money and don’t return it, rob the
shareholders. But when they themselves lend the money
that they borrow, and are a considerable portion of tho
shareholders, and don’t meet their calls when the bank
goes bung, they might just as well have put their hands
into the pockets of the solvent shareholders for all the
difference that it makes to those unfortunates.
* * * *
Gyles Turner, at Melb. Bankers’ Institute, was
all right in denouncing the hollow mockery of the “ joint
and several guarantee.” The cheerful guarantors, as the
Commercial Bank manager feelingly remarked, “start
raeingclubs, cricket clubs, and even churches, and when
they find them financial failures, include their share of
the liability in their schedules, and walk out with un-
abashed contentment.” The'guarantee goes jointly and
severally bung.

It no lunger pays to systematically boil down
sheep for their tallow, few N.S.W. or Queensland holdings
being overstocked, and the Menindie Boiling Down Co.’s
business has been taken over by a largely-English co.,
which will have most to do with meat-preserves and beef-
extract.
* * • *
Rabbit-preserving hasn’t been remunerative in
South Australia. So far the Robe works have failed ;
and now the Mt. Gambier Co. owns up to a loss on the
half-year of 618. Tinned rabbits, it is found, are unsale-
able at a paying price ; hut there are still hopes that a
profit can he got out of the frozen article.
* * * *
The Plunderderry (Coolgardie) strong-room
theft was more extraordinary than would be judged from
Eastern paper accounts, inasmuch as the burgled chamber
was underground, at the 200 ft. level, close up to the main
shaft. The stuff stolen was specimen quartz—36slb. of it,
estimated to contain lOOoz. gold.
* * * *
Poor, hard-up Westralian Govt, has just crush-
ingly put up rates on the Coolgardie railway-line. Some
figures for the 385 miles from Fremantle to Coolgardie
Mining machinery, £2 14s. 7d. to £4 19s. Bd. ; horse-feed,
except chaff, £2 14s. 7d. to f 3 19s. 2d. ; cutlery, hardware,
&c., £4 19s. Bd. to £7 6s. 7d. ; Corrugated iron, £2 14s.
7d. to 13 19s. 2d. ; candles, t 2 14s. 7d. to £4 19s. Bd. ; and
food-stuffs, in many cases, 50 per cent. Also, scores of
civil servants have been “ sacked.”
* * * *
Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) mining and smelt-
ing directors see a big new industry with inter-state Free-
• trade. In connection with the smelting of the local
copper and Barrier sulphide ores, 10,000 tons of sulphur
is annually wasted—enough to make 20,000 tons of
“chamber” sulphuric acid, valuable in superphosphate
manure manufacture. The present £5 per ton duty and
the refusal of owners to take small quantities in mixed-
cargo vessels together kill the industry at present—or,
rather, won’t let it be born.
* * * *
An inspector for fire-insurance underwriters
lately visited a Melb. kerosene store with a lighted pipe
in his mouth. He was promptly extinguished before 1 e
could achieve an awful explosion, and when quite out of
danger he got permission to walk around and see how the
“ risk” was looking.

“ Had Australia been federated twenty
years ago, New Guinea would be ours.” — Purvcs.
“That worm crawling over the face of
public affairs.”—Scobie Mackenzie, Maoriland
politician, on his political enemy.
Tomjoe Byrnes is Australia’s most pro-
mising Premier. It is very hard now to find any-
thing that Byrnes hasn’t promised.
John Downer is again to lead S.A. Oppo-
sition during the coming session. His chief
difficulty, as usual, will be to find the Opposition.
The biggest Vic. majority in favor of
Federation came from Ballarat. And Ballarat is
also the only place in Australia which ever took
up arms and shed its blood in a square fight
against oppression.
N.S.W. Public Works Committee has
consumed, between July, 1895, and present date,
over £25,000 in fees and travelling expenses. The
political gang, in its present voracious propor-
tions, expires with the present Assembly. R.I.P.

In his unwavering refusal to depart from
the Commonwealth Bill, Premier Kingston is
backed by all the other S.A. delegates. “As far
as South Australians are concerned, they have
distinctly reached the limit of concessions,” says
John Downer.
The only anti-Federalists in S.A. Assembly
who were sacked by their constituencies at the
recent Referendum were Labor-leader Batchelor
(W. Adelaide) and Single-taxer Frank Hourigan
(W. Torrens). Not a single Leg. Council district
went against the Bill.
A courageously-logical anti-Billite M.L.A.
holds that no sitting member of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment should be changed in favor of a new man,
unless that new man polls, as well as a majority of
votes, a minimum of 40 per cent, of all the votes
on the electoral district register.
Newcastle Morning Herald :
Mr. Brunker stated at the train last night that Federa-
tion was “ all right.” But beyond this he did not commit
himself.
But Papa has committed himself. And if Federa-
tion is “all right ” Brunker is all wrong !
Even in the form of taking the Federal
referendum there were absurd differences between
the several provinces. In S.A., if you wanted
the Bill you had to make a cross opposite the
“ Yes ” and leave the “ No ” alone ; in the other
provinces the “No ” had to be struck out.

Westralia lias put the Federal Bill on the
shelf. Its Enabling Act provided that W.A.
wouldn’t go in unless N.S.W. did likewise, so
Forrest’s province is going to await developments,
and the concert of the five provinces, which was
uot together with such difficulty, steadily breaks
up.
A section of the N.S.W. Provincialists
raise a loud outcry because the four leaders of the
local Federalists are lawyers. They, however, see
nothing wrong in being led by Reid, Want, Hey-
don, Salomons, Pilcher, &c. A canvas of the
Sydney Bar would show a larger proportion
against the Federalists than with them.
S.A. Parliament meets this Thursday
(23rd). Kingston will probably have his own
way although the Laborites are semi-hostile as
the result of the Federation movement. As an
illustration of Opposition weakness may be cited
the fact that the main charge against the Govt,
will be alleged Hospital maladministration !
Premier Reid, booming his proposed
Federation of the Eastern Australias, says that
Queensland is the natural ally of N.S. Wales.
And why isn’t South Australia? Anyway, a
good slice of N.S.W., which hasn’t any direct
communication with Sydney, is entirely depend-
ent upon South Australia both for railways and
supplies.
Gov. Patterson made short work of the
Alien difficulty in the early days of Australia.
That doughty blusterer simply issued a proclama-
tion forbidding any person not a natural-born
subject of the King to engage in trade or
settle in the territory without a previously-
obtained permission from the Governor or other
officer in command. A breach entailed heavy
fine and expeditious ejection.

stricken Tas. consumer wakes
np sufficiently to protest against
high though unprotective cus-
toms duties the stock argument

eight millions pay, by way of a

The Australian Anti - Bill
party insists on counting in the
people who didn’t vote as op-
ponents of the Federal Consti-
tution. They were silent —there-
fore they didn’t want any
change. Yet the burglar is a
silent man, and he wants all
the change he can get. The
man who didn’t vote was a man
who didn’t think the preserva-
tion of his provincial condition
worth the trouble of a vote.
And he didn’t think Federation
worth the trouble of a vote
either because, after a long ex-
perience of provincialism, he
had lost his interest in politics
of all kinds. The motto of the
man who doesn’t vote is : “ The
thing we has ain’t worth keep-
ing, and after a long experience
of the thing we has I don’t s’pose the thing we’re
goin’ to have’s any better.”
Tasmania notches another record. Of the
three Isle of Dreams electorates which polled
a majority against the Commonwealth Bill, two
return convention delegates—Dobson and Speaker
Brown—to the Assembly. Dobson and Brown
are therefore the only two delegates enthusiasti-
cally favoring the Bill whose pocket boroughs
“turned dog ” on them.
Vic. Premier Turner proposes to draft a
Bill to restrict the influx of inferior races by means
of an educational test. As his Ministry has put
up a record for badly-d rafted Acts, the services
of an expert should be called in. Turner took
great kudos for drafting the “ Purification of
Rolls Act,” which has since had to be tinkered
with a host of amendments.
John Downer announced, just prior to
opening of S.A. Parliament, that the Opposition
had not as yet had a meeting. Downer leads in
S.A. the most disorganised collection of second-
rate politicians yet collected together in Australia
as a party. Last year there were such jealousies
and differences as to leadership that one or two of
the most prominent of the crowd were hardly on
speaking terms.

Premier Reid, at the smoke-concert given
him by his admirers—
He felt deeply that Mr. Want was fighting a great
battle for the just rights and interests of N.S. Waies—
(applause)—at a great sacrifice of his strength, health,
and professional business; whilst he (Mr. Reid) was in the
very novel position of not being in the front of the fight

him anywhere else.

If 125 N.S.W. electorates had to poll an
average of 640 affirmative votes each to give the
Federal Bill a show of passing, why shouldn’t
they have to show at least 640 votes in favor of
the winning candidate at election time—if no can-
didate got 640 votes the constituency to be un-
represented till next election ? Out of 118 mem-
bers who had to fight for their seats in ’95, only
79 got as many as 640 votes.
Q. Labor Convention had nothing to say
re Federation. Perhaps, however, the Laborites,
having extended the modest 25 original planks of
the famous “ platform ” to 50, thought they had
done enough for once in a while—a conclusion in
which a weary world will, probably, concur. No
effort, apparently, was made towards compromise
with the hapless Opposition, and the close of ’9B
will, probably, witness a furious scramble for
dubious seats under the delighted eyes of the
Govt, nominees.
Dear Bulletin.—
Those who expect crimi-
nals to be treated in-
telligently, with a view to
their return to the ranks
of harmless workers, for-
get that humanity has
never yet found time to
treat its honest poor with
intelligence. It is doubt-
ful if it treats the worst
criminal worse than it
trea ts those who have been
unfortunate enough to lose
money and health, or those
who have never had either.
Humanity, in fact, treats
its members as sheep are
treated where they are
kept in vast numbers, and
are as cheap as in Aus-
tralia ; and not as the
English farmer treats
them, with an eye to
every unit of the flock.
And so it will have to
remain until the cost of
life in labor is much re-
duced. Not till we find
time to sit on a log are we
able to invent a chair. —

Dear Bulletin. —You butter A. I. Clark,
the new Judge. Now, when in the Assembly,
A.I. was notorious for slipshod legislation and for
his advocacy of shindykit Bills. Moreover, he
gave Hobart the most rascally Corporation Act
the modern world has seen, for it disfranchises
the tenants by doubling the landlord vote.
Further, though an ardent Federalist—especially
ardent for the Bill he had a hand in making—he
did not support the Commonwealth Bill lately sub-
mitted. Of course,-that doesn’t necessarily argue
that he is not a good Judge.— Jollytail.
Just on the eve of N.S.W. general eleo»
tion Premier Reid announces that he thinks no
more public-house licenses should be issued at
present. “There are too many public-houses.”
How characteristic of him—at general-election
time ! No publican wishes to see more public-
houses. Nor does any teetotaller. Votes from
both quarters for Mr. No-Yes On-the-Fenee.
Three hundred and forty-one Japanese
laborers landed a few days ago at Mourilyan Har-
bor and Dungeness (N.Q.)—for service on Northern
sugar-plantations. The Japanese Emigration So-
ciety, a semi-official body, organised the expedi-
tion, supplied the men with white uniforms, straw
hats, and the indispensable sleeping-mat. . . And
a fevv days ago the Q. Premier was proclaiming
the sleepless hostility of himself and his Govern-
ment to the immigration of Asiatic aliens.
If by any chance Australia can get an
amended Federation scheme which will provide
for the immediate taking over by the Common-
wealth of the customs, the railways, the debts,
and, if possible, the Education department, it
will certainly be a good thing. But as the N.S.W.
Premier fought his hardest to prevent the rail-
ways and the debts being taken over it is obvious
that he isn’t the man to get these valuable altera-
tions anyhow. And it was because the debts and
railway deficits were to be left (largely at Reid’s
instigation) on the hands of the States that it
seemed necessary to insert some kind of guarantee
that the Federal Govt, would raise a large revenue
and hand some of it back to the States to pay
their way with—hence Braddon’s famous “ Blot ”
and all the trouble which followed.
Vic. railway department has sold the
wooden bridges and culverts on Penshurst-Dun-
keld railway-line for ,£25, which ends, as far as
the department can end it, the most pitiful item
in the Gillies raihvay-boom. It was a little cross-
country line from a dead town with 550 people
and a dog, to a casual village with 300 people and
a lame horse, and as both places had a railway
already the affair was so hopeless that it was left
unopened. It was built at a cost of £50,000, and
then the department tore up the rails and sleepers,
and removed or sold the stations, and let it slide.
The Penshurst-Dunkeld line had become only a
legend when the eagle eye of Commissioner
Mathieson fell upon it, and he promptly rescued
the last possible £25 out of the lamentable spec,
by trading off the bridges for firewood.
S.A..’s ink-slinging parson contributes this
charming epistle to an Adelaide paper :
THE REFERENDUM VOTES —SOUTH AUSTRALIA,
1896-1898.
To the Editor.
Sir, —
Two years ago they voted, five to one,
To keep their “ perfect ” schools from God’s Own Son ;
This year, on fourth of God s bright June,
They, after racing, shout the self-same tune ;
And vote, consistently with what they breed,
To rear a Commonwealth without a Creed.
Such are their “ Referenda”—registered above
As proofs of folly, and as calls to Love
To raise His pleadings for His foes anew :
“ Father, forgive ; they know not what they do ! ”
I am, sir, &c.,
John W. Owen.
And yet the humorless clergy complain of the
decay of the religious spirit!
Premier Reid is already fishing anxiously
for votes in many troubled waters. Within the
last few days he has promised that the Civil
Service Board will raise the salaries of the Public
Service ; he has announced that he is in favor of
raising the Age of Consent—which will probably
catch the votes of the Dirty Saints ; and he has
started to angle for the public-house vote by
declaring himself in favor of opening hotels on
Sunday, and of stopping the issue of new licenses
for a time—which will make the monopoly of the
present license-holders more valuable. At the
same time, he has promised that a member of the
Ministry (presumably Cook) will have a chance to
introduce a Bill providing for local and State
option without compensation—which is the biggest
reach-out for the Cold Tea vote up to date.

Maoriland nearly had a vacancy the other
day for a Premier. Digger Dick was inspecting
the unfinished new Houses of Parliament, when
a brick fell on him from a great height and re-
bounded off his breast-bone. It was a narrow
escape, but Seddon’s tremendous chest stood the
shock well, and the vacancy didn’t happen.
WHISKY AND HEALTH.
Whisky of the right sort, such as the “ D.C.L.”
Scotch (Black Bottle), taken in the right way, is
good for body and mind, it preserves health, and
is often an invaluable remedy in disease. Note
“D.C.L.”*
“Ricardo”: Melb. demise, Howard, ex-
President of the Stock Exchange, a grand old-
schooler, handsomest man of his day and business,
and straight' as a die. The temptations of a, stock-
broker are like those of a bookmaker who is am-
bitious to back or, more dangerous still, own
horses. Yet the fielder must win, if he sticks to
his last, lays to his book in the widest sense. A
Howard shows us how the honest broker, as Bis-
marck termed himself, can go through life most
cozily, and, though the boom tried to flatten him,
it happily did not succeed.
AN UP-TO-DATE BUSINESS.
The establishment of Messrs. Washington H.
Soul and Co. (at ICO and 2GB Pitt-street, Sydney),
which began in a rather small way, has become
one of the largest of Australasian institutions by
giving good value for money and by being right
up to date. The ladies’ new department, in
charge of a trained nurse, is cordially approved
by the whole medical profession as well as by the
ladies themselves. This department is fitted up
for the convenience and privacy of ladies requiring
to purchase any of the articles necessary to the
relief of ailments, any of the thousand articles for
beautifying the complexion, perfumery, perfumed
soap and dentifrice. And the firm’s buyers in
Europe and America are sending specialities to
this department by every mail. Lately, too, the
entire establishment has been altered and extended
—more room and more light given to customers —so
that the most exacting may be pleased. The
packing department has 40 men constantly em-
ployed in turning out boxes and packing etceteras ;
and at 268 Pitt-street many are employed in
manufacturing the famous Soyer’s Baking Powder
and Soul’s Australian Relish. The confectionery
dept, has a stock of pure sweets, especially
American candy, which should draw the whole
juvenile population of N.S.W. to the counter.
Another dept., by no means connected with the
above, is the dispensing dept., where a staff of
duly qualified members of the Pharmacy Board
dispense the purest drugs under a complete system
of check. There is also a man’s trying-on room.
The whole business is controlled by the proprietor
Mr. W. H. Soul, assisted by his brother-in-law,
Mr. W. R. Bird, as general supervisor.

There are Several Best Bicycles,
But when they are compared with the great Red Bird Special
they drop to second-grade.
IT IS ONLY NECESSARY to adjust the hearings of the great “ RED BIRD
SPECIAL ” once a year. Each ball is contained in separate steel case. It is im-
possible for them to jam or otherwise cause friction. Axis is parallel with the
wheel, which gives the easiest-running bearing in the world.
The bottom bracket bearings are contained in a separate steel barrel which can
he adjusted in the bottom bracket proper, keeping chain alignment perfect.
Sprocket is set between the bearings, giving chain an even pull without side twist.
These are only a few of the many improvements of the great Red Bird Special.
WHY NOT CALL AT THE DEPOT AND SEE A SAMPLE ? IT WILL
TEACH YOU SOMETHING ABOUT BICYCLES.
