SPORTING NOTIONS.
There was a notable absence of triers at Canter-
bury Park Races last Saturday, but punters were more
successful than usual. The respective winners were the
aged Blacklock, Kiteware, Lyrist, Flintlock, P.N., and
Annadour, starting respectively at seven’s, two’s, two’s,
ten s, twelve’s, and four’s. The consistent Savourneen
was beaten by a head in the handicap byP.N., butshe was
unlucky to lose.
» * • •
Kiteware, who won Canterbury Park Stakes, is
a new arrival from the country, and is a likely-looking
sort. He hadn’t much to beat on Saturday, but he did it
well and should be heard of again.
• • * *
The attractions of “a, cut of the sweep’’will
produce a big field in Rosehill ’Cup to-day (Sat.). The
heavy support awarded to Vigorous looks as if business
was meant in that quarter ; Loch Maree, Devoted, True
Blue, Bob Ray, The Tola, and Robin Hood being also well
backed. Of this lot, True Blue and The Tola may be
found most forward at the finish, though, should Pre-
caution be supported in the right quarter, this flying
mare may secure the prize, as she will have the distinct
advantage of performing on her own course.
• * * *
Undeterred by the recollection of previous dis-
asters, the ever-ready and willing backer is supporting
his “own particular” for V.R.O. Grand Nationals, Soult
for Hurdles being already a pronounced favorite, with
So-and-So, Legs, Paul Pry, and Homespun also receiving
attention. If Acton could be depended on to “ lep” well,
his chance should be second to none, whilst Irish Stew
and The Sketch also have nothing to complain of in the
matter of weight. The Steeple fancies are Doondiah,
Havseed, Songster, and Blue and Gold, but intending
backers should not overlook the chances of A r igil and
Medicine, who may on the day be found capable of
“downing” more fancied animals.
* « * »
Weights for the V.R.O. Nationals have not
much affected the small and early market for Hurdle and
Steeple goods. Whernside got more than was expected
(his immediate scratching, along with sundry stable com-
panions, suggests that chairman Miller is very cross
indeed with the club’s handicapper just now), but other
popular candidates are kindly treated, as a rule. So-and-
so, The Bulletin’s fancy last year, should stand a good
chance in the Hurdle Race, with list. 41b., presuming
that Alec Taylor has worked an improvement in the
speedy offspring of Fryingpan, which is almost certain.
Hayseed is not crushed out of the Steeplechase, and
Doondiah—but lots of things may happen before the
second Saturday of the Flemington “ sticks” meeting.
* * * *
Not much room left for doubting that the
English top-sawyers are a bit faster than Australian
ditto. Newhaven was beaten at Ascot by The Rush, who
ran M’rman to a neck last year when giving 71b. to the
Australian over 2J4 miles. Newhaven probably had 51b.
the worst of the weights at Ascot, but then he finished
third only. It looks as though The Rush were quite the
equal of our exported cracks. Well, Galtee More would
have been backed to give him a stone over any course.
• » • *
Lord W. Beresford, who manages things racing
for “ Mr.” Jersey, placed Maluma right at Lewes, where
the Malua mare easily won back part of her purchase-
money. Flying at too high game has unprofitably ex-
posed Newhaven’s form, and it will need generalship to
enable the erratic one to repay exes. There is a greater
opening and more money in “ chasing,” and Australian
owners would be wise in turning their attention to
“ Jeppers,” as ’Ome form appears much below par.
* * • »
Mr. Buckland had bad luck in his record-
breaking trial with trotter Fritz in Maoriland. Rain fell
the night before and made the going heavy. Fritz trotted
a quarter in 31 2-5, half in 1.3}, three-quarters in 1.33 J,
and the mile in 2.14 J. Brought out later on, he could
only put up 2.15.
« * * *
At Toowoomba, recently, a Bulletin correspon-
dent saw a pen of 10 horses sold for 17s. 6d. the lot! The
shadow of the bike ?
* * » *
Westralian climatic conditions operate most
beneficially on imported horseflesh. Paul Pry was a
commoner ere he raced in the West. Faultless", another
failure in Melbourne, put up some really smart hurdle
p-rformances on Perth courses. The Provost, like Paul
Prv, cleared the board in his class among the sand-
gropers, then showed form in S.A., and should run well
in V.R.O. Grand National, for he is a great stayer and
clever jumper. A few others which improved greatly or
regained in W.A. apparently lost form—Common, Stead-
fast, Thunder, Speculation, Tellus, Duke of York, Euglo.
» * » *
At a W.A. country meeting the running of a
metropolitan horse in one event had been so peculiar,
and the smell so pungent, that even the stewards looked
serious, and the owner was requested to appear before
them. That worthy swung nonchalantly into the august
presence, apparently more interested in his cigar than in
any other thing on earth. He was asked a few questions
by the chairman, a noted heavy bettor, and replied to
each with airy insolence. “ Bad ? Well, yes, it did look
bad.’’ “Explain? No, he didn’t think so.” “Say?
Oh, no ; he’d just leave it to them.” He was told to re-
tire, and did so, but suddenly his head appeared in the
doorway as, with outstretched finger pointed menacingly
at the chairman, he exclaimed, in tones pregnant with
meaning : “ If I go out, he goes out too. Mind that—he
goes out too, and perhaps one or tw r o more of you.”
Next morning’s paper stated that “ Mr. Blank’s explana-
tion was accepted as satisfactory.”
* * » *
A.’s mare seemed a certainty for the big race of
the meeting, and 8., owner of the next best, having A.’s
assurance that all was right, backed the mare for a fair
stake. But A. had worked the double on B.’s jockey and,
thank s to the latter’s market operations, got a nice price
about the second best. According to instructions (from
A. B.’s jockey made all the running, the mare lying off
and coming with a late run at the finish. There was an
exciting battle in the last 50 yards and, just when it was
apparent that the boy on.the favorite had come a little
too late, B. ’s horse stumbled, and the mare won by a
length or so. A. went nearly insane when B. thanked
him for the information, by which he had profited so well,
and had a private;chat to one of the stewards. Result—
B. horse, and-jockey (whose incrimination of A. w'as
regarded as a malicious invention) were disqualified for
12 months.
* * * *
Adelaide W.C.T.U., backed by an assortment
of parsons and other allegedly pious folk, is actively work-
ing up a big petition against the totalisator. The move-
ment brings no alarm to friends of the machine, which is
particularly strong in public favor at present. The tote,
properly and honestly administered as it is now under
the direct supervision of the police, has considerably re-
duced betting. The bookmaker element has been cut
down so much that juvenile ante-post betting, rampant
awhile ago, has now almost disappeared. The unpracti-
cal saints, who are so palpably playing into the hands of
Ikey Mo, have no chance of succeeding in their stupid
campaign.
* * * *
A prominent Australian bookie, whose lang-
widge is somethink orful, has a pleasing habit of throw-
ing a client’s cash on the ground if aforesaid client is dis-
satisfied with the odds he offers. At a recent meeting,
however, he got a lesson in kind which will probably last
him his lifetime. A man desiring to back a certain prad,
and hearing on all sides the offers of 7 to 1, supposed that
the forcible one’s quotations were the same, and handed
over five sovs. “Six to one,” said the book. “ I want
sevens," responded the punter. “Then, blank yer
eyes, yer won’t get it here,” and with a swing the book
scattered the five pieces on the grass, when on the
instant the toe of the backer's boot landed hard on the
bottom of the riqgman’s bag and a beauteous fountain of
gold and-silver coins) to say nothing of notes, played for
a twinkling all around. Then while the book and his
penciller were pouncing on the bundles of notes, the
backer had collected his fiver and was off. And as all the
stable-boys and spielers within cooey rushed to help the
book to collect his scattered fortune, he didn’t do so
badly by coining out only £lO7 short 1
« * m w
“ Holdar M.”: The three-card operator haunts
Sydney senior football matches, despite the utterly un-
promising nature of the attendant crowd. At a recent
gathering one of these generally-astute gents approached
a group whose preliminary glare of stony unbelief and
flippant demeanor would have soured the self-assurance
of Premier Reid. The party with the colored cardrboard,
however, wasn’t a bit dismayed, and his invitations were
loud and lingering, although a piece of mellow fruit in
the eye presently interrupted his “buttoner’s” heavy
wager. He refused to be discomfited and recovered his
vision just as a hnnk of soil scattered his elderly gamp.
Is the monte man’s discretion waning, or is he merely
the victim of a congested profession?
* * * •
An attendance of upwards of 8000 at the Pirate-
s match on Saturday last, was a football record
for Sydney Cricket Ground. In the first half, Randwick,
by a splendid bit of unselfish play, scored a try, from
which Barrie kicked a goal. For the rest of the
half no further score was made, although the game was
fast and interesting. Soon after the second spell began
Pirates scored a try and goal, which seemed to give them
fresh heart, as they scored rapidly, and when time was
called the game stood Pirates 16, Randwick 8. In the
Wallaroo-University match, also played on Sydney
Cricket Ground, Wallaroo won by 21 points to 10. For
the premiership the clubs now stand : Wallaroo, 7 points ;
Pirates and Randwick, 6 ; Sydney, 5 ; and University, 4.
University will now have to defeat Pirates to secure a
place in the semi-finals for the Cricket Ground trophies.
* * * *
Randwick is the most unpopular team in Syd-
ney, the reason given being that they win all their
matches by brute strength and stupidity. Interesting to
note, however, that if ever there is a good, open, fast
game it is always between Randwick and University.
’Varsity always plays an open game, but Randwick’s
strength lies in tackling any team at its own game and
beating it.
* * * *
Just when another succession of victories by
Essendon threatened to fairly kill public interest in Vic.
League football, th#big club goes down on consecutive
Saturdays before Geelong and Collingwood. Result :
Essendon and Fitzroy equal, and four others, all equal,
close up—and assured big gates for the future.
* * » a
Football has had a revival in Adelaide. There
is a triangular duel between three strong teams, Norwood
and South and Port Adelaide. The former club, in old
times perhaps the best team in Australia, is, so far, at the
top with an unbeaten record. Adelaideans still remember
how Norwood, years ago, beat “ Sowth ” Melbourne, then
Victoria's premier team, in all of three premiership
matches.
* » * *
A youth, recently fined at Carlton (Melb.), for
street-footballing, had the penalty heightened because
he had previously been convicted of larceny. Beak-logic
is indeed a fearsome thing.
* * * *
Harry Musgrove’s letter to the Age of Thursday
week puts a totally different complexion on the baseball
team and its American worries, and shows how necessary
it always is to take the muttered thunder of the other
fellow with a packet of salts. Mus'grove' comes out all
right, and it should not have been forgotten tkat the trip
was persisted in despite his emphatic advfbe to the
contrary. The Age is now satisfied that “the charges
made against Mr. Musgrove’s integrity and business
capacity are without foundation.” Nothing succeeds
like success, and nothing fails like failure. The
Mr. Harry Musgrove who managed the lucrative Austra-
lian eleven was another party altogether from the fellow
who ran the dead-beat and broken Australian baseballers.
• * * *
Melb. laerosseurs, who are shortly to invade
Sydney (shooting begins Aug. 13) sensibly tell the Sydney
men not to attempt to return the lavish hospitality, they
received in Melb. last July. The Sydneyites, knowing
their association is not yet strong financially, feared
the Melb. boys would expect extensive preparations, and
were nobly determined to make them at all risks.
* * * *
Ranji Barden won the first race he started in on
his return to England—a mile pursuit against a top-
notcher like Walters. Doesn’t seem to support the
“ lost form ” theory by which he accounted for his lick-
ings in Australia.
* * * *
During the “ off ” season the two Melb. cycling
papers strive to keep up circulation by getting each other
by the wool. The older publication goes in for tolerably
dignified rebuke, but its opponent hits out in all direc-
tions, coming out every week with some new “exposure”
of the perfidy of its reptile contemporary, and caricatur-
ing the latter’s awfulness on its front page. Altogether,
the scrimmage is the liveliest thing Australian cycling
has seen since the Bilmartin stoush incident.
Cycling journalism down South is in a parlous
state, anyhow. One paper has fallen out with some of its
advertisers, and never loses an opportunity of rubbing it
into them in its columns, but, as the contract happens to
be unexpired, the paid-for page of space is used by the
assailed parties to get back on the editorial column.
Consequently the reader is informed in one column of the
Scorcher that the Blankley Cycle co. is a howling “ do”
that sweats its employes, and never won an honest race
with its wares, while the next quotes previous issues of
the Scorcher itself to prove that sheet a humbug of the
deepest dye.
* * * *
Young Body, the tough bike-plugger of Mel-
bourne, unlike most cycle pros., has ideas above his
machine, and is the proud author of a new and original
torpedo (copyrighted) which he is taking to England with
the idea of fame and fortune. Body is a clever mechanic,
and has earned £2OOO on his bike in 24 months, to enable
him to push his war machine in the right quarter.
* * * *
S.S. :Re the bicycle face. Is it my fancy, or is
bike-riding, especially the professional work, developing
a peculiar chin ? It seems to me that every second man
who pushes pedals for his daily bread has a hard, obtru-
sive chin that overshoots the mark.
* * * *
The meeting, at Brisbane, between M'Gowan, of
Melb., and Tweedie, of Sydney, proved a great mill. For
12 rounds Tweedie had all the best of it on points, but
early in the 13th round M’Gowan landed a lucky right on
“ the point ” and knocked out the Sydney man. Tweedie
is second only to “ Griffo ” in science, but his very clever-
ness.proves his downfall, as by over-confidence he throws
his chance away. The same thing happened in his one
fight in America.
» * * •
Yousoff, the “ Terrible Turk,” has been causing
a commotion in the States. In his second meeting with
wrestler Roeber, in N.Y., the Turk could not throw
Roeber, and, losing his temper, struck him. Roeber,
who knows something of the boxing game, then hauled
off and “ bashed ” the Turk. Billy Brady jumped into
the ring claiming a foul for the Turk, whereupon Fitz-
simmons, Brady’s mortal enemy; took a hand, and tried
to pull him out of the ring. Brady struck Fitz, and the
latter, getting a “holt,” nearly broke Brady’s neck. A
score of policemen jumped into the arena, followed by a
strong man known as the “ Terrible Greek,” who tried to
get the strangler’s hold on the “ Terrible Turk.” At the
close of the hubbub, Brady declared Fitzsimmons had
deliberately tried to break his neck, and had severely
strained his back and neck. Taken altogether, it was the
biggest brush the Yanks have had since the war began.
* • * *
Steve O’Donnell has received still another beat-
ing, this time at the hands of one “Stockings” Conroy.
Evidently time O'Donnell tried another game.
* * * *
Goddard’s beating of Peter Maher seems to
have been purely and simply a bit of luck for Goddard.
Maher started to knock Goddard all over the ring. God-
dard, in getting out of the way, made a back-handed
swipe at Maher, whose jaw happened to be in the road,
and the subsequent proceedings interested him no more.
Bad luck for the rising Maher, prospective champion.

to his country on condition that he should be a “ kernel.”
Pink ’Un suggests for Jonel the post of Inspector-General
of Canteens.
* * • *
“Ponte”: Clothes make the parson if not the
man. I met one at a quiet country hotel, and out of
pure cussedness suggested a game of billiards. He said :
“ I would be only too delighted, but unfortunately I have
no mufti with me. Of course I cannot play in my clerical
costume.” I lent him a coat and he took me down for
the table, then for drinks, and, lastlv, for five bob.

Time, 8 p.m., Referendum night. Drunk wob-
bling in middle of street. Cheer goes up as a fresh re-
turn is posted. Drunk wheels round, tacks across to
edge of crowd and reads laboriously—“Victor-ia-a (hie)
two —mill-ionan—twenty thousun.” Looks mixed, reads
again, and appears satisfied. “ Blank (hie) fine score,
bigosh ! Take the (hie) blanky Englishm’n all blanky
(hie) time to beat that.”
* * *
The writer, at Rooty Hill the other day, asked
an enthusiastic old trainer of greyhounds for a specific for
fleas in dogs. “Never used anything myself,” was the
reply. “ I sleeps with ’em, and fleas won't stay on dogs
while a man’s about.”
* « * »
Dear B.—The hares no doubt think coursing
not quite the game it is cracked-up to be. When the
hare is collared by the dogs it is worried by them until
the attendants come up and drag them off, when the
hare is thrown on one side. On Friday last, one poor
brute, terribly mauled, and with one foot bitten clean off,
recovered sufficiently to hobble away. If the “ kill ”
takes place within hearing distance, it is pitiable to
listen to the poor little brute’s cries whilst being crunched
up by the dogs, but the ladies present seem to think it
all fair game. What price the Society for Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals, which follows to the bitter end a
dealer who uses a horse with a sore shoulder, and stands
by whilst a hare is eaten alive ? —W.

The Bulletin has received the Sydney
issue of The Globe-Trotter, the little newspaper
which Messrs. Barri&re and Gorini, the Parisian
travelling journalists, have undertaken to publish
(in the local tongue) in every important city of
their itinerary. The preceding issue was in
’Frisco, and it is the intention of these enterprising
young men—who support themselves as they go
by absolutely anything they can find to do—
to successively enliven with the Globe-Trotter
the dreary solitudes of Melbourne, Colombo, Cal-
cutta, Cairo, Athens, and Rome. It is 2g years
since they left Paris without a son, and nearly 2
years more will be needed to complete their plucky
but fantastic undertaking. The G. T. itself is not
BARRIERS and GORINI, the voluntarily-
penniless French globe-trotting journalists now in
Sydney.
remarkable for Gallic journalistic “go,” and,
probably, the youth of the adventurous pair of
scribes is responsible for their ingenuous surprise
at the discovery that “ English girls are not
tall and thin, with long faces and immense teeth.
On the contrary, they are graceful, with pretty
eyes and a charming smile. . . . And, what
liberty they are allowed ! A young Miss returns
home at midnight with her sweetheart, and no
one finds fault that a kiss should terminate the
conversation.” This, if it hasn’t much of the
rus6 Parisian pressman about it, promises better
things to come. We remember noticing such
little matters ourselves !
Reid’s New Bill.
N.S.W. Parliament opened last Tuesday, and the
Reid Government announced the amendments it
wants made in the Federal Constitution. Said
announcement, which leaves things just about as
blank as they were before, was as follows, to quote
word for word from the Governor’s speech :
(1) An objection to the principle of equal representa-
tion in the Senate, which, if not altered, must be accom-
panied by the removal of the stipulation requiring that a
majority vote at a joint sitting of both Houses to be
effective shall consist of three-fiths of the members pre-
sent ; or, failing that, the principle should be qualified
by a provision for a national referendum instead of a
joint sitting.
(2) Some of the financial provisions to be recast, and
the Braddon clause omitted altogether.
(3) Money bills not to be amended by the Senate.
(4) The same protection for the territorial rights of
each State, as there is for the representation of each State
in the Federal Parliament and this should include more
definite provisions with regard to inland rivers.
(5) Seat of Government—instead of the proposal in the
Bill, adoption, in a slightly-modified form, of the plan
followed in the Canadian Constitution.
(6) It is also considered that the appellate jurisdiction
should be remodelled.
As regards equal representation in the Senate and
the three-fifths majority, Reid barracked and
voted for both of them in the Convention. He
also voted for the financial scheme as it appears in
the Bill. In the above statement he doesn’t ex-
plain which of the financial provisions are to be
re-cast, or how they are to be re-cast;
or what alteration he wants in the clauses
relating to rivers, or what further provision is
wanted for the “territorial rights of each
state,” or what special “rights” are to be
provided for ; or what alterations are wanted re
the Court of Appeal; or how he wants the capital
fixed. With regard to the capital, however, he
wants the Canadian plan adopted with some un-
specified modifications, so presumably he is again
on the grab to have the Federal city located in
Sydney, where his supporters’ bricks and mortar
are mostly located. The federalising of the debts
and railways has seemingly been quite forgotten
again, and the bounty question isn’t even men-
tioned, and, in the absence of definite information
about the things that are mentioned, New
South Wales still doesn’t know' what it is sup-
posed to be asking, or the other provinces what
is supposed to be asked. But even if Reid
did specify his alterations it wouldn’t make
any difference in the choice between him and
Barton at the coming election. If N.18.W. wants
concessions it can’t ask them through Reid with
much hope of success. The difference between the
two men is just this : Barton never played the
traitor to the other provinces by inserting first a
50,000 and then an 80,000 minimum after he had
arranged a scheme with no minimum in it at all.
He didn’t speak against the Bill and vote for it.
He didn’t vote persistently to put things in the
Bdl and then appeal to the country to reject the
Bill because of the things be had done his best to
put in it. He didn’t go partners with the bitter
provincialist Want, the man who has denounced
any form of Federation as a “hydra-headed
monster basking in its constitutional beastliness,”
&c, and who calls the other federating provinces
“ wolves” and “robbers.” He wasn’t a political
traitor all through, and he never fooled the other
provinces with bogus invitations to unite. And
the other provinces could negotiate with him
knowing that he negotiated in good faith and
wouldn’t go back on his word, whereas no decent
Federationist can afford now to be found dead in
the same paddock with Reid. It doesn’t matter
now what alterations Reid wants or doesn’t want.
N.S.W. has got to send a man to conduct
the next set of negotiations in whose honesty
the other parties concerned can put reasonable
trust. And that man isn’t G. H. Reid.
In a last week’s “ Political Points ” para-
graph about an Adelaide newspaper’s remarkable
change of front re Federation, the Observer
(attitude of which has been consistently in favor
of the measure) was, The Bulletin regrets, in
advertently mentioned instead of the Adver-
tiser.
“Warwick’s presents to Warwick” is a
standing headline in the Brisbane papers. Byrnes
represented Warwick (Q.) at the Jubilee, and took
gifts therefrom to Warwick (Eng.) which, in
return, felt called upon to send out a loving cup
and an address to her namesake. These arrived
the other day and will be presented by the
Premier, on which occasion he is expected to fill
out his policy somewhat.

T. H. Griffith, the local man who pro-
poses to compete with Ball for Albury (N.S.W.)
at next elections, is formally introduced to the
electoral public as “ the gentleman who, when
Mayor, displayed hospitality to distinguished visi-
tors.” M.L.A.’s of all sides hope he will-be elected
for somewhere. Of late, a generous hand at tho
Parliamentary refreshment-bar has been difficult
to find.
At Fitzroy (Melb.) court recently, a man,
charged with resisting the police, pleaded that he
is subject to fits, and when he has a fit coming on
his struggles are very violent. The policeman in
this case tried to arrest him at the outset of a fit.
Similar epileptic struggles had occurred on seven
or eight previous occasions when the Law was en-
deavoring to run him in, so the beak
summed up against defendant as follows: “The
constable tried to fit a charge of theft upon him
because he took a fit in his head, it is alleged, to
steal a bag of oatmeal, and there was some diffi-
culty about a fit with the handcuffs, and we will
now try to make the punishment fit the crime.”
Fined £2. The little jokes of the Great Unpaid
must not be permitted to gush unseen.
•Retort courteous from Inspector of Nui-
sances to Smithfield (N.S.W.) Council :
Since my last report I have inspected the dairies and
found them fairly satisfactory and the cows in good
health. I have also inspected Alderman Downey’s
slaughtering premises and found them in fairly good
order and cattle in good health, and would have no doubt
taken the prize at any lean cattle show. I have also in-
spected Mr. M'Tagget’s premises and found them not too
favorable, but he will reniove shortly. I could not see
anj’ maggots crawling around, as Alderman re-
marked and Alderman —— saw crawling. I think that
boys that live in glass houses should not throw stones.
And in reference to dead beasts, I found the owners of
eight and had them removed or burnt. One I could not
find the owner of and had it burnt. The other I found
the owner of. He was not able to pay for it, so I thought
it was advisable not to take proceedings against him as
he left the district. I had it also burnt. It was in a very
bad state and very difficult to burn, and recommend pay-
ment for burning them the sum of 10s. each. —Your
worthy Inspector, James Stein.
Melbourne's Reform Club, which was
given up for dead a while ago, has arranged to
carry on its “work” in the old rooms of Melb.
Bicycle Club, and the other night it gave a social
with Walter Madden in the chair, and Murray
Smith on the right hand of that calamitous legis-
lator. The social filled a long-felt want by
enabling somebody to explain the political pur-
pose of the Reform Club. Reformers Madden
Smith and Co. are seeking the welfare of the great
body of the public. Bearing m mind that intel-
lectual capitalists (such as Walter Madden) are
regarded with ignorant suspicion and ridiculous
misgiving by “people lower in the
the Club intends to stoop to conquer the lower
orders ” aforesaid by force of wise proposals for
the general good. Also, the Club will cultivate a
courteous manner to its inferiors, and spread its
smile of self-satisfaction over the “community at
large,” so to speak. Then, in the course of a few
years, the Club will rise to a dizzy height of in-
fluence, and the name of Walter Madden will be
associated in the minds of the toiling millions
with Freedom and Sweetness and Light and other
desirable matters. At present the Club is sending
round the hat for further funds, and the Multi-
tude’s opinion of the chairman is not all that it
ought to be.
The Poetical Version of His N.S.W. Ex-
cellency’s tussle with that pig :
Some Sydney Hampden that with dauntless front
The little tyrant of his fields withstood.
A Semaphore (S. A.) parson, on the Sunday
after Referendum day, prayed “that God would
forgive all those who voted, in favor of the Com-
monwealth Bill.” But, though the parson talks
like that, he doesn’t mean it —not as a rule, any-
how. What he particularly wants to convey is
that the fellow who voted for the Bill is in a fair
way to be damned if God doesn’t forgive lnm.
And he gets his remark off in the pulpit, where
the other fellow can’t talk back at him.
It looked rather bad to see the Brisbane
Labor Convention excluding the press from its
deliberations. When Fat Man attempts any such
exclusion democrats are loud in their invectives
against “Star Chamber procedure,” and so forth.
It is, however, a satisfaction to know that the
field of reform-legislation was extended ; that a
party which found it utterly hopeless to carry One-
man-one-vote in Parliament has improved its
chances (in its own estimation) by substituting
One-adult-one-vote ; that all the proceedings were
jierfectly harmonious and gladsome ; and that
the members had their portraits taken and printed
for the benefit of the public.

A shockingly amusing miser is located in
Ballarat Hospital just now. He was lately

covered on his aromatic person. At the hospital

A married woman at Ch. Towers, who com-
plained of her husband’s unkindness, procured
kerosene, saturated her clothes with it, and
applied a match. The blackened, writhing body
was taken to the Towers hospital, where it
lingered in life several days. Kerosene appears
to be the favorite feminine remedy for earthly
troubles in N.Q. Third case of the kind in 12
months. As for the N.Q. men, their remedy is
dynamite-in-the-mouth.
Suicide is usually understood to be the
prerogative of man. The animals, however,
occasionally usurp it. Writer once owned
a big black draught horse. Feed for this
hor. e "as kept in a small, square-built room
on blocks down the allotment. One day
the door was left ajar, a sack of corn
standing just inside it. The horse came along,
saw the corn, put his head inside the door and
began to eat. Some one coming along behind
gave a shout to frighten the animal away, and the
big brute, hastily drawing back his head in his
attempt to escape, pulled the door to also. The
more he tried to withdraw his head the closer
he drew the door, until he fell down, and was
suffocated. The room was nearly shifted off the
blocks. A minute or two sufficed for the whole
business.
“Tasmanian” to The Bulletin :
Re your story of the Lady Franklin transport ship, the
facts are, I think, wrong. There was no Port Arthur in
1814. The special hell of V.D.L. at that time was Mac-
quarie Harbor. The barque might have been built there,
and then. But, ]f so, not “for transporting convicts
from V.D.L. to Norfolk Island,” for the latter place was
then an appendage of N.S.W., whither all convicts en
route to the island, were transferred. Again, the vessel
was named after the wife of Sir John Franklin, who did
not become Governor of V.D.L. until 1837. I remember
the barque well, and the curious horror with which I once
inspected the fittings between decks, where the convicts
were confined during transport. It is good to break her
up, for her timber must reek with the taint of unspeak-
able horrors.
Pretty little Bowen has been the cradle of
Queensland’s Attorney-Generals. Beor, Pope-
Cooper, and Chubb were all members for Bowen,
and all subsequently Attorneys-General. T. J.
Byrnes, though never member for Bowen, was a
Bo wen schoolboy. In the face of all this, it was a
very unkind cut when Bananaland Govt, robbed
Bowen of its Judge and transferred him to Towns-
ville. Bowen had certainly given (in a legal way)
far more than it had received.

“ Cabbage-tree ”on certain Sydney adven-
turers :
Coming up Erskine-street, the other day, saw a little
girl howling over a broken bottle, contents running
down the pavement. Between sobs little girl told the
rapidly collecting crowd that she had dropped a bottle of
vinegar, and was afraid to go home, as her mother would
thrash her to death. Good Samaritan in crowd gave her
a shilling to get another bottle. Two davs later saw
same performance, by same performer, going on in Kent-
street, and still another listener'unable to stand her tale
of woe. Evidently good biz. Another adroit Sydney
beggar is a dark, thickset man smelling audibly of long-
drunk whisky, and who rapidly reels off this (he mostly
“ gets there,” too): “ Good evening, Mister . Do
have pity on me ! I haven’t the face to ask you for food
—to say that lam starving—for that would not be true.
I cannot now eat anything at all. Haven’t eaten for four
days. But lam dying for a drink. If I don’t get a
drink I shall faint to death. O save me, for God’s sake !
Give me sixpence to get some good whisky. I was dis-
charged from the Hagey institute a week ago after being
there three weeks ; but though they pronounced me
cured I was as bad as ever within two days.” I’m told
he’s a liar—that he has never been to the Hagey place.
But his confidence-trick yarn is good, and more or less
new, and it fills a long-felt want.

The t otherside element in Westralia is
not so enthusiastic for Federation at once as
might be expected. This element fears a test just
yet fears to let the sandgropers dispose of the
matter for a stretch of years. So the disposition
is to cause delay until a few thousand more
t othersiders have got on the rolls to- ensure an
affirmative vote. Up to date the new-chums have
been scandalously remiss in seeking enfranchise-
ment ; and the Forrest crowd hasn’t helped
them much.

“To keep young, live in the metropolis.”
—Bulwer.
The Westralian beak is a law unto him-
self. Also he is a small tin-god and a good deal of
a hog. For instance: A Norseman digger lost, or
said he lost, 40 sovs., and his mate was arrested
and taken to Esperance on suspicion. Twenty
sovereigns were found on the mate, but he proved
that he had been honestly possessed of a cheque
for £ 5O, and he also produced the publican on
whose premises he had drunk the other .£3O. Tho
publican’s evidence, the cheque, and a certain
lingering rum-fragrance made up such a solid body
of evidence that the jury acquitted the mate with-
out hesitation. Then the court sentenced him to
one day’s imprisonment, and declined even to
state what it was for.
“ Locust ” : The memory of G. It. Parkin,
the Imperial Federation drum-thumper who visited
Australia in ’B9, lately C.M.G.’d, will be hal-
lowed in the North as that of the only man ever
seen in Rockhampton with a belltopper. I
shall never forget the surprise with _ which
he was viewed, nor the sustained interest
which he created. Small boys, fascinated, fol-
lowed him through the streets, and gazed with
open mouths at his strange garb. It was a hot
summer, yet he stuck to the “ topper ” and the
black frock-coat of commerce. Old men, thus
reminded of their youth, wept reminiscent tears,
j Young men, who had seen such things in the
I illustrated papers, were interested, but not sur-
prised. 'At night, the School of Arts hall was
crammed with expectant people, who thought
Parkin an adjunct to a circus. His discourse,
delivered at the rate of 180 words a minute, con-
sisted in showing that the Almighty had placed
all the large coal-deposits in British possessions,
as a special mark of His interest in the British
race. He (Parkin) held a long billiard-cue in his
hand, and had a big map showing where the
dominions and the coalbeds were. He never
finished a sentence, and he never pointed at any-
thing. He ran up a hundred times to the map in
a threatening manner, but always ran back again
to finish an unfinished sentence which he never
completed. I always now associate Imperial
Federation with a very long black coat, a very
shiny top-hat, a very hot summer, a billiard-cue,
and unfinished sentences, beginning in the middle
with the word “ because” and having no end.

“ Q.” to The Bulletin on some artists: —
“ O. and 0.E.” can’t teach me much about the amateur
Bohemianism current in the studios of our Australian
Little Billees. I have met the juvenile geniuses who have
done a 48 hours’ starve—within reach of cash, credit and
friends —just for the sake of talking about it and making
a note of it in their ridiculous diaries. I know the simple
infants who swig absinthe because they have read in Vie de
Boteme or elsewhere that to do so is the proper caper in
the Latin quarter; and I’ve been hored to death by the
cacklers of studio cant with its “ Philistine,” “ Boorah-
wah,” “ Chic,” etc., etc., and its George Mooreish gush
over things read about but not even superficially under-
stood. That is why I fume over imitated fringes arid
neckties, seeing in them only another form of meaning-
less cant. “O. and 0.E.” mistakes my main point, which
was certainly not an attack on black-and-white artists as
such. My complaint is that so many allegedly budding
geniuses contemptuously profess to do journalistic work
in order to gain a subsistence to enable them to
devote their energies to the “higher art.” Which, of
course, is bosh, unless fulfilled. They will not admit
that they are journalistic artists. No; they are, in their
own imaginations, -something-immeasurably superior ;
though, beyond their words, no one ever gets a sign of
it. They keep on doing bad black-and-white work, and
produce no pictures at all, nor attempt to. The best
b. and w. men here and everywhere have succeeded by
good and conscientious work. Let the Litlle Billees leave
the “ higher art” cant alone, and put their energies and
enthusiasm into the b. and w. efforts, and they will simi-
larly succeed, for they have talent—some of them.
Painters like Longstaff, Withers, &e. &c., don’t touch
b. and w. work at all, but lay to their canvas. Capable
b. and w. men like Keene, May, Gibson,' &e., &c., usually
take up palette and brush only for amusement, and lay
to their books, the Simes, Manuels, Dudley Hardys, and
a few others being exceptional. Perhaps Tee Bulletin
is satisfied with Australian black-and-white work. I, of
course, don’t matter, but personally I think it too often
unspeakably slovenly and wooden. We seem to be de-
veloping a new type—the journalistic draughtsman—who,
like the average newspaper reporter, is soulless, sordid,
unpicturesque, and mechanical. It is the journalisation
of Art, and tends to the increase of Cant and the decrease
of Graft.

40 Years of Rheumatism.
A LADY’S MARVELLOUS RECOVERY.
OVER 74 YEARS OF AGE, BUT NOW IN
PERFECT HEALTH.
“ Until a short time ago T had not known for 40
years what permanent relief from pain was.” Thus
spoke Mrs. T. J. Thomas, of Heathcote, Victoria,
recently to a reporter. How greatly Mrs. Thomas
suffered from rheumatism, repeated attacks of in-
fluenza, enlargement of the liver, and inflammation
of the kidneys is vividly shown by her remarks.
mrvrm
“ I am over 74 years
of age,” she contin-
ued. “It was, as
near as I can re-
member, in ’57 that
I had my first attack
of rheumatism, and
these have followed
me up, on and off,
for 40 years. About
nine years ago my
troubles were added
to by enlargement
of the liver, and this
in itself, more than
sufficient to kill most
people of my age,
was aggravated by
inflammation of the
kid neys. As you
may imagine, I was
in a very critical condition for a long time, and
when just about to pull together again an-
other severe attack of rheumatism brought me
down. This brings me to about five years back,
when I caught influenza for the first time.
But, to shorten my story, I had further at-
tacks of rheumatism and influenza again the fol-
lowing year, and though the influenza missed
me for the next two years, rheumatism did not.
But I had more to face yet, for about a year and
uine months I was laid up with a third attack of
influenza. I never seemed to bo able to throw
this off as I had the others, and it made me feel
terribly low-spirited. My oldest enemy, rheuma-
tism, kept going at me, and it seemed that
between them they were going to get the best of
me. I had four or five doctors, but their ad vice
and physic was not the least bit of good, and I
just felt I could not last much longer. The pain
f suffered beats description. I could not lie
down, and the only position in which partial
relief was to be found was sitting, bearing to one
side. I could not bear anything to touch my
back. I don’t like to think of the torture through
which I have passed ;it was terrible. I lost all
power in my legs. At this stage my attention
was attracted to some of the published cures
which had been effected by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills for Pale People of cases which had been
pronounced by doctors and hospitals as hopeless,
and I determined to make a trial of them. This
was in the early part of April, and I was feeling
about as bad as I had ever felt. By the time the
third box was taken a marked improvement was
noticeable, and this happy alteration continued
until I was at the twelfth box quite enabled to
discontinue the treatment. My friends and
acquaintances were astonished at my recovery,
and quite a number are taking the pills, and
others have expressed their determination to do
so should they ever feel in need of treatment.”
“ I believe you sometimes go into town ? ”
“ Quite true, and this means a walk of three
miles, with a rise or two to face ; a very fair
undertaking for one on the wrong side of 74.”
“ And you are confident that Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People have been the means of
restoring you to health ? ”
“ I am perfectly confident of this, and have no
hesitation in saying so, and I shall recommend
your wonderful.remedy whenever I have an oppor-
tunity.”
Pale and Sallow Girls.
What can be more distressing than to see a girl
drooping and fading in the springtime of youth.
Instead of bright eyes, glowing, rosy cheeks, and
an elastic step, there are dull eyes, pale, yellow, or
greenish complexion, and a languidness of step
that bespeaks disease and an early death if proper
treatment is not promptly resorted to and per-
sisted in until the impoverished blood is enriched,
and the functions of life become regular.
Upon parents rests a great responsibility at
the time their daughters are budding into
womanhood. If your daughter is pale, com-
plains of weakness, is “tired out” upon the
slightest exertion; if she is troubled with head-
ache or backache, pain in the side, if her temper is
fitful and her appetite poor, she is in a condition
of extreme peril, a fit subject for the development
for the most dreaded of all diseases—Consumption.
If you notice any of the above symptoms, lose no
time in procuring Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. They
will assist the patient to develop properly and
regularly ; they will enrich the blood and restore
health’s roses to the cheeks; bright eyes and
lightness of step will surely follow their use, and
all danger of consumption and premature death
will be averted. Wise and prudent mothers will
insist upon their daughters taking Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills upon the approach of the period of
womanhood, and thus avoid the risks of disease
or early decay.

A WELL-KNOWN BLAYNEY MAN IN
AGONY.
SAVED FROM THE HOSPITAL
BY A MIRACLE.
Mr. George Hynds, a well-known resident of
Blayney, was recently interviewed by a Blayney
Advocate reporter about his serious illness and
remarkable cure. He obtained the following in-
ssS*-"'
jr ns
teresting narrative, which Mr. Hynds said he
would be glad to ha ve published.
“ About two years ago I fell a victim to sciatica.
I was drawing wood at the time. The complaint
Fearful Influenza.
A MAN WHO WAS NOTHING BUT SKIN
AND BONE.
HOW HE BEGAINED HIS STRENGTH
AND VIRILITY.
“ My illness,” said Mr. Hinnigan, of 18 Bay-
street, Sydney, to the reporter who called upon
him, “started as a good many other illnesses do—
just a cold, and at first I didn’t take much notice
of it, for I’ve often had colds before and got rid
of them in a week or so. This time, however, I
didn’t recover in the ordinary way, but began to
get thin and week.
“ This made me feel a bit nervous, especially
as I couldn’t tell exactly what was the trouble
with me, so I went to a chemist and got some
medicine. But as, in spite of the physic, I con-
tinued to get thinner and weaker, I went up
to the Sydney Hospital. The doctors there
examined me, and told me I had a severe attack
of influenza, and that I required medicine and
rest. Well, I did exactly as they ordered me,
and took rny medicine regularly four times a day,
but I can’t say that I felt any better for it.
What I know for certain is that I slowly con-
tinued to get worse and worse. I lost my appe-
tite altogether. I eat, certainly, but I might
have been chewing up so much paper for all the
pleasure that eating gave. I also lost my voice,
so that I had to go quite close to people if I
wished them to hear what I was saying, and my
face regularly caved in about the jaws, and
became a sort of dingy yellow color, something
like a piece of dirty paper. I was just able to
crawl out in the sun on fine days, and I was more
than once taken for a fever-stricken New Guinea
miner; and those poor fellows, as you know, are
nothing but skin and bone when they land back

Queen's Gate Terrace, London,
The Ghoul of Aneityum.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Well, it might be “murder” —I call it “justi-
fiable homicide.” Listen, and then give your
verdict.
We were trading in the New Hebrides —our
headquarters at Api. Jack took the schooner on
a run up north, to the Banks Islands. I soon
became sick of loafing round at the station, and,
when I heard from a passing trader that Jack
intended going on to the Santa Cruz Group, I
just fitted cut the whaleboat, and, with a native
crew, set sail for the south. I called at Efate,
then went on to Tanna, and here it was I learnt
of “Mad Morton.” Occasional rumors had
reached Api of an old “ insect-man ” down at
Aneityum, but, as he wasn’t in our line, I didn’t
pay much attention to them. M‘Donald, a trader
on Tanna, told me that Morton lived entirely
alone —did no trade, had no plantation—but
bought food from the natives, and devoted all his
time to preserving insects, fishes, and so on. This
aroused my curiosity. I had plenty of time, so I
determined to visit him.
♦ • * *
The sun was sinking when we reached Anei-
tyum, and the heavy sea breaking on the reef gave
us a bad time before we got safely in. We were
met on the beach by a number of men and women,
who had just come in from the taro-fields. I
could only see the native houses on the hillside, so
I asked where the white man lived, and they
pointed along the beach. I left the crew behind
and trudged along the sand. At the end of
the beach, almost hidden by the vegeta-
tion, I came to a rough fence, which
enclosed an uncultivated piece of ground. This
was so unusual that I knew I had found my man,
and, leaping the fence, advanced towards the
dwelling. It was like the native huts—bamboos
had been bent over a ridge-pole supported by two
forks ; these had been fastened by vines, and a
thatch of plaited coeoanut leaves laid on. A
reed-screen was in front of the doorway. As I
approached, the reeds were parted, and a man’s
head was thrust out. I cried : “ Hullo, Morton.
I’ve come to look at your museum.”
He came out, and, upon my word, he was the
ugliest, dirtiest creature I have ever seen. He
was short, but his body, bent almost double, made
him appear even smaller. His head was bald ex-
cept for a fringe of dirty white hair at the base,
and a long beard, once white, but now grimy and
matted with filth, flowed over his bare chest. A
glance at his face, with its hook nose, d ‘ep-sunken
glittering eyes, and harsh, yellow, wrinkled skin
drawn tight over projecting cheekbones, made me
wish I h idn’t come. Still, there was no retreat
now, so I determined to see it out.
Ho lo iked at me for a moment, then turned,
and,pushing through the screen,said, “Come in.”
When I entered I saw that this hut differed
from the others in that it had a floor of bamboos
laid at the level of the low walls. Over the floor
were spread a number of native mats except in
the centre, where an old packing-case did duty as
a table. This, together with a three-legged stool,
was all the furniture ; but at the end I saw another
screen which extended right across the hut,
dividing it into two compartments.
The old man, motioning ine to stay where I
waa> went through into the inner room and re-
turned wi u th a tin oil-lamp. By its light I saw
two or three small cases with glass tronts, in
which were ranged a few insects, hanging from
the roof> °
J strolled over and looked at then, the old man
watching me through half-closed eyes.
A glance was sufficient for me, and turning to
Morton I asked : “ What are you doing in this
island ? ”
“ Collecting insects,” he replied, and the accorn-
panying chuckle made me shiver.

response as he lifted the lamp and beckoned me

_ Morton took a stiff glass of rum and swallowed
it> then, bringing in the stool from outside, sat
down next to me.
. “I am an anthropologist, and my special branch
is craniology,” lie whispered. “I am on the eve
of a great discovery, for I think I can, by means
of this science alone, determine the different races
of men which, mixing together, have formed the
people now known as Melanesians.”
I burst out laughing. “ What !by feeling their
bumps? I see you are a phrenologist, then ! ”
His rage was something awful. He jumped from
his stool, and raved like a madman for about five
minutes; then he cooled down and answered:
“ Curse your ignorance; phrenology is quackery,
but craniology is an exact science. It lias been
studied by ”
Then followed a list of French and German
names which made my head reel,
“ Well,” I answered, “I’m sorry if I have hurt
your feelings, but I have seen hundreds of skulls
in the northern islands, and never could tell one
from another.”
“That is because of your ignorance,” he said.
“I believe that by studying skulls separately, the
localities of which are certain, we will be able to
discover fixed characteristics which will permit of
our identifying any race by merely examining the
crania.”
I felt sure he was talking nonsense, but I didn’t
know enough to contradict him, so let him go on.
A faint tinge of red appeared under his yellow
skin, his eyes glistened, and he had become quite
garrulous ; he was evidently on his hobby-horse.
“ For instance, you can always tell a Tas-
manian by the curious median sagittal ridge, with
lateral grooves.” Here he turned to me suddenly
and put out his hand. I edged backwards away
from him. “ Have you ever been in Mallicolo? ”
he asked eagerly.
“ Often,” I answered.
“Well, have you ever noticed that the medio-
frontal suture is persistent in adults there? The
proportion of metopic skulls is infinitesimal every-
where else, but in Mallicolo they say all the crania
show this suture ? ”
“ I don’t know what you are talking about.”
“ Pah ! I forgot,” said the old man, contemptu-
ously ; “ you are ignorant, but I will show you.”
• * « *
He took the lamp and went into the outer room.
Coming back he looked carefully around. “ We
are safe ; there is no one about.”
Stooping, he drew aside one of the mats and
raised three pieces of the bamboo flooring. Going
down on his knees, he peered into the hole, and
presently I could hear him muttering the
Aneityumese numerals : Ethi, ero, eseij, eirian-
wan, &e., up to 12.
Without lifting his head he called me over. I
didn’t care to kneel beside him, so I took the lamp
and placed it near the hole, and by its light saw
12 human skulls ranged on the ground below.
“ What do you keep them there for ? ” I asked.
“ Hush ! ” he murmured. “ Speak low—ten of
the skulls belong to this island, and if the people
knew I had them I would be murdered.”
“ How did you get them ? ” I asked, for I knew
that on Aneityum they had “ sea-burial,” i.e. , the
body was taken on to the edge of the reef,
wrapped in bandages of native cloth, the face
painted red, and then launched into the sea with a
stone tied to the feet.
Morton chuckled gleefully.
In the dim light he appeared a veritable demon,
and as he rose, clasping one of the skulls in his
arms, a hideous grin spread over his face. I felt
strongly tempted to clear out.
“ That took me a long time to arrange,” he
said. “For days I was out in the boat tracing
the currents, but at last I was successful. I found
that from the, outermost point of the reef, where
the bodies were thrown in, a current set outwards
for a little distance, then there was a back current
which led round the point to a little bight about
two hundred yards away. When a body is
thrown into the sea, if the stone is very heavy, it
sinks, and I cannot find it, and sometimes, again,
it gets into another current and is carried away,
but, now and then, it travels as I have said, and
all I have to do is to drag near the bight, haul up
the body, cut off the head, and consign the re-
mainder again to the ocean. I go at night time,
and, guided by the light of the fire which the
natives make on the reef for the spirit of the
deceased to warm itself at, I begin my fishing.
No ! I am not an icthyologist,” he excitedly
cried. “ What do I care for those paltry things !”
(pointing to the glass jars); “ these are the fish I
seek” (gently patting the skull in his arms).
“Many, many nights I’ve been out, but only ten
times have I been fortunate —just halfway—l
want twenty, then I am away for Mallicolo. Bub
I forgot—l wanted to speak to you about those
Mallicolo skulls. You see, in this one, from
Santo, the . . .
* • * •
I appeared to listen to him, but my eyes were
directed towards the screen. I had seen it move.
The old man kept on talkingquickly and excitedly
every now and then he app aled to me or asked
a question. I did not heed him, and he answered
himself. I was watching intently and again saw
the screen move. This time I was certain we had
a visitor.
The old man had stopped speaking now and was
leaning over the hole again. “ What is the
matter ?” I asked.
“ Yes,” he went on again, apparently not notic-
ing my question, “ this is the skull of which I was
speaking,” and he brought a second one forward:
“ I must tell you its history. I only recovered it
last night. It is the skull of Inlolan. She was
the most beautiful and cleverest girl on the island.
Only married a fortnight ago to Naha—a fine
savage. By the way, I intend to have his head—
it is a splendid one. He is very intelligent and
can speak English fairly well—he learnt it on a
Santo plantation.” I saw the screen parted and a
head was silhouetted on the wall in front of me.
J looked at the newcomer but he did not see me ;
his eyes were fixed on the skull in Morton’s hand.
The thought flashed through my brain —“Can
this be Naha ! ” If so, then God help Morton
unless I interfere. Shall I? Is it worth while
risking my own life to save Hat of this unclean
body-snatcher? I hardly think so. Anyway, I’ll
wait.
“ Yes,” continued the old man, “I could have
saved this young girl’s life, only I badly wanted
her skull. They came to me for medicine to cure
her ; but, when I found out who was sick, I told
them I had none. So she died, and now I have
her skull. Oh ! it is such a pretty one ” —and
Morton fondled his treasure as a woman would a
kitten.
Naha—l knew it was he, now—was half-way
through the screen. I saw a knife in his hand,
and 1 knew the skull-hunter was doomed. Curse
the brute; let him die! I rose. “Good-night,
Morton,” I said. “I'm full of your devilish
talk.” He did not hear me, but was kneeling over
the hole again, counting his riches. Ethi ero, &c.
I had reached the doorway before the blow was
struck. I heard the fall, and then stood aside
whilst a dark figure rushed past me, bearing a
white object in his hand. I turned towards the
inner room, but tongues of fire bursting through
drove me back —the lamp had been overturned.
* * * *
An inquiry was held afterwards. Twelve skulls
were discovered in the ruins ; and no one knew
which was Morton’s (the irony of it !) —so they
were all buried together.
WINDA.


During the last few months a number of
Vic. miners have returned from Westralia to find
that in their absence unlicensed prospectors have
jumped the family claim. Tragedies have nearly
occurred in some cases; in other m- nces the
husbands have philosophically acted ln<e Enoch
Arden —said nothing, and reconnoitred the situa-
tion through the window, then vanished without
leaving even the trace of their boot-soles behind.
The Tertium Quid, in one recent affair, hearing
that the husband was expected home at any
moment, carried the obtrusive baby eight miles on
horseback in the dead of night to the next hamlet,
and there deposited it on the doorstep—good old
Mother Doorstep again !—of a respectable family-
man who had never looked acquisitively on a
strange woman in the whole course of his life.
The old Scotch song should be popular with re-
turned Westralian miners :
Hame cam oCr gudeman,
Ilame cam he;
“ I see a bairn where
A bairn suldna be !
Hoo cam the bairn there
Without the leave of me?”
Ladies and gentlemen who visit Sydney at
any time are cordially invited to call at our estab-
lishment and leave a record of their measures with
our dressmakers or tailoi’s. And while you are in
ask to see one of the firm. We are always glad to
see our country customers, and talk over the best
way to send goods, packing, etc., etc. Hordern
Brothers, 203, 205, 207, 209, 211 Pitt-street
(only), Sydney.*

Man’s Companion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I met him in a billabong a-fishing with a frog,
And all he talked was bony bream and dog—but
mainly dog.
“ Look here ! ” he said . . . and cast his line with
elegance and force . . .
“ To travel on the track, a dog licks any time a
horse.
As long, in course, as you are up to snuff the same
as me ;
Fur edereation does it all, and hang to pedigree !
The more yer cuffs a blanky pup, the more he loves
yer sight ;
But try that on a moke . . . bedamned !” (for
here he missed a bite).
“ You makes him understand that you are boss of
all the show,
And tell him, when he gets a bone, he oughter
had a blow.
But ” . . . and with painful care he fixed another
piece of frog
Upon his hook ... “if you should wish to keep
this selfsame dog,
By all that’s holy, never let another fellow touch
The blessed brute ; for if ye do, he won’t be up to
much.
The moment he forgets to growl at any bloke he
sees,
He’ll soon be in a nigger’s camp, or taking to
Chinese.
Now, look at that here slut of mine ! She isn’t
much to brag,
But I have taught her . . . there, at last !—By
! another snag ! . . .
Yes, mate, with that here system which to you I
just explained,
I’ve made her what you see her now ; you watch
her, how she’s trained.
You mark her how she fawns to me, and damned
well snarls at you
Though you chucked her that piece of steak, and
I a curse or two.
It’s training, mate ! I would not take a fiver for
that dog ”...
And then he heaved a sigh, and went to find
another frog.
The whaler and his pattern slut, I saw them dis-
appear
Behind an overhanging tree, and thought—of yow,
my dear.
It struck me I had never got such good advice in
life :
For though I haven’t got a dog, you know, I have
—a wife !
N.Q.
VON KOTZE.
Fair division To the poor—the dirty
work; to the rich—the filthy lucre.
Kitchener, of Atbara fame, doesn’t believe
in strong waters on active service. Says coffee’s
a better stimulant than whisky, and tea a better
quencher than beer. Which is also the Australian
bushman’s experience.
Advertisement of a Shanghai dairy ; “We
open at Woosong, in the south of the Telegraph
co., for sale the foreign milk, the taste are sweet,
the milk are pure, and the prices are just. We
haven’t put any water in it, if examine out, won’t
pay a single cash. If you want to by so you will
know the Foreign cow’s shop.”
A wail from Cairns : “ Kanakas will not
stay with white men for ten shillings a week
when Chinese are offering fifteen!” Wherefore
the Cairns Farmers’ Association intends taking
the matter up with intent to see if they cannot
euchre the Chinese.” Poor, poor Cairns ! Not
only does it takeoff its hat to the Chow, but now
it actually undersells him in the labor-market.
“ Mean as a Cairns white.”
Westralian Mayors have to do a prodigious
amount of big-visitor entertaining, and they are
beginning to insist that the thing should be put on
some sort of a basis. Wherefore the province’s
municipal conference, sitting at Coo'gardie, has
agreed that every council should have power to
vote its Mayor not more than 20 per cent, of the
rates, with a maximum allowance of £5OO. A
proposal to make it 5 per cent, was scorned.
Maoriland, the place where they take their
religion seriously, and where, as a natural result,
the missionaries of every bounding brotherhood
swarm like flies, is much overrun now by the
Mormon elder. The humble followers of Joseph
Smith are built on one easily-recognisablepattern.
They are elderly and seedy and clothed in the
misfitting slop garment of righteousness, and they
always look as if they had slept the previous night
in a hen-house. Also they have long elfin locks,
and great, stragglesome crops of greasy beard, and
each one carries a large, blackened volume under
his arm. They are long, bony men, as a rule,
and walk rapidly, throwing out their big, eager
feet in front of them like men who are making
fast time on the road to New Jerusalem. The
Mormon missionary is also hard-working, parch-
ment-faced, not over-clean, with big, gnarled,
brown, bony, grasping hands, and eyes like the
eyes of one who sees a farthing in the distance.
And they are a hard lot to tackle. The other day
one of the tribe was sloping with his long, flapping
straddle through the arcade in Dunedin, and just
as he got opposite the shop of Slop Hee, green-
grocer, that heathen’s big black dog mistook the
volume under his arm for steak and flew at it.
But the long arm and the big hand of the Latter
Day Saint came down on Slop Hee’s dog with the
promptitude born of long wrestling in the good
fight, and that animal is now gradually con-
valescing under the counter with a cloth tied
round his head. Evidently it is no good sooling
dogs on the rough-and-ready church of Smith.
Nina and Her Father.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just Nina Mitchell. A little stick of a child,
who smiled apprehensively when she met anyone
bigger than herself, and let her face drop into the
crook of her arm if said person showed signs of
hostility; a good, quiet child, never to be sus-
pected when pencils were squeaked, or wattle-
seeds crushed “to make a smell.”
She was the only child that stood up when
teacher, for the fun of the thing, asked how many
girls would like to have been boys. It was Nina’s
shrill, sad, little voice that chimed out, “Please,
miss, I’d like to have been a boy ; ” followed by
the frightened explanation nudged out of her by
sharp-elbowed neighbors, “ Please, miss, ’cause
God’s a boy.”
In winter Nina’s work began. Often teacher
would meet her setting out across the hills, a
three-miles journey, to bring home father’s cow,
or wandering about the chill uplands' to cut the
wool from the frozen sheep. With a certain fascin-
ation, teacher would watch the thin legs and
flapping petticoats vanishing among the tussocks,
and the small, unhappily smiling face alternately
paling and darkening in the varying light of the
cloud-cumbered sky. Her father was a surface-
man on the new line of railway. He had four
other children, all younger than Nina, also all girls.
That winter there was some scandal in the third
class because Nina appeared in her father’s
trousers cut down for her, and barely concealed
by her blue cotton dress. Economy, not new
womanhood, accounted for this curious garb.
In the playground Nina was generally attended
by a body-guard of her schoolfellows silently
watchful. This circumstance teacher put down
to the bifurcated garment in question and thought
no more of it. She had not heard that Nina
Mitchell was rumoured to be “so bad inside she
might drop down dead any minute,” and that her
perpetual escort simply indicated a general desire
to be on hand when that interesting event should
take place. Consequently, she was quite unpre-
pared when the Children rushed up to her one
morning and informed her that Nina Mitchell
“ couldn’t come to school to-day because she was
dead. And please, Miss, we all sor her doing it.”
The general interest did not subside with the
decease and exhaustion of the particulars thereof,
but continued to the day of the funeral. This
took place opportunely on a Saturday, so that the
small surviving Mitchells might run in and out of
the house and tell their friends how things were
going on. “ She’s quite screwed down now,”
came the last bulletin ; “ but wait on a bit and
see her being brought out.”
Six of them were crowded about the gate when
teacher came past on her way to the store.
Teacher saw it all. She was a town girl, and her
face blazed as she drove the little assembly out
into the wire-fenced road. Then she was horror-
stricken to discover the bereaved father sitting
outside his weatherboard-hut. He must have
heard every word of that merciless chatter !
He raised his weather beaten troubled face, and
the look seemed to crave her sympathy.
She crossed the clearing, rough with the decayed
remnants of fire-eaten trees. Trembling with
nervous sympathy she clasped his big, black hand.
“ I feel so much for you, Mr. Mitchell. Nina
was such a good girl ”
“Yes, Miss, it's hard, it’s hard.” Hi's troubled
eves met hers. “ But there’s always the risk with
children. We rear ’em, and spend money on ’em,
and when they’re beginning to be useful they die
off like rabbits with phosphorus. It’s bin a bad
year all round, Miss. Good-bye, Miss, and thank
yer ; thank yer kindly for yer sympathy.”
The bereaved father went indoors.
Maoriland.
CONSTANCE CLYDE.
Didn’t Come Off.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My inspector is quite faddy on the Socratic
method of teaching. He knows “ Glad man’s
School Manageni nt ’’ off by heart. Consequently,
when the annual inspection ordeal comes round,
we get whips of Socratic method and moral sua-
sion. Miss Amelia Noney Novice is a fourth-class
pupil-teacher —a splendid worker, and quick to
pick up and assimilate hints and ideas.
“You must not tell your pupils so much, Miss
Novice,” I said to her one day; “you must use
the Socratic method more freely. It’s beautifully
explained by Gladman in his ‘School Manage-
ment.’ Make the pupils think —think for them-
selves. Get the answers OUT of them ; do not
put the answers in. Induce—educe—but don’t
cram. Now, you were just about to tell that boy
several synonyms for the word ‘ descend,’ because
he could not give you the meaning of it, or a
synonym for it. Now watch me get the answer
OUT of him, by the Socratic method.
“ ’Tenshun ! Stand up, Johnny Cookie ! I
want you to tell me what the word ‘ descends ’
means ? ”
“ Please, sir, I dunno ! ”
“Ah ! Well, suppose you were to climb up that
gum-tree at the back of the weather-shed, and
crawl right out on that crooked limb after a
’possum’s nest; and then, just as you had got a
young ring-tail out, a sudden gust of wind should
blow you off—what would you do? ”
I looked triumphantly at Miss Novice.
“ Please, sir, Vd fall an' bust meself, an’ squash
th’ bloomin’ ’possum ! ”
I went back to my own classes.
